The Four Schools and the Binary Division of the Known
Al-Jurjani enumerates four schools on non-existence and the intermediary, then presents the binary division of the known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four possible positions regarding non-existence and the intermediary state, and how the People of Truth derive a binary division of the known into existent and non-existent.

Core Idea
The known is divided into existent and non-existent, with no third state.

Al-Jurjani begins by laying out the four schools of thought on the status of non-existence and the intermediary state (al-hal). He then focuses on the position of the People of Truth (ahl al-haqq), which rejects both the fixedness of non-existence and an intermediary state, resulting in a simple binary division of the known into that which has external realization and that which does not. This binary division is the foundation for further subdivisions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four possibilities
Read the first part of the passage carefully. Notice that there are two binary choices: whether non-existence is fixed or not, and whether there is an intermediary between existent and non-existent. This yields four possible schools.
Each school was held by a group of theologians.
2
Focus on the first school
The first school (People of Truth) holds that non-existence is not fixed and there is no intermediary. This is the position from which the binary division of the known follows.
3
Understand the binary division
According to this school, the known is either that which has no realization in the external world (non-existent) or that which does (existent). This is a binary division, with no third category.
The phrase 'but the qualification 'in the external' is considered because they do not affirm mental existence' explains why the division is based on external existence.
4
Recognize further subdivisions
The passage states that this binary division is followed by two ternary divisions and a quaternary division. This hints at more detailed classifications that build on the initial dichotomy.
These further divisions are derived by subdividing the two primary categories.

Source Text

الاحتمال
أربع تقسيمات مبنية على مذاهبهم الأربعة وبيان ذلك أنه (إما أن يقال: بأن المعدوم ثابت أولا وعلى التقديرين إما أن تثبت الواسطة بين الموجود والمعدوم وهو الحال أو لا فهذه أربعة احتمالات) ذهب إلى كل واحد منها طائفة منهم . (الأول المعدوم ليس بثابت ولا واسطة أيضاً بينهما (وهو مذهب أهل الحق فالمعلوم ) أي ما من شأنه أن يعلم ) إما أن لا يكون له تحقق في الخارج إنما اعتبر قيد في الخارج لأنهم لا يقولون بالوجود الذهني ( أو يكون والأول ) هو (المعدوم) في الخارج ( والثاني ) هو ( الموجود ) فيه فهذه قسمة ثنائية يتبعها ثلاثيتان ورباعية .

قوله : (إلى معروضات الأمور (العامة قيد بذلك لأن له تقسيمات أخر كالتقسيم إلى تصوري وتصديقي وإلى بديهي وكسبي وإلى بسيط ومركب وإلى غير ذلك . قوله : أي) ما من شأنه أن يعلم فسر بذلك للتنبيه على أن المعلومية بالفعل ليست بمعتبرة في الموجود والمعدوم حتى لو فرض عدم تعلق العلم كان موجوداً والمعدوم معدوماً لا لأنه يلزم خروج ما لا يتعلق به العلم بالفعل عن القسمين على ما ،وهم لأنه يرد عليه أن المعلوم أعم مما يكون معلوماً للقوى العالية أو القاصرة ومما لا يكون بالكنه أو بالوجه ولا شك في شموله لجميع المفهومات .
قوله : (يتبعها إلخ) باعتبار قسمة القسم الأول إلى قسمين أو القسم الثاني أو كليهما .
المعلوم بالأفراد فإن قلت : المراد تقسيم المعلوم على المذاهب، فلذا جمعه قلت : الاختلاف في الأقسام لا في المقسم فلا وجه لجمع المقسم . ثم إن هذا التوجيه لا يتأتى في قول الشارح، وأما الحكماء فقالوا : في تقسيم المعلومات لا يقال : إضافة القسمة إلى المعلومات لأدنى تلبس أي القسمة إلى المعلومات كما في ضرب يوم الجمعة، لأن قول الشارح إلى معروضات الأمور العامة يأبى عنه، وقد يقال : في الجمع إشارة إلى أنه من تقسيم الكل إلى الأجزاء لا تقسيم الكلي إلى الجزئيات فإن كل واحد منهما يقع في كلامهم ولك أن تقول : الجمع بناء على اشتماله على تقسيم أنواع المعلوم من الممكن والحادث وبالجملة فيه ملاحظة أقسام الأقسام.
(
قوله: (أي ما من شأنه أن يعلم قيل: لا احتياج إلى هذا التفسير لأن كل شيء معلوم لله تعالى بالفعل وأجيب بأن فرقة من المتكلمين ينكرون شمول علمه تعالى على ما سيأتي، فالتفسير المذكور ليصح التقسيم على رأي كل فرقة وأنت خبير بأن حمل المعلوم على معلوم الله تعالى مما لا يتبادر إليه الأفهام وأيضاً قد تمنع تلك الفرقة المبطلة كون كل شيء من شأنه أن يعلم له تعالى، ولك أن تقول : لا احتياج إلى هذا التفسير وإن حمل على معلومنا لأن كل شيء معلوم
The Possibility
Four divisions based on their four schools, and that is explained by: (Either it is said that non-existence is fixed or not, and in both cases, either the intermediary between existent and non-existence is affirmed, which is the state (al-hal), or not. So these are four possibilities. Each one was held by a group of them. (The first: non-existence is not fixed, and there is no intermediary between them (this is the school of the People of Truth (ahl al-haqq). So the known (al-ma'lum) — i.e., that which is apt to be known — either has no realization in the external world, but the qualification 'in the external' is considered because they do not affirm mental existence, or it does. The first is non-existent in the external, and the second is existent therein. So this is a binary division, followed by two ternary divisions and a quaternary division.

His saying: (to the subjects of the universal (matters) is a restriction because it has other divisions, such as the division into conceptual (tasawwuri) and assent (tasdiqi), into intuitive (badihi) and acquired (kasbi), into simple (basit) and composite (murakkab), and others. His saying: (i.e., that which is apt to be known) is interpreted thus to indicate that actual knownness is not considered in the existent and non-existent, so that even if knowledge is supposed not to attach, the existent would remain existent and the non-existent non-existent. It is not because it would necessitate that what is not actually known falls outside the two divisions, as is imagined, because it is objected that the known is more general than what is known by high or low faculties, and than what is known in essence or in aspect, and there is no doubt it encompasses all concepts.
His saying: (followed by, etc.) is based on dividing the first division into two, or the second, or both.
The known by individuals: if you say the intent is the division of the known according to the schools, therefore he gathered it, I say: the difference is in the divisions, not in the divided term, so there is no reason to gather the divided term. Then this justification does not hold in the commentator's statement. As for the philosophers, they said: in the division of information (taqsim al-ma'lumat), it is not said that the addition of division to information is for minimal association, i.e., the division into information, as in 'the beating of Friday,' because the commentator's statement 'to the subjects of the universal matters' precludes it. And it may be said that the plural indicates it is a division of the whole into parts, not a division of the universal into particulars, for each occurs in their speech. And you may say: the plural is based on its inclusion of the division of the types of the known from the possible and the originated; in short, it observes the divisions of divisions.

(
His saying: (i.e., that which is apt to be known) — it is said: there is no need for this interpretation because everything is actually known to God Almighty. And it is answered that a group of theologians deny the comprehensiveness of His knowledge, as will come. So the mentioned interpretation is to make the division valid according to every group's opinion. And you are aware that applying the known to what is known to God does not readily occur to minds. Also, that annulling group may deny that everything is apt to be known to Him. And you may say: there is no need for this interpretation even if it is applied to our known, because everything is known
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage presents and explains the four divisions of the known (al-ma'lum) based on different schools regarding non-existence (al-ma'dum) and the intermediary state (al-hal). The commentator begins by outlining the four possibilities: either non-existence is considered fixed (thabit) or not, and in either case, whether there is an intermediary between existent and non-existent (i.e., the state, al-hal) or not. This yields four possible positions, each held by a group. The first position is that of the People of Truth (ahl al-haqq): non-existence is not fixed, and there is no intermediary. Consequently, the known is divided into two: that which has no realization in the external world (i.e., non-existent) and that which does (i.e., existent). This binary division is followed by two ternary and one quaternary division (referring to further subdivisions of each primary division). The commentator then discusses a comment about the phrase 'to the subjects of the universal matters,' explaining that this is a restriction because the known can be divided in other ways, such as into conceptual/assent, intuitive/acquired, simple/composite, etc. He explains that the definition of the known as 'that which is apt to be known' is intended to avoid the problem that actual knowledge may not attach; the existence or non-existence of something is not dependent on actual knowledge. He then addresses a subtlety about the plural 'divisions' (taqasim) versus singular 'divided term' (al-ma'lum): the difference is between different divisions, not in the term itself. The philosophers' view on the expression 'division of information' is also noted, contrasting with the commentator's phrasing. Finally, there is a discussion on why the interpretation 'apt to be known' is needed: it ensures the division works for all schools, including those who deny that God knows everything actually. Some argued that the interpretation is unnecessary if one considers only our knowledge, but the commentator dismisses that.

الواسطة

قسمة ثنائية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the known includes an intermediary state (al-hal) between existent and non-existent.
Correction: According to the People of Truth, there is no such intermediary; the known is only existent or non-existent in the external world.

Checkpoint
According to the People of Truth (ahl al-haqq) as described in this passage, how is the known primarily divided?
A) Into three categories: existent, non-existent, and the intermediary state (al-hal).
B) Into two categories: that which has realization in the external and that which does not, with no intermediary.
C) Into four categories based on the schools various opinions.
D) Into conceptual and assent divisions only.

Key Takeaway
The known is fundamentally divided into existent and non-existent, with no middle state, according to the People of Truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever treat your own thoughts or intentions as if they exist in a 'gray area' between reality and non-reality? How does the categorical division of all that is known into existent and non-existent challenge you to be more decisive in your beliefs?
?
When you consider matters of faith — like the existence of God or the reality of the afterlife — do you mentally slip into a vague intermediary state, giving less than full conviction? How can you root out such hesitation and affirm that what is true is truly existent?
?
What in your daily life do you act as though it is 'sort of real' — not fully existent but not non-existent either? Could accepting that everything is either existent or non-existent radically change how you prioritize your time and heart?

advanced

The Definition of the Known and the Scope of Division
Al-Jurjani explains the definition of the known and justifies its interpretation, then discusses the plural form of the division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the known is defined as 'that which is apt to be known' and how this definition ensures the division applies across different schools, as well as the significance of the plural 'divisions' in this context.

Core Idea
The known is defined by aptitude, not actual knowledge, to avoid inconsistencies.

After establishing the binary division, the commentator discusses the definition of the term 'known' (al-ma'lum). He explains that it must be interpreted as 'that which is apt to be known' rather than 'actually known' to accommodate differences in theological schools, including those who deny comprehensive divine knowledge. The discussion also touches on why the division is referred to as 'divisions' (plural) and not just 'division,' relating to the idea of dividing the whole into parts.

Guided Path
1
Understand the need for the interpretation
The passage explains that 'known' is interpreted as 'apt to be known' because actual knownness is not required; even if no one knows something, it remains either existent or non-existent.
This prevents the division from excluding things not actually known.
2
See the restriction to subjects of universal matters
The phrase 'to the subjects of the universal matters' is a restriction, meaning that other divisions of the known (e.g., into conceptual and assent) are not being considered here.
The focus is on the most general categories.
3
Understand the plural vs singular debate
Some question why the passage says 'divisions' (plural) rather than 'division' (singular). The answer is that the division is of the whole into parts, not of a universal into particulars, and the plural reflects the inclusion of further subdivisions.
The philosophers use 'division of information' but here it is 'divisions of the known'.
4
Grasp the justification for the interpretation
The interpretation 'apt to be known' is necessary to make the division valid for all schools, including those who deny that God knows all things. Without it, the division would fail for them.
Some argue it is unnecessary, but the commentator insists on its importance.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage presents and explains the four divisions of the known (al-ma'lum) based on different schools regarding non-existence (al-ma'dum) and the intermediary state (al-hal). The commentator begins by outlining the four possibilities: either non-existence is considered fixed (thabit) or not, and in either case, whether there is an intermediary between existent and non-existent (i.e., the state, al-hal) or not. This yields four possible positions, each held by a group. The first position is that of the People of Truth (ahl al-haqq): non-existence is not fixed, and there is no intermediary. Consequently, the known is divided into two: that which has no realization in the external world (i.e., non-existent) and that which does (i.e., existent). This binary division is followed by two ternary and one quaternary division (referring to further subdivisions of each primary division). The commentator then discusses a comment about the phrase 'to the subjects of the universal matters,' explaining that this is a restriction because the known can be divided in other ways, such as into conceptual/assent, intuitive/acquired, simple/composite, etc. He explains that the definition of the known as 'that which is apt to be known' is intended to avoid the problem that actual knowledge may not attach; the existence or non-existence of something is not dependent on actual knowledge. He then addresses a subtlety about the plural 'divisions' (taqasim) versus singular 'divided term' (al-ma'lum): the difference is between different divisions, not in the term itself. The philosophers' view on the expression 'division of information' is also noted, contrasting with the commentator's phrasing. Finally, there is a discussion on why the interpretation 'apt to be known' is needed: it ensures the division works for all schools, including those who deny that God knows everything actually. Some argued that the interpretation is unnecessary if one considers only our knowledge, but the commentator dismisses that.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the known must be actually known by someone to be considered existent or non-existent.
Correction: The known is defined as 'apt to be known' (i.e., capable of being known) regardless of whether it is actually known by any intellect.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator interpret 'known' (al-ma'lum) as 'that which is apt to be known' rather than 'actually known'?
A) To emphasize that God knows everything.
B) To ensure the division of the known into existent and non-existent applies even when something is not actually known.
C) To limit the division to intuitive knowledge.
D) To exclude composite things from the division.

Key Takeaway
The definition of the known as 'apt to be known' is crucial for the universal applicability of the binary division across theological schools.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The commentator defines 'the known' as what is apt to be known, not just what is actually known. What is something in your life that you have not actually learned about but which is 'apt to be known'? How does this definition change your responsibility toward it?
?
If the division of the known into existent and non-existent works only because of this careful definition, then subtle distinctions matter in knowledge. Where in your understanding of Islam have you overlooked a subtle definition that might affect your consistency?
?
The plural 'taqasim' reflects a deeper structure: the division is not just a simple classification but includes subdivisions. In what area of your spiritual life have you settled for a simplistic division when you should have recognized a more detailed structure?

The Third Possibility: Non-Existent is Affirmed
Sanusi presents the Mu'tazilite view and two divisions of the known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite claim that the non-existent has some reality (thubut) and how they divide the known into the negated and the affirmed, with three real categories.

Core Idea
For the Mu'tazila, the non-existent is not nothing; it has a type of fixity.

This lesson introduces a central Mu'tazilite doctrine: the non-existent (ma'dum) is not simply nothing but has a kind of affirmation (thubut). Al-Sanusi presents two divisions of the known that lead to three real categories. Understanding this will clarify a major theological disagreement about the status of non-existent entities before creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get the overall argument. Notice that the passage begins by stating the third possibility and then gives two different ways of dividing the known.
2
Identify the two divisions
The passage gives two divisions: first, the known is either negated (no realization) or affirmed (has realization); second, the known is either without being (non-existent) or with being (existent). Find these in the text.
The second division is introduced with 'Then they divided the known with another division...'
3
Discover the three real categories
Despite two divisions, the author concludes there are only three real categories: the negated, the affirmed existent, and the affirmed possible non-existent. Identify why the absolute non-existent is not a fourth.
The absolute non-existent reduces to the negated and the possible non-existent.
4
Understand the clarification about dividing the affirmed
The author explains that one might mistakenly think the negated is part of the affirmed if we divide the affirmed into existent and non-existent without qualification. The correct division only includes the possible non-existent.
This prevents a misunderstanding.

Source Text

الاحتمال الثالث المعدوم ثابت ولا واسطة وهو مذهب أكثر المعتزلة فالمعلوم على رأيهم إما لا تحقق له في نفسه أصلاً وهو المنفي المساوي للمتنع أو له تحقق في نفسه بوجه ما وهو الثابت المتناول للموجود والمعدوم والممكن ثم قسموا المعلوم تقسيماً آخر فقالوا: وأيضاً فإما أن لا كون له في الأعيان وهو المعدوم ممكناً كان أو ممتنعاً أو له كون فيها وهو الموجود والمنفي عندهم أخص مطلقاً من المعدوم لاختصاصه بالممتنع منه أي المعدوم وأنت تعلم أن نقيض الأخص مطلقاً أعم مطلقاً من نقيض الأعم فيكون الثابت الذي هو نقيض المنفي أعم من الموجود الذي هو نقيض المعدوم لصدقه عليه أي لصدق الثابت على الموجود وعلى المعدوم الممكن فقد ذكر على رأي هؤلاء تقسيمين، لكن الأقسام عندهم في الحقيقة ثلاثة هي المنفي والثابت الموجود، والثابت الذي هو المعدوم الممكن، وأما المعدوم مطلقاً فهو راجع إلى المنفي والمعدوم الممكن فلا يكون قسماً رابعاً، وكأنه لم يقسم الثابت على رأيهم إلى الموجود والمعدوم كما فعله غيره، لئلا يتوهم من إطلاق المعدوم على المنفي كون قسيم الثابت قسما منه، لكنه مند مندفع بأن قسم الثابت هو المعدوم الذي له ثبوت أعني المعدوم الممكن، وذلك لا يطلق على المنفي، وإنما يطلق عليه المعدوم مطلقاً، وليس قسماً من الثابت حقيقة.
قوله: ( في نفسه أصلاً أي في حد ذاته مع قطع النظر عن اعتبار المعتبر قيد بذلك لأن المنفي له تحقق اعتباري بطريق التشبيه والتنظير على ما سيجيء نقلا عن الشفاء أن المستحيل لا تحصل له صورة في العقل أي ليس لنا سبيل إلى إدراكه في نفسه بحيث يحصل منه صورة نفسه، فلا يمكن أن يتصور شيء هو اجتماع النقيضين أو الضدين، فتصوره إما على سبيل التشبيه أو التمثيل إلى آخره.
قوله: ( وهو المنفي المساوي للممتنع إن أريد بالممتنع أعم من أن يكون امتناعه باعتبار نفسه، أو باعتبار التركيب كان المنفي مساوياً للممتنع لشموله المركبات الخيالية أعني ما تكون أجزاؤه ممكنة وامتناعها باعتبار التركيب بناء على ما قالوا: إن التركيب لا يتصور حال العدم، وإن الثابت حال العدم إنما هو البسائط وإن أريد به ما يكون امتناعه باعتبار نفسه، كان المنفي أعم منه، وكلا الإطلاقين واقع في كلامهم كما لا يخفى على المتتبع فافهم فإنه مما تحير فيه الأفهام.
قوله: (بوجه ما سواء كان كوناً أو ثبوتاً.
قوله: (لا) كون (له الكون يرادف الوجود عندهم والتحقق أعم منه.
ظاهر، ولكن حق العبارة حينئذ أن يقال: فقولنا صفة يخرج الذوات ولموجود صفة المعدوم لأن قوله لأن الذوات ولأن صفة المعدوم يشعر بأن الغرض الأصلي من ذكرهما الاحتراز على أن صفة المعدوم، وإن كانت معدومة إلا أن صفة ما ليس بموجود ولا معدوم لا يظهر خروجها عند غير القائل بحاليتها إلا بالقيد المذكور.

قوله: (وهو المنفي المساوي للممتنع فيه بحث لأن الخياليات الممكنة غير ثابتة عندهم كما سيأتي، فلا معنى لجعل المنفي مساوياً للممتنع إلا أن يراد بالممتنع أيضاً ما لا ثبوت له، وهو اصطلاح جديد لا نقل عليه في كلامهم.

قوله: ( بأن قسم الثابت إلخ ) بناء على أن المقسم معتبر في الأقسام.
قوله: (حقيقة) وإن جعل قسماً منه ظاهراً حيث قسم الثابت إلى الموجود والمعدوم من غير تقييده بالممكن.
The third possibility: the non-existent is affirmed and there is no intermediary, and this is the doctrine of most Mu'tazila. So the known, according to them, is either that which has no realization in itself at all—and it is the negated, equal to the impossible—or that which has realization in itself in some respect—and it is the affirmed, encompassing the existent, the non-existent, and the possible. Then they divided the known with another division and said: 'Moreover, it is either that which has no being in external entities—and it is the non-existent, whether possible or impossible—or that which has being in them—and it is the existent.' And the negated, according to them, is absolutely more specific than the non-existent because it is restricted to the impossible among the non-existent. And you know that the contradictory of the absolutely more specific is absolutely more general than the contradictory of the more general. So the affirmed, which is the contradictory of the negated, is more general than the existent, which is the contradictory of the non-existent, because it applies to it and to the possible non-existent. So according to their view, two divisions have been mentioned, but in reality the categories are three: the negated, the affirmed existent, and the affirmed which is the possible non-existent. As for the non-existent absolutely, it reverts to the negated and the possible non-existent, so it is not a fourth category. And it is as if he did not divide the affirmed according to their view into the existent and the non-existent as others did, so that it would not be imagined from the application of the term 'non-existent' to the negated that the counterpart of the affirmed is a part of it. But this is repelled because the part of the affirmed is the non-existent that has fixity, i.e., the possible non-existent, and that term is not applied to the negated; rather, the term 'non-existent' absolutely is applied to it, and it is not a part of the affirmed in reality.
His saying: (in itself at all) means in its essence, disregarding the consideration of the considerer; he restricts it because the negated has a conceptual realization by way of analogy and representation, as will come quoting from al-Shifa that the impossible has no image in the intellect, i.e., we have no way to perceive it in itself such that an image of itself is obtained from it, so it is impossible to imagine something that is the conjunction of two contradictories or two opposites; its imagination is either by way of analogy or representation, etc.
His saying: (and it is the negated, equal to the impossible) if by 'impossible' is meant the more general, whether its impossibility is by virtue of itself or by virtue of composition, then the negated is equal to the impossible because it includes imaginary composites, i.e., those whose parts are possible but their impossibility is due to composition, based on what they said: composition is not conceivable in the state of non-existence, and what is affirmed in the state of non-existence is only the simple parts. And if by it is meant what is impossible by virtue of itself, then the negated is more general than it. Both usages occur in their speech, as is not hidden from the investigator, so understand, for it is something that confuses intellects.
His saying: (in some respect) whether it is being or fixity.
His saying: (no) being (for it) - being is synonymous with existence according to them, and realization is more general than it.
(Apparent, but the correct expression then is to say: so our saying 'attribute' excludes the essences and [the phrase] 'the existent has an attribute of the non-existent' because his saying 'because the essences' and 'because the attribute of the non-existent' suggests that the main purpose of mentioning them is to avoid [the problem] that the attribute of the non-existent, although non-existent, the attribute of that which is neither existent nor non-existent does not appear to be excluded for one who does not affirm its statehood except by the mentioned restriction.)
(In a state)
His saying: (and the answer is that the intended meaning is etc.) It is said: the possibles are originated according to them, so before origination the definition of state does not apply to substantiality, for example. We say: apparently they do not affirm a state before the existence of the described entity, as has passed. So substantiality before the existence of any substance in the world was not a state; then it became a state after its existence. Yes, this answer is valid only if they do not affirm statehood for a genus none of whose individuals exist externally, so reflect.
His saying: (by the affirmation of the non-existent) - for he does not affirm that the non-existent is qualified by anything, since being qualified requires a type of fixity for the qualified according to him.
His saying: (and it is the negated equal to the impossible) contains a discussion, because the imaginary possibles are not affirmed according to them, as will come, so there is no meaning to making the negated equal to the impossible unless by 'impossible' is also meant that which has no fixity, which is a new terminology not transmitted in their speech.
His saying: (And as for the non-existent absolutely) - it is outside the second division.
His saying: (that the part of the affirmed etc.) - based on the fact that the divided is considered in the divisions.
His saying: (in reality) - even if it is apparently made a part of it, when he divided the affirmed into the existent and the non-existent without restricting it to the possible.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the third possibility concerning the status of the non-existent (ma'dum) according to most Mu'tazila. The author first states that the non-existent is affirmed (thabit) and there is no intermediate state (hal). Then he divides the known (al-ma'lum) into two: (1) that which has no realization in itself—the negated (al-manfi), which is equal to the impossible (al-mumtani'), and (2) that which has realization in some respect—the affirmed (al-thabit), which encompasses the existent (al-mawjud), the non-existent (al-ma'dum), and the possible (al-mumkin). Then he presents a second division: either it has no being (kawn) in external entities—the non-existent, whether possible or impossible—or it has being—the existent. He then discusses the relationship between the negated and the non-existent: the negated is more specific because it is restricted to the impossible. From this, the affirmed is more general than the existent. He clarifies that in reality there are only three categories: the negated, the affirmed existent, and the affirmed possible non-existent. The absolute non-existent is not a fourth category because it reduces to the negated and the possible non-existent. He then addresses a potential misunderstanding: if one divides the affirmed into existent and non-existent without qualification, one might think that the negated (also called non-existent) is part of the affirmed, but this is not the case because the affirmed non-existent is only the possible non-existent. The negated is not included. After the main text, the commentary (qala) provides detailed clarifications: on the meaning of 'realization in itself', on the equality of the negated and the impossible (and two definitions of impossibility), on the term 'being' vs 'existence', on an objection about states and the definition of 'hal', and on the affirmation (thubut) of the non-existent. The commentator also critiques the notion that the negated is equal to the impossible, as imaginary possibles are not affirmed. Finally, he explains that the phrase 'As for the non-existent absolutely' refers to what is outside the second division, and the reasoning about the part of the affirmed is based on the divided being considered in the divisions. The last gloss clarifies that dividing the affirmed into existent and non-existent without restriction is only apparent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the negated (al-manfi) is equivalent to the non-existent absolutely, but the author shows it is only specific to the impossible.
Correction: The negated is more specific than the non-existent; it is restricted to the impossible. The non-existent includes both the negated (impossible) and the possible non-existent.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many real categories does the known consist of?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila distinguish the non-existent into two types: the impossible (negated) and the possible (affirmed), leading to three fundamental categories of the known.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the possible non-existent has fixity in God's knowledge, does that change how you view your own future possibilities? Are your potential choices already fixed in some sense? How should that affect your sense of responsibility today?
?
The lesson distinguishes between negated impossible and affirmed possible non-existent. Are there areas in your life where you treat possibilities as impossible when they might actually be possible? What keeps you from seeing them as affirmed?
?
Sanusi warns against misunderstanding the term 'non-existent' to include the negated. In your own thinking, do you ever conflate something being nonexistent with being impossible? Give a concrete example from your life and reflect on how this lesson corrects that mistake.

Rational Judgments
intermediate
4 min
Understanding the Negated: The Two Meanings of Impossible
Commentary on the equality of the negated and the impossible, with two definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two definitions of 'impossible' (mumtani') in the context of the negated (al-manfi) and why the negated may or may not equal the impossible.

Core Idea
The word 'impossible' can mean either what is impossible by itself or by composition, changing the scope of the negated.

In the previous lesson, we learned that the Mu'tazila consider the negated (al-manfi) to be equal to the impossible. But the commentator clarifies that this depends on how we define 'impossible'. This lesson unpacks that nuance, distinguishing between self-impossible and composite-impossible, and shows how it affects the classification.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentary passage
Read the Arabic and translation of the commentator's explanation of 'His saying: (and it is the negated, equal to the impossible)'. Pay attention to the two alternatives.
2
Distinguish the two definitions
The passage gives two ways to understand 'impossible': (1) what is impossible by itself, like '2+2=5', and (2) what is impossible due to composition, like a mountain of gold (possible parts but impossible whole).
3
Understand the consequence for the negated
If we take the first meaning, the negated equals the impossible. If we take the second, the negated is more general than the impossible (since imaginary composites are negated but not impossible by themselves).
4
Reflect on the importance
This nuance shows the precision of kalam terminology and prevents confusion when reading different theologians.
Both usages occur in their speech, as the passage says.

The third possibility: the non-existent is affirmed and there is no intermediary, and this is the doctrine of most Mu'tazila. So the known, according to them, is either that which has no realization in itself at all—and it is the negated, equal to the impossible—or that which has realization in itself in some respect—and it is the affirmed, encompassing the existent, the non-existent, and the possible. Then they divided the known with another division and said: 'Moreover, it is either that which has no being in external entities—and it is the non-existent, whether possible or impossible—or that which has being in them—and it is the existent.' And the negated, according to them, is absolutely more specific than the non-existent because it is restricted to the impossible among the non-existent. And you know that the contradictory of the absolutely more specific is absolutely more general than the contradictory of the more general. So the affirmed, which is the contradictory of the negated, is more general than the existent, which is the contradictory of the non-existent, because it applies to it and to the possible non-existent. So according to their view, two divisions have been mentioned, but in reality the categories are three: the negated, the affirmed existent, and the affirmed which is the possible non-existent. As for the non-existent absolutely, it reverts to the negated and the possible non-existent, so it is not a fourth category. And it is as if he did not divide the affirmed according to their view into the existent and the non-existent as others did, so that it would not be imagined from the application of the term 'non-existent' to the negated that the counterpart of the affirmed is a part of it. But this is repelled because the part of the affirmed is the non-existent that has fixity, i.e., the possible non-existent, and that term is not applied to the negated; rather, the term 'non-existent' absolutely is applied to it, and it is not a part of the affirmed in reality.
His saying: (in itself at all) means in its essence, disregarding the consideration of the considerer; he restricts it because the negated has a conceptual realization by way of analogy and representation, as will come quoting from al-Shifa that the impossible has no image in the intellect, i.e., we have no way to perceive it in itself such that an image of itself is obtained from it, so it is impossible to imagine something that is the conjunction of two contradictories or two opposites; its imagination is either by way of analogy or representation, etc.
His saying: (and it is the negated, equal to the impossible) if by 'impossible' is meant the more general, whether its impossibility is by virtue of itself or by virtue of composition, then the negated is equal to the impossible because it includes imaginary composites, i.e., those whose parts are possible but their impossibility is due to composition, based on what they said: composition is not conceivable in the state of non-existence, and what is affirmed in the state of non-existence is only the simple parts. And if by it is meant what is impossible by virtue of itself, then the negated is more general than it. Both usages occur in their speech, as is not hidden from the investigator, so understand, for it is something that confuses intellects.
His saying: (in some respect) whether it is being or fixity.
His saying: (no) being (for it) - being is synonymous with existence according to them, and realization is more general than it.
(Apparent, but the correct expression then is to say: so our saying 'attribute' excludes the essences and [the phrase] 'the existent has an attribute of the non-existent' because his saying 'because the essences' and 'because the attribute of the non-existent' suggests that the main purpose of mentioning them is to avoid [the problem] that the attribute of the non-existent, although non-existent, the attribute of that which is neither existent nor non-existent does not appear to be excluded for one who does not affirm its statehood except by the mentioned restriction.)
(In a state)
His saying: (and the answer is that the intended meaning is etc.) It is said: the possibles are originated according to them, so before origination the definition of state does not apply to substantiality, for example. We say: apparently they do not affirm a state before the existence of the described entity, as has passed. So substantiality before the existence of any substance in the world was not a state; then it became a state after its existence. Yes, this answer is valid only if they do not affirm statehood for a genus none of whose individuals exist externally, so reflect.
His saying: (by the affirmation of the non-existent) - for he does not affirm that the non-existent is qualified by anything, since being qualified requires a type of fixity for the qualified according to him.
His saying: (and it is the negated equal to the impossible) contains a discussion, because the imaginary possibles are not affirmed according to them, as will come, so there is no meaning to making the negated equal to the impossible unless by 'impossible' is also meant that which has no fixity, which is a new terminology not transmitted in their speech.
His saying: (And as for the non-existent absolutely) - it is outside the second division.
His saying: (that the part of the affirmed etc.) - based on the fact that the divided is considered in the divisions.
His saying: (in reality) - even if it is apparently made a part of it, when he divided the affirmed into the existent and the non-existent without restricting it to the possible.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the third possibility concerning the status of the non-existent (ma'dum) according to most Mu'tazila. The author first states that the non-existent is affirmed (thabit) and there is no intermediate state (hal). Then he divides the known (al-ma'lum) into two: (1) that which has no realization in itself—the negated (al-manfi), which is equal to the impossible (al-mumtani'), and (2) that which has realization in some respect—the affirmed (al-thabit), which encompasses the existent (al-mawjud), the non-existent (al-ma'dum), and the possible (al-mumkin). Then he presents a second division: either it has no being (kawn) in external entities—the non-existent, whether possible or impossible—or it has being—the existent. He then discusses the relationship between the negated and the non-existent: the negated is more specific because it is restricted to the impossible. From this, the affirmed is more general than the existent. He clarifies that in reality there are only three categories: the negated, the affirmed existent, and the affirmed possible non-existent. The absolute non-existent is not a fourth category because it reduces to the negated and the possible non-existent. He then addresses a potential misunderstanding: if one divides the affirmed into existent and non-existent without qualification, one might think that the negated (also called non-existent) is part of the affirmed, but this is not the case because the affirmed non-existent is only the possible non-existent. The negated is not included. After the main text, the commentary (qala) provides detailed clarifications: on the meaning of 'realization in itself', on the equality of the negated and the impossible (and two definitions of impossibility), on the term 'being' vs 'existence', on an objection about states and the definition of 'hal', and on the affirmation (thubut) of the non-existent. The commentator also critiques the notion that the negated is equal to the impossible, as imaginary possibles are not affirmed. Finally, he explains that the phrase 'As for the non-existent absolutely' refers to what is outside the second division, and the reasoning about the part of the affirmed is based on the divided being considered in the divisions. The last gloss clarifies that dividing the affirmed into existent and non-existent without restriction is only apparent.

منفي
ممتنع
امتناع

بسيط
مركب
خيالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'impossible' always means something that cannot exist in any way, but it can also refer to a combination that is impossible even if parts are possible.
Correction: The passage distinguishes between impossibility by itself and impossibility by composition. Imaginary composites are impossible as a whole but their parts are possible, so they fall under the negated but not under self-impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, when is the negated more general than the impossible?
A) When impossible means only self-impossible
B) When impossible includes composition
C) The negated is always more general
D) The negated is never more general

Key Takeaway
The term 'impossible' can be ambiguous; understanding its two meanings is essential for correct interpretation of Mu'tazilite ontology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you have said 'that's impossible' in your life, were you careful to distinguish between what is truly impossible by its nature and what is only impossible due to circumstances? How might this lesson change how you speak about Allah's power?
?
The scholars of kalam spend great effort on precise definitions. In your own pursuit of knowledge, where have you been satisfied with vague terms that later caused confusion? Will you commit to seeking clarity, even if it demands more effort?
?
If a thing can be impossible by composition while its parts are possible, what does that reveal about your assumptions regarding what Allah can create? Can you think of a situation where you dismissed something as impossible, when in fact its components are possible and you only lacked the composition? Confess that to yourself and ask Allah to expand your understanding.

The Mu'tazilite View: States and the Four Categories of the Known
The author presents the Mu'tazilite view affirming states, resulting in four categories of the known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite position that affirms states as real entities and how it leads to four categories of the known.

Core Idea
States are real: the Mu'tazila add a third category between existence and non-existence.

The fourth possibility in the debate over the scope of the affirmed is the Mu'tazilite doctrine that affirms states (ahwal) and holds that the non-existent can be affirmed. This passage explains their division of the known into four categories, including the state as a distinct category. We'll see how this compares to previous doctrines.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite claim and division
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author begins by stating the fourth possibility: the non-existent is affirmed and the state is real. Then he presents the division of that which occurs in external entities into independent (existent) and derivative (state).
The division is part of a larger framework of dividing the known.
2
Identify the four categories
From the passage, list the four categories that result: existent, state, possible non-existent, and negated (impossible). Note which ones are considered 'affirmed' (thabit).
The affirmed includes the existent, the state, and the possible non-existent—three things.
3
Compare with prior doctrines
The passage mentions that according to the third doctrine, the affirmed covers the existent, non-existent, and possible only (three things), and according to the second doctrine, only the existent. Compare these to see how the Mu'tazilite view expands the affirmed.
This comparison shows the range of positions on what counts as real.
4
Understand the gloss's defense
Read the gloss that addresses a potential objection: that the division is not of the known. It explains that the division implicitly uses the known as the divided, relying on context. This reinforces that the Mu'tazilite categorization is valid within the framework of dividing the known.
The gloss clarifies that the speaker presupposes the known as the overarching category.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاحتمال الرابع المعدوم ثابت والحال حق أيضاً وهو قول بعض المعتزلة من مثبتي الأحوال فيقول: الكائن في الأعيان إما أن يكون له كون بالاستقلال وهو الموجود أو يكون له كون بالتبعية وهو الحال فيكون الحال الذي هو قسم من الكائن في الأعيان أيضاً قسماً من الثابت كما أن الموجود والمعدوم الممكن قسمان منه وغيره أي غير الكائن في الأعيان هو المعدوم فإن كان له تحقق وتقرر في نفسه فثابت وإلا فمنفي فالأقسام أربعة فظهر أن الثابت الذي يقابل المنفي يتناول على هذا المذهب أموراً ثلاثة: الموجود والحال والمعدوم الممكن، وعلى المذهب الثالث يتناول الموجود والمعدوم والممكن فقط وعلى الثاني يتناول الموجود.
قوله: ( فيقول الكائن إلخ ) أي يقول ذلك البعض بعد تقسيم المعلوم إلى الكائن، وغير الكائن إلخ فيقسم كلا منهما إلى قسمين، فلا يرد أن هذا ليس تقسيماً للمعلوم.
قوله: (الكائن) في الأعيان إما بالاستقلال إلخ فإن قلت: قد مر أن الكلام في تقسيم المعلومات فيجب أن يجعل المقسم مفهوم المعلوم، ولم يجعل هاهنا قلت: لو سلم الوجوب في قوة قولنا المعلوم: إما كائن أو غير كائن والكائن كذا وكذا إلخ وإنما لم يصرح به فهو اعتمادا على السياق.
The fourth possibility: the non-existent is affirmed and the state is also real, and it is the doctrine of some Mu'tazila from among the affirmers of states. So he says: that which occurs in external entities is either that which has being independently—and it is the existent—or that which has being derivatively—and it is the state. So the state, which is a part of that which occurs in external entities, is also a part of the affirmed, just as the existent and the possible non-existent are two parts of it. And other than it, i.e., that which does not occur in external entities, is the non-existent. If it has realization and fixity in itself, then it is affirmed; otherwise, it is negated. So the categories are four. Thus it appears that the affirmed, which is opposite to the negated, according to this doctrine encompasses three things: the existent, the state, and the possible non-existent. According to the third doctrine, it encompasses the existent, the non-existent, and the possible only. According to the second doctrine, it encompasses the existent [only?].
His saying: (so he says: that which occurs etc.) i.e., that [particular] group says this after dividing the known into that which occurs in external entities and that which does not, etc., so he divides each of them into two parts, so it is not objected that this is not a division of the known.
His saying: (that which occurs) in external entities is either independently, etc. If you say: it has passed that the discussion is about dividing the known, so the divided must be the concept of the known, and he did not make it so here. I say: even if the obligation is conceded, it is implied by our saying: the known is either that which occurs or not, and that which occurs is such and such, etc. He simply did not state it explicitly, relying on context.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth possibility, which is the doctrine of some Mu'tazila who affirm states (ahwal). They hold that the non-existent is affirmed (thabit) and that states are real entities intermediate between existence and non-existence. The author explains their division: that which occurs in external entities (al-ka'in fi al-a'yan) is either (a) having being independently (bi al-istiqlal)—the existent, or (b) having being derivatively (bi al-tabi'iyya)—the state (al-hal). The state is thus a part of the affirmed, as are the existent and the possible non-existent. As for that which does not occur in external entities, it is the non-existent, which is either affirmed if it has realization in itself (the possible non-existent) or negated if it does not (the impossible). This yields four categories: existent, state, possible non-existent, and negated. The author then compares this to the third and second doctrines. The glosses clarify that the division is indeed a division of the known, because the concept of the known (al-ma'lum) is implicitly the divided in the statement, and the speaker relies on context to show that the division is of the known.

Misconception: The state (al-hal) is not a genuine category distinct from existence and non-existence.
Correction: Some Mu'tazila affirm states as real entities that have being derivatively, making them a distinct category alongside the existent and the possible non-existent.

Checkpoint

A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) One

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite doctrine of states introduces a third real category—the state—resulting in four divisions of the known: existent, state, possible non-existent, and negated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila affirmed that states are real even though they have derivative being. What 'states' in your own life—like your intentions, your reliance on Allah in unseen matters—do you treat as less real than tangible actions? How can you give them the weight they deserve?
?
This lesson divides the known into four categories, including the possible non-existent—things not yet present but affirmed in knowledge. What promises or commitments have you made that you treat as if they don't exist because they are not yet realized? Will you affirm them now as real obligations?
?
The passage shows that the affirmed includes not only what exists but also what is possible. In your faith, are you limiting 'truth' to only what you can see and experience? What aspects of the unseen (al-ghayb) do you need to affirm as real, even if they are not currently experienced?

The Philosophers' Division of the Known
Philosophers divide what can be known into non-existent and existent, with reality and identity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' classification of what can be known into non-existent and existent, and within existent, particular and universal based on reality and identity.

Core Idea
All knowable things are either non-existent or existent, and existent is particular or universal.

The philosophers (al-hukama') approach the question of what can be known by first dividing it into two fundamental categories: that which has no reality at all (the non-existent) and that which has some reality (the existent). Then they further analyze the existent in terms of its essence and individuality. This passage sets up a key distinction that shapes their theory of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the philosophers begin with a broad division.
2
Identify the categories
Find the two main categories: non-existent and existent. Then note the subcategories of existent: particular and universal, based on reality and identity.
3
Consider the implication
The passage ends with a note that the philosophers' claim about the mind perceiving only universals is subject to debate. Reflect on why this could be important.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحكماء فقالوا في تقسيم المعلومات (ما يمكن أن يعلم ولو باعتبار ) إما لا تحقق له بوجه من الوجوه ( وهو المعدوم، وإما له تحقق ما هو الموجود ولا بد من انحيازه بحقيقة ) أي لا بد من أن ينفرد الموجود وينحاز ويمتاز عن غيره بحقيقة يكون بها هو هو ( فإن انحاز مع ذلك ) عن غيره ( بهوية ) أي جزئية ( فهو جزئي، وإلا فكلي ) ثم قال: ( وما عدا ذلك من حاصله أن المعلوم أما المعدوم المطلق وأما الموجود. والموجود إما جزئي وإما كلي، ثم أخذ في بيان أن الأذهان لا تدرك إلا الكليات وأن الجزئيات لا تدرك إلا بالحواس، إلى أن قال: (فإن الذهن لا يدرك إلا أمراً كلياً) فيه بحث.
As for the philosophers, they said in the division of what can be known (that is, what may be known, even if by consideration): either it has no realization in any way whatsoever – and that is the non-existent – or it has some realization – that is the existent. And the existent must be isolated by a reality; that is, the existent must be distinguished, isolated, and set apart from others by a reality through which it is what it is. Then, if it is additionally isolated from others by an identity (i.e., a particular identity), then it is particular; otherwise it is universal. Then he said: 'And aside from that, the conclusion is that the known is either the absolute non-existent or the existent. The existent is either particular or universal.' Then he proceeded to explain that minds perceive only universals and that particulars are perceived only by the senses, until he said: 'For the mind does not perceive anything but a universal – this is subject to discussion.'
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' (al-hukama') division of what can be known (ma ya'malu an yu'lama). They divide it into two primary categories: (1) the absolute non-existent (al-ma'dum al-mutlaq), which has no realization in any way, and (2) the existent (al-mawjud), which has some realization. The existent must be distinguished by a reality (haqiqa) through which it is what it is. Then it is either particular if it has an additional particular identity (huwiyya juz'iyya), or universal if not. The author then notes that the philosophers hold that minds (al-adhhan) perceive only universals, while particulars are perceived by the senses. However, the author adds a note of caution: this claim ('the mind does not perceive anything but a universal') is subject to discussion (fihi bahth). The passage thus gives the philosophers' classification and raises a critical point about the scope of intellectual perception.

الذهن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers deny that the mind can perceive any particular at all.
Correction: The passage states that the claim 'the mind perceives only universals' is subject to discussion (fihi bahth), so it is not a settled doctrine.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, what distinguishes a particular existent from a universal?
A) Having a reality (haqiqa)
B) Being isolated by an identity (huwiyya)
C) Being perceived by the senses
D) Being non-existent

Key Takeaway
The philosophers divide the known into the non-existent and the existent, and the existent is further classified as particular or universal based on reality and identity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You constantly think about things that have no physical existence—like the future, numbers, or your own thoughts. According to this passage, those are all 'known' even if non-existent. Does it unsettle you to realize that much of what you know has no 'reality'? How does that change your trust in your own knowledge?
?
The philosophers claim your mind only grasps universals, not the specific things themselves. When you look at a loved one, do you really see *them* as a unique particular, or do you only see a manifestation of 'humanity'? If the latter, how can you claim to know them personally?
?
If the absolute non-existent is still classed as 'known', then what about your knowledge of Allah? He is existent but not perceivable by senses. This division classifies Him as existent and particular? Or universal? Reflect: does your mind reduce Him to a universal concept? How will you guard against that?

Definition and Division of the Knowable
Iji defines the knowable and divides it into non-existent and existent with a key condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the knowable as what can be known, even by a consideration, and its division into non-existent and existent, along with the condition that the existent must be set apart by a reality.

Core Idea
What can be known includes both existent things and the absolutely non-existent, considered as a mental concept.

In this passage, the author begins by defining the knowable and immediately divides it into two categories. A key condition—'even by a consideration'—ensures that the absolutely non-existent, which cannot be known in external reality, is still included in the division. This sets the stage for understanding how Islamic philosophers and theologians categorize objects of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the English translation. Notice the definition and the division.
Pay attention to the phrase 'even by a consideration'.
2
Focus on the condition
Consider why the author adds 'even by a consideration'. What objection does it preempt? How does it allow the absolutely non-existent to be part of the division?
The commentary will help you understand the reasoning.
3
Understand the objection and its repelling
The passage mentions an objection: the absolute known is impossible to know. See how the condition resolves this by distinguishing between knowledge of essence and knowledge by description.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالوا في تقسيم المعلومات (ما يمكن أن يعلم ولو باعتبار ) إما لا تحقق له بوجه من الوجوه ( وهو المعدوم، وإما له تحقق ما هو الموجود ولا بد من انحيازه بحقيقة ) أي لا بد من أن ينفرد الموجود وينحاز ويمتاز عن غيره بحقيقة يكون بها هو هو. قوله : ( ولو باعتبار فيه دفع لما يرد على التقسيم من أن المعلوم المطلق يمتنع علمه، إذ لو علم لكان له تحقق ذهني، وقد جعل قسماً مما يمكن علمه فقد جعل قسيم الشيء قسماً منه، وحاصل الدفع أنه معلوم باعتبار وصف كونه معدوماً مطلقاً داخل في المقسم، وإن كان مما علمه نظراً إلى ذاته فهو يكون فرداً للموجود الذهني باعتبار العارض مقابل للموجود باعتبار يمتنع ذاته ولا استحالة فيه. قوله: (ما يمكن أن يعلم إلخ) المعدوم المطلق ليس بمعلوم بالفعل عندهم لقولهم : بالوجود الذهني خلافاً للمتكلمين، فلذا قال : هاهنا ما يمكن أن يعلم، وقال : ثمة في المعلوم.
And they said in the division of known things: 'What can be known, even by a consideration, is either (i) something that has no actualization in any respect, which is the non-existent, or (ii) something that has some actualization, which is the existent; and it is necessary for it to be set apart by a reality.' That is, it is necessary for the existent to be singled out, set apart, and distinguished from other things by a reality through which it is what it is. His statement: 'even by a consideration' contains a repelling of the objection that could be raised against the division, namely that the absolute known is impossible to know, because if it were known it would have mental actualization, and it has been made a part of what can be known, thus making the counterpart of a thing a part of it. The summary of the repelling is that it is known by virtue of its description of being absolutely non-existent, which is included in the divided whole, although if it is known in respect of its essence, it becomes an individual of mental existence by virtue of an accident, as opposed to the existent whose essence is impossible (to not exist), and there is no impossibility in that. His statement: 'What can be known, etc.' The absolutely non-existent is not actually known in their view because of their doctrine of mental existence, contrary to the theologians, so he said here 'what can be known', and said there 'in the known'.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage defines the knowable (al-ma'lum) and divides it into two primary categories. The author (Iji) first states the definition: the knowable is 'what can be known, even if by a consideration.' The phrase 'even if by a consideration' is crucial: it allows the inclusion of the absolutely non-existent (al-ma'dum al-mutlaq) in the division, since although it cannot be known in external reality, it can be considered as a concept in the mind. The division then proceeds: everything knowable is either (a) that which has no actualization in any respect—this is the non-existent (ma'dum), or (b) that which has some actualization—this is the existent (mawjud). For the existent, it is necessary that it be set apart (yanhāzu) by a reality (haqiqah) that makes it what it is. The commentary then explains that the condition 'even if by a consideration' wards off an objection: if the absolute non-existent were truly unknowable, it could not be a part of the division; but it is knowable as a concept, hence it is included. The commentator also notes that the philosophers do not consider the absolute non-existent as actually known (bi al-fi'l) because they hold to mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni), unlike the theologians; so the author deliberately says 'what can be known' rather than 'what is known'.

Key Terms
المعلوم
المعدوم
الموجود
الوجود الذهني
الحقيقة
الاعتبار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The absolutely non-existent is completely unknowable and cannot be included in any division of knowledge.
Correction: The author includes it by considering it as a mental concept, so it is knowable by consideration even if not in external reality.

Checkpoint
Why does the author add the condition 'even if by a consideration' (wa law bi i'tibar) in the definition of the knowable?
A) To indicate that knowledge of the non-existent is impossible.
B) To include the absolutely non-existent, which would otherwise be excluded as unknowable.
C) To emphasize that only existent things can be known.
D) To restrict the division to actual knowledge only.

Key Takeaway
The knowable is defined broadly to include even the absolutely non-existent through mental consideration, and the existent is marked by being set apart by a reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you limit what you can truly know to only physical objects you can see and touch? This lesson reminds you that even non-existence is knowable as a concept. How does this expand your understanding of knowledge, and what does it say about your pursuit of knowing Allah, who is Existent but not limited by physical reality?
?
The passage says the existent must be 'set apart by a reality'. When you consider your own existence, do you know your own reality that makes you distinct? Or do you define yourself by temporary attributes? How can this help you appreciate your purpose as a created being with a fixed essence from Allah?
?
The objection raised is that the absolute known cannot be known because it would have mental actualization. In your own learning, do you sometimes reject concepts because they don't fit your immediate experience? How can you train yourself to accept knowledge that comes by 'consideration' even if it is not physically evident?

External vs Mental Existence
Iji distinguishes external and mental existence by individuation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Iji distinguishes external existence from mental existence using the criterion of personal identity and individuation.

Core Idea
External existence is individuated; mental existence is universal.

Iji begins by drawing a fundamental distinction in ontology: the difference between things that exist outside the mind and things that exist only in the mind. The key is whether the existent has a personal identity that makes it unique and non-shareable. This passage lays the foundation for understanding the reality of individuals versus universals.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Focus on the main distinction between external and mental existence.
Notice the terms 'huwiyyah shakhsiyyah' and 'mahiyyah kulliyyah'.
2
Identify the key criterion
Look for the phrase about being set apart by a personal identity that prevents sharing among many. This is the defining feature of external existence.
If an existent lacks this, it is mental.
3
Understand the reason for mental existence
The passage explains that the mind only apprehends universals. Think about why mental existents cannot have unique personal identities.
Contrast this with external existents that have both universal quiddity and particularization.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن انحاز مع ذلك عن غيره بهوية شخصية يمتنع بها فرض اشتراكه بين كثيرين فهو الموجود الخارجي وإلا فهو الموجود الذهني فإن الذهن لا يدرك إلا أمراً كليا فالموجود فيه لا ينحاز عن غيره إلا بحسب الماهية الكلية بخلاف الموجود الخارجي، فإنه ينحاز عن غيره بماهية كلية وتشخص. قوله : (ويمتاز عن غيره بحقيقية يكون بها هو هو إن ثبت أن في كل فرد حصة من الماهية مغايرة لحصة فرد آخر فعموم الغير ظاهر وإن لم يثبت فالمراد بالغير هو كل ما عداه من الأنواع وأفرادها وأما امتيازه عن سائر أفراد نوعها فهو إما بالهوية فقط، أو بمجموع الهوية والحقيقة، إذ المراد بالحقيقة ما الهوية كما سيجيء عن قريب.
If along with that it is set apart from other things by a personal identity (huwiyyah shakhsiyyah) which prevents the supposition of sharing among many, then it is the external existent; otherwise it is the mental existent, for the mind (al-dhihn) apprehends only a universal thing, so what exists in it is set apart from other things only by virtue of the universal quiddity (al-mahiyyah al-kulliyyah), contrary to the external existent, for it is set apart from other things by a universal quiddity and a particularization (tashakhkhus). His statement: 'And it is distinguished from other things by a reality through which it is what it is: if it is established that in every individual there is a portion of the essence (mahiyyah) different from the portion of another individual, then the generality of 'other' is apparent; if it is not established, then by 'other' is meant everything other than it of the species and their individuals. As for its distinction from the other individuals of its species, it is either by identity alone, or by the combination of identity and reality, for the intended meaning of 'reality' (haqiqah) is what identity is, as will come soon.'
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage defines and distinguishes between external existence (al-wujud al-khariji) and mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni). The key criterion is whether the existent is set apart (yanhāzu) by a personal identity (huwiyyah shakhsiyyah) that prevents it from being shared among many individuals. If it has such an identity, it is an external existent; if it lacks it, it is a mental existent. The author then explains the reason: the mind apprehends only universals (kulliyat), so things in the mind are distinguished only by their universal quiddity (mahiyyah kulliyyah), not by a unique identity. In contrast, external things are distinguished both by a universal quiddity and by a particularization (tashakhkhus) that makes them individuals. The commentary clarifies the phrase 'distinguished from other things by a reality through which it is what it is': the 'other' can mean either other individuals within the same species (if each individual has a distinct portion of the essence) or other species and their individuals. The distinction of an individual from its conspecifics is either by identity alone (if the essence is the same) or by both identity and reality, where 'reality' here is synonymous with identity.

Key Terms
الموجود الخارجي
الموجود الذهني
الهوية الشخصية
الماهية الكلية
التشخص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary criterion for distinguishing an external existent from a mental existent according to this passage?
A) Whether it is material or immaterial.
B) Whether it has a personal identity that prevents sharing among many.
C) Whether it is perceived by the senses or by the intellect.
D) Whether it has a universal quiddity or not.

Key Takeaway
External existence is defined by having a unique personal identity that prevents it from being shared among multiple individuals, while mental existence lacks this individuation and is universal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own identity, do you primarily see it as something external (your body, actions) or mental (your thoughts, self-concept)? The passage teaches that external existence requires a unique personal identity that cannot be shared. How does this challenge you to see your own existence as uniquely accountable before Allah?
?
The mind only apprehends universals. How often do you reduce other people to mental categories (like 'brother', 'student') instead of encountering them as unique external individuals? What would it take today to treat one person as an external existent with a personal identity rather than as a mental category?
?
If mental existence lacks personal identity, your thoughts and intentions are real but not individuated in the same way as actions. Does this give you an excuse for sinful thoughts? Or does it make them more serious because they are connected to your external self? Site one concrete thought you had today and ask: is it merely mental, or does it reflect your true personal identity? Commit to acting against it if needed.

Classification of Existence into Necessary and Possible
Al-Iji classifies existence into necessary and possible based on acceptance of non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how existence is divided into necessary and possible based on the criterion of accepting non-existence, and why the phrase 'for itself' is used for both categories.

Core Idea
Existence either cannot not exist (necessary) or can exist or not (possible).

Al-Iji begins his discussion of existence by drawing a fundamental division. This passage establishes the two primary categories—necessary and possible—and explains the precise meaning of the qualification 'for itself' (li-dhātihī). Understanding this classification is essential for all subsequent arguments about God's existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage slowly, then its English translation. Notice the concise style and key terms.
Focus on the phrases 'لذاته' and 'بالغير'.
2
Identify the two categories
Find the definition of 'necessary for itself' and 'possible for itself'. Note the criterion: acceptance of non-existence.
3
Understand the restriction
Al-Iji explains why 'for itself' is added to both. For the necessary, it excludes the necessary by another; for the possible, it is not a safeguard but for conformity. Reflect on why there is no 'possible by another'.
This clarifies that possibility is an essential property, not accidental.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والموجود في الخارج إما أن لا يقبل العدم لذاته وهو الواجب لذاته أو يقبله وهو الممكن لذاته فتقييد الواجب بقوله : لذاته احتراز عن الواجب بغيره وتقييد الممكن بذلك ليس احترازاً عن شيء إذ لا ممكن بالغير، بل هو رعاية للموافقة وإظهار لكون الإمكان مقتضى الذات كالوجوب .
And existence in the external is either not accepting non-existence for itself, and that is the necessary for itself, or accepting it, and that is the possible for itself. So restricting the necessary by His saying 'for itself' is a safeguard against the necessary by another, and restricting the possible by that is not a safeguard against anything, for there is no possible by another, rather it is for the sake of conformity and showing that possibility is a requirement of the essence like necessity.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage classifies external existence into two categories: necessary (wājib) and possible (mumkin), based on the acceptance of non-existence ('adam). The necessary is that which does not accept non-existence essentially (li-dhātihī), and the possible is that which does accept it essentially. The commentator explains that the qualification 'for itself' (li-dhātihī) for the necessary excludes the necessary by another (wājib bi-ghayrihī), such as something that becomes necessary due to a cause. For the possible, the same qualification is not a safeguard because there is no possible by another; every possible is possible in itself. Rather, the repetition of 'li-dhātihī' for the possible is for rhetorical conformity and to show that possibility, like necessity, is a requirement of the essence itself. This establishes the fundamental division of contingent beings.

Key Terms
الواجب لذاته
الممكن لذاته
العدم
الواجب بالغير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'possible by another' is a valid category along with 'necessary by another'.
Correction: Al-Iji explicitly denies this: there is no possible by another. All possibilities are essentially possible; their possibility is not borrowed from something else.

Checkpoint
Why is the phrase 'for itself' (li-dhātihī) used for both necessary and possible existence?
A) To indicate that both are caused.
B) To safeguard against the necessary by another and for conformity to show essence-based possibility.
C) To show that both are mental existents.
D) To indicate that possibility is accidental.

Key Takeaway
Existence is fundamentally divided into necessary (cannot not exist) and possible (can exist or not), with possibility being an essential property of possible beings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are a possible being—you can either exist or not exist. Your very essence is characterized by dependence on the Necessary Being. Does your daily consciousness truly reflect this dependence, or do you often act as if you are self-sufficient? What one specific habit can you change today to remind yourself of your contingency?
?
The passage says possibility is 'a requirement of the essence'—not accidental but essential to who you are. If your very nature demands that you rely on something else for existence, then all your achievements are ultimately given to you. How does this realization challenge your pride? Can you name a moment recently where you forgot this and took credit for what was granted?
?
Al-Iji uses the term 'safeguard' (ihtiraz) to protect the concept of the Necessary from being confused with what is necessary by another. In your spiritual life, do you guard the uniqueness of Allah's necessity by avoiding attributing any form of necessity to created things (e.g., thinking your job, health, or relationships are 'necessary' for your happiness)? What created 'necessity' do you need to mentally break?
