Substance and Accident: The Two Divisions of the Possible
Sanusi divides the possible into substance and accident, defining each with its relation to a subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the possible is divided into substance and accident, and how form qualifies as a substance despite existing in matter.

Core Idea
The possible is either substance or accident — but form is a substance even in matter.

This passage from Sanusi's Sharh al-Mawaqif establishes the foundational classification of all possible entities. It defines substance and accident not merely by existence in a locus but by the relationship of constitution. Understanding this distinction is crucial because it determines how we categorize beings in theology and philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once through, then the literal translation. Pay attention to the technical terms introduced.
Note that the passage includes commentary phrases (like 'قوله') which are part of the original text but can be understood as clarifications.
2
Identify the two definitions
Locate the definitions of accident and substance. Accident exists in a subject (locus) that constitutes it. Substance does not exist in a subject, whether it lacks a locus or exists in a locus that does not constitute it.
The second part of the substance definition is crucial for form.
3
Understand the exception: form
Notice that form exists in matter (a locus), but matter does not constitute form; rather form constitutes matter. Therefore, form is a substance despite being in a locus.
This challenges the naive assumption that anything in a locus is an accident.
4
Review the distinctions of generality
The passage explains that 'locus' is more general than 'matter', and 'state' is more general than 'form'. Understand how subject and matter are distinct specifics under locus, and accident and form are distinct specifics under state.
This clarifies the taxonomy of terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وهو) أي الممكن لذاته (إما أن يوجد في موضوع أي في محل يقوم ذلك المحل (ما حل فيه وهو العرض (أولا) يوجد في موضوع ( وهو الجوهر) سواء لم يوجد في محل، أو وجد في محل لا يكون موضوعاً ( فقولنا : يقوم ما حل فيه احتراز عن الصورة لوجودها في محل وهو المادة (لكنه أي ذلك المحل الذي هو المادة غير مقوم لما حل فيه وهو الصورة ) ( فإن المادة هي المتقومة بالصورة عندهم) كما ستعرفه ( فالصورة جوهر) مع كونها حالة في محل ( فالمحل أعم من المادة ) لصدق المحل على الموضوع أيضا ( والحال أعم من الصورة ) لصدق الحال على العرض أيضاً والموضوع والمادة متباينان مندرجان تحت المحل اندراج الأخص تحت الأعم، وكذا العرض والصورة متباينان مندرجان تحت الحال كذلك. قوله: ( يقوم ) أي يكون له مدخل في قوامه ووجوده . قوله: (لا يكون موضوعاً) أي مقوماً بل متقوماً . قوله: ( فإن المادة إلخ) لما تبين في محله أن الصورة علة وجود الهيولى، والهيولى يحتاج إليها في تشخصها لا في وجودها على ما قالوا تصورت فوجدت ووجدت فتصورت أي تصورت الهيولى بصورة ما، فوجدت في الخارج بصورة شخصية فافهم فإنه مما خفي على بعض الناظرين.
And it, meaning the possible by itself, either exists in a subject, i.e. in a locus such that that locus constitutes what subsists in it, and it is the accident, or it does not exist in a subject, and it is the substance, whether it does not exist in a locus, or it exists in a locus that is not a subject. So our saying: 'constitutes what subsists in it' is a safeguard against the form, because it exists in a locus which is matter, but that locus, which is matter, does not constitute what subsists in it, which is the form, for matter is constituted by form according to them, as you will learn. So the form is a substance despite being in a locus. So the locus is more general than matter, because the locus also applies to the subject, and the state is more general than the form, because the state also applies to the accident. And the subject and matter are distinct, falling under the locus as the more specific under the more general; likewise the accident and form are distinct, falling under the state similarly. His saying: ('constitutes') meaning it has an entry in its constitution and existence. His saying: ('not a subject') meaning it constitutes, rather it is constituted. His saying: ('for matter, etc.') because it has been shown in its place that form is the cause of the existence of hyle, and hyle needs it for its individuation not for its existence, according to what they said: it formed and existed, and existed and formed, meaning hyle formed with some form, then it existed externally with a particular form. So understand, for it is among what is hidden from some observers.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage classifies the possible (al-mumkin li-dhatihi) into two primary categories: substance (al-jawhar) and accident (al-'arad). It begins by stating that the possible either exists in a subject (mawdu') — meaning in a locus (mahall) that constitutes (yuqawwim) what subsists in it, which is the accident — or it does not exist in a subject, which is the substance. The definition of substance includes two cases: either it does not exist in a locus at all, or it exists in a locus that is not a subject (i.e., the locus does not constitute it). This second case is exemplified by form (sura), which exists in matter (madda) but matter does not constitute form; rather matter is constituted by form. Therefore, form is considered a substance despite being in a locus. The passage then explains that the term 'locus' (mahall) is more general than 'matter', because it also applies to the subject (mawdu') of accidents. Similarly, 'state' (hal) is more general than 'form', because it also applies to accident. The subject and matter are distinct, and both fall under locus as the more specific under the more general; likewise accident and form are distinct and fall under state. The commentary on 'constitutes' emphasizes that 'constitution' here means having an entry in the constitution and existence of the thing, not necessarily being part of its essence. The commentary on 'not a subject' clarifies that the locus (matter) does not constitute the form; rather it is constituted by the form. The final commentary elaborates on the relationship between matter (hyle) and form: form is the cause of hyle's existence, and hyle needs form for its individuation, not for its existence. This subtle point is important for understanding why form is classified as a substance.

Key Terms

الجوهر
العرض
المحل
الموضوع
المادة
الصورة
الحال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Anything that exists in a locus is an accident, because accidents are defined by inhering in a subject.
Correction: Form exists in matter (a locus) but is a substance because matter does not constitute form; rather form constitutes matter. The key is whether the locus constitutes the thing, not mere existence in a locus.

Checkpoint
Why is form considered a substance even though it exists in a locus (matter)?
A) Because form is not perceptible.
B) Because matter does not constitute form; rather form constitutes matter.
C) Because form is eternal.
D) Because form is an accident of matter.

Key Takeaway
The essential criterion for substance is not absence of a locus, but absence of a constituting subject; form is a substance because it is not constituted by its locus (matter).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson teaches that accidents depend entirely on their subject for existence. Consider your own life: are your happiness, identity, or sense of worth accidents that depend on external subjects (people, possessions, status)? If those subjects were removed, would you cease to exist as a person? What does that reveal about how you should anchor your self-worth?
?
Form is a substance even though it dwells in matter. You may find yourself in difficult circumstances, but this does not mean your essence is defined by those circumstances. Are there situations in your life where you feel 'trapped' in a matter that you believe defines you? How can the truth that form is not constituted by its locus free you to see yourself as a substance independent of your environment?
?
Sanusi's classification forces a sharp distinction between substance and accident. In your daily actions, do you treat the acts of worship (prayer, charity) as accidents that depend on perfect conditions, or as substances rooted in your soul? What practical change can you make today to ensure that your faith is not an accident dependent on comfort but a substance that stands on its own?

The Mutakallimun's Division of Existents
Sanusi presents the mutakallimun's division of existents into eternal and originated, then substance, accident, or neither.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutakallimun's twofold division of existents into eternal and originated, and the further subdivision of the originated into substance, accident, and abstract entities.

Core Idea
Everything that exists is either eternal or originated; if originated, it is either a substance, an accident, or neither.

Al-Sanusi begins by distinguishing between eternal and originated existents, a fundamental division in kalam. He then explains that the originated can be further classified into three types: substances (which occupy space), accidents (which inhere in substances), and abstract entities (which do neither). This classification is crucial for understanding how the mutakallimun think about the world and about Allah, who is neither a substance nor an accident nor originated.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very precise definitional style here.
2
Identify the primary division
The passage first divides existents into eternal and originated. Note how each is defined: eternal has no beginning, originated has a beginning.
The definition of eternal avoids mentioning time deliberately.
3
Identify the three subcategories of originated
The originated is further divided into substance (extended by itself), accident (a state in substance), and neither. Understand the criterion: 'pointability' with sensory indication.
Substance is defined as what can be pointed to by itself.
4
Reflect on the commentary points
Read the commentary to understand why the mutakallimun avoid defining eternal as 'not preceded by time' and what 'pointed to' really means.
This clarifies doctrinal nuances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال المتكلمون الموجود أي في الخارج إذ لا يثبتون الموجود الذهني إما أن لا يكون له أول أي لا يقف وجوده عند حد يكون قبله العدم وهو القديم أو يكون له أول أي يقف وجوده عند حد يكون قبله العدم وهو الحادث والحادث إما متحيز بالذات أو حال في المتحيز بالذات أو لا متحيز ولا حال فيه فالمتحيز بالذات هو الجوهر ونعني به أي بالمتحيز بالذات المشار إليه الذي يشار إليه بالذات إشارة حسية بأنه هنا أو هناك اعتبر قيد بالذات احترازاً عن العرض فإنه قابل للإشارة على سبيل التبعية، وقيد الإشارة بكونها حسية لأن المجردات على تقدير وجودها قابلة للإشارة العقلية والحال في. قوله: ( أي لا يقف وجوده إلخ) لم يقل : لا يكون وجوده مسبوقاً بالعدم زماناً لأنه يشعر بقدم الزمان وتقسيم القدم إلى الذاتي والزماني والمتكلمون لا يقولون بشيء منها . قوله: (أي الذي يشار إليه يعني أن المراد بالمشار إليه ما يقبل الإشارة.
And the mutakallimun said: the existent, i.e. in the external world, for they do not affirm mental existence, either has no beginning, i.e. its existence does not stop at a limit before which there is non-existence, and it is the eternal; or it has a beginning, i.e. its existence stops at a limit before which there is non-existence, and it is the originated. And the originated is either extended (mutahayyiz) by itself, or a state (hal) in what is extended by itself, or neither extended nor a state in it. So the extended by itself is the substance, and by 'extended by itself' we mean that which is pointed to, which is pointed to by itself with a sensory indication that it is here or there. The restriction 'by itself' is a safeguard against the accident, for it is capable of indication in a derivative way; and the restriction of indication as sensory is because abstract entities, if they exist, are capable of intellectual indication. And the state in. His saying: ('i.e. its existence does not stop') He did not say: its existence is not preceded by non-existence in time, because that suggests the eternity of time and the division of eternity into essential and temporal, and the mutakallimun do not affirm any of them. His saying: ('i.e. that which is pointed to') means that by 'pointed to' is meant that which accepts indication.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage presents the classification of existents (al-mawjud) according to the mutakallimun (theologians). It begins by noting that they consider only external existence, not mental existence. They divide existents into two primary categories: the eternal (al-qadim) and the originated (al-hadith). The eternal is that which has no beginning — its existence does not end at a limit before which there is non-existence. The originated is that which has a beginning — its existence does end at a limit before which there is non-existence. The originated is further divided into three: (1) that which is extended (mutahayyiz) by itself, (2) that which is a state (hal) in what is extended by itself, and (3) that which is neither extended nor a state. The extended by itself is the substance (al-jawhar), and it is defined as that which is pointed to (musharun ilayhi) by itself with a sensory indication, such as 'here' or 'there'. The qualification 'by itself' guards against the accident, which can be pointed to only derivatively (by being in a substance). The qualification 'sensory' guards against abstract entities, which, if they exist, can be indicated only intellectually. The commentary then explains two points: first, the definition of eternal does not mention 'not preceded by non-existence in time' because that would imply the eternity of time and a division of eternity into essential and temporal, which the mutakallimun reject. Second, the phrase 'pointed to' means that which is capable of indication, not that it is actually being pointed to.

Key Terms
الموجود
القديم
الحادث
المتحيز بالذات
الجوهر
الإشارة الحسية
المجردات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the eternal is simply 'what is not preceded by time'.
Correction: The mutakallimun avoid that definition because it implies the eternity of time and a division of eternity into essential and temporal, which they do not affirm. Instead, they define eternal as 'that which has no beginning' without reference to time.

Checkpoint
Why do the mutakallimun avoid defining the eternal as 'not preceded by non-existence in time'?
A) Because time is not real.
B) Because it implies the eternity of time and a division of eternity that they reject.
C) Because the eternal is not related to time.
D) Because time is an accident.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun classify all existents into eternal and originated, and further subdivide the originated into substance, accident, or abstract, using 'pointability' as a criterion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are an originated being, dependent on the Eternal for every moment of your existence. Do your daily choices reflect this dependence, or do you live as if you are self-sufficient? What would it look like to truly live as someone who could have not existed?
?
The mutakallimun deny mental existence, yet your inner world of thoughts and intentions is undeniably real to you. How do you ensure that your inner life aligns with the external reality of the Eternal, rather than becoming a self-contained illusion?

Definitions of Eternal and Temporal in Kalam
Sanusi enumerates the two divisions of existence: eternal and temporal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mutakallimun define 'eternal' and 'temporal' based on the concept of a beginning limit before which there is non-existence.

Core Idea
Eternal and temporal are defined by the presence or absence of a beginning, not by time.

The mutakallimun (Islamic theologians) begin their discussion of creation by dividing all existent things into two categories: eternal (qadim) and temporal (hadith). Their definition is carefully constructed to avoid metaphysical pitfalls. Notice that they define these terms using the notion of a 'limit' rather than chronological priority.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic definition
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze. Then read the translation. Let the author's own words introduce the two categories.
The passage defines 'existent' as external existence first.
2
Identify the two categories
Find the definitions of 'eternal' and 'temporal'. Note that each definition includes the phrase 'a limit before which there is non-existence'.
3
Understand the significance of 'limit'
Reflect on why the mutakallimun use 'does not stop at a limit' rather than 'not preceded by time'. This distinction is crucial for their broader metaphysics.
The next lesson will delve into the reasons for this wording.

وقال المتكلمون الموجود أي في الخارج إذ لا يثبتون ( الموجود ) الذهني إما أن لا يكون له أول أي لا يقف وجوده عند حد يكون قبله ( أي قبل ذلك الحد ) العدم وهو القديم أو يكون له أول ( أي يقف وجوده عند حد يكون قبله العدم ) وهو الحادث . قوله : ( أي لا يقف وجوده إلخ) لم يقل : لا يكون وجوده مسبوقاً بالعدم زماناً لأنه يشعر بقدم الزمان وتقسيم القدم إلى الذاتي والزماني والمتكلمون لا يقولون بشيء منها .
The mutakallimun said: The existent — i.e., in the external [world], since they do not affirm mental existence — is either that which has no beginning, meaning its existence does not stop at a limit before which (i.e., before that limit) there is non-existence, and it is the eternal; or it has a beginning, meaning its existence stops at a limit before which there is non-existence, and it is the temporal. His statement: (meaning does not stop at a limit, etc.) He did not say: its existence is not preceded by non-existence in time, because that would imply the eternity of time and the division of eternity into essential and temporal, and the mutakallimun do not affirm any of them.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage defines the two primary divisions of existence according to the mutakallimun: the eternal and the temporal. The definition focuses on the concept of a 'beginning' (awwal) as a limit before which there is non-existence, rather than a temporal priority, to avoid implying the eternity of time. The commentator explains why the author chose the wording 'does not stop at a limit' instead of 'not preceded by non-existence in time' — the latter would commit the mutakallimun to a problematic view of time and a division of eternity that they reject.

The mutakallimun define eternal and temporal based on the presence of a beginning limit, not on temporal duration.

Why Precise Wording Matters: Avoiding the Eternity of Time
Sanusi explains why the author avoided defining eternal in terms of time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author avoided saying 'not preceded by non-existence in time' and what theological issues it avoids.

Core Idea
Wording implies theological commitments; careful phrasing protects doctrine.

Having defined eternal and temporal, the commentator explains why the author used the phrase 'does not stop at a limit' rather than the more common 'not preceded by non-existence in time'. This subtle choice reveals a deep awareness of metaphysical implications regarding the nature of time.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentator's remark
Read the second part of the passage carefully. Note the exact wording that was avoided and the reason given.
2
Understand the rejected wording
Identify the phrase 'not preceded by non-existence in time'. Understand that this wording is plausible but rejected.
The commentator says the author did not use this expression.
3
Analyze the reasons
The commentator gives two reasons: it would imply the eternity of time and it would introduce a division of eternity into essential and temporal. Consider why these are problematic for the mutakallimun.
The mutakallimun believe time is created, so they cannot assume its eternity.

Division of Temporal into Three Categories
Sanusi classifies the temporal into three categories and defines the self-subsistent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mutakallimun classify the temporal into three categories and how they define the self-subsistent (substance) through the concept of sensory indication.

Core Idea
Everything temporal is either self-subsistent, inherent, or neither.

This passage introduces the fundamental classification of created things (al-ḥādith) upon which much of kalam reasoning relies. Sanusi presents three categories — self-subsistent (substance), inherent in a self-subsistent (accident), and neither. Then he clarifies the definition of self-subsistent, addressing potential confusions about what it means to be 'pointed to' sensorily versus mentally. This sets the stage for understanding how substances and accidents are distinguished.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully along with the translation. Notice the three categories stated at the beginning: self-subsistent, inherent, and neither.
Pay attention to the qualifiers 'by essence' and 'sensory indication'.
2
Identify the definition
Find the definition of 'self-subsistent by essence' (mutahayyiz bi-dhāt). Note that it is defined as that which can be pointed to sensorily by itself, e.g., 'here or there'.
The definition excludes accidents and abstract entities through its qualifiers.
3
Understand the exclusions
Think about why the accident is excluded by 'sensory indication' rather than 'by essence'. The commentary clarifies that accidents can be pointed to mentally by essence but externally only dependently.
This shows the care the mutakallimun take in precise terminology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحادث إما متحيز ( بالذات ) أو حال في المتحيز ( بالذات ) أو لا متحيز ولا حال فيه فالمتحيز بالذات ( هو الجوهر ونعني به أي بالمتحيز بالذات ( المشار إليه ) أي الذي يشار إليه ( بالذات إشارة حسية بأنه هنا أو هناك ) اعتبر قيد بالذات احترازاً عن العرض فإنه قابل للإشارة على سبيل التبعية، وقيد الإشارة بكونها حسية لأن المجردات على تقدير وجودها قابلة للإشارة العقلية ( والحال في المتحيز هو العرض . قوله : (أي الذي يشار إليه يعني أن المراد بالمشار إليه ما يقبل الإشارة. قوله : ( فإنه قابل إلخ) أي في الوجود الخارجي قابل للإشارة بتبعية المحل وإن كان قابلاً في الوجود العقلي بالذات لامتياز العرض عن الجوهر عند العقل والمراد بقولنا : المشار إليه ما يشار إليه في الوجود الخارجي، فلا يرد أن العرض مشار إليه بالذات بالإشارة العقلية، فالاحتراز عنه إنما هو بقوله إشارة حسية لا بقوله : بالذات ولا يحتاج إلى ما قيل أن قوله بالذات متعلقة بقوله إشارة حسية فهو متأخر عنه، وإنما قدمه الشارح في البيان رعاية لظاهر المتن، فإنه يأبى عنه عدم تقييد الشارح الإشارة بالحسية في قوله : فإنه قابل للإشارة على سبيل التبعية . قوله: (لأن المجردات على تقدير وجودها إذ على تقدير عدمها تكون مشاراً إليها
The temporal is either self-subsistent (by essence) or inherent in a self-subsistent (by essence) or neither self-subsistent nor inherent. The self-subsistent by essence is the substance, and by it (i.e., by the self-subsistent by essence) we mean that which is pointed to (i.e., that which can be pointed to) by essence with a sensory indication, such as 'it is here or there'. The qualification 'by essence' was added to exclude the accident, for it is capable of being pointed to by way of dependency. The qualification of the indication as sensory was added because abstract entities, if they exist, are capable of being pointed to mentally. And the inherent in the self-subsistent is the accident. His statement: (meaning that which is pointed to) means that what is meant by 'pointed to' is that which accepts indication. His statement: (for it is capable, etc.) means that in external existence it is capable of being pointed to by dependency on the substratum, although in mental existence it is capable by essence because the accident is distinguished from the substance by the intellect. And what we mean by our saying 'pointed to' is that which is pointed to in external existence, so it is not objected that the accident is pointed to by essence with mental indication. Therefore the exclusion of the accident is only by his saying 'sensory indication', not by his saying 'by essence'. And there is no need for what is said that his saying 'by essence' is connected to his saying 'sensory indication' and is posterior to it, and the commentator only presented it in the explanation out of regard for the apparent meaning of the text, for it refuses the commentator's not qualifying the indication as sensory in his saying 'for it is capable of being pointed to by way of dependency'. His statement: (because abstract entities, if they exist, for if they do not exist, they are pointed to)
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage classifies the temporal (ḥādith) into three categories: (1) self-subsistent by essence (mutahayyiz bi-dhāt), which is the substance (jawhar); (2) inherent in a self-subsistent (ḥāl fī al-mutahayyiz), which is the accident; and (3) neither. The definition of 'self-subsistent by essence' is given as that which can be pointed to sensorily by itself, as in 'here or there'. The commentator clarifies the qualification 'by essence' to exclude the accident, which is only pointed to indirectly through its substratum, and 'sensory' to exclude abstract entities (mujarradāt) which, if they exist, are only pointed to mentally. The commentary further explains why the accident is not excluded by 'by essence' but by 'sensory indication', and addresses a potential objection about the order of the qualifiers.

Key Terms

جوهر
عرض
مشار إليه
إشارة حسية
حال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'pointed to' refers only to physical objects, and that 'by essence' alone excludes accidents.
Correction: The mutakallimun carefully distinguish sensory vs. mental pointing and essential vs. dependent pointing. Accidents are excluded by 'sensory' because they are pointed to only dependently in external existence, while 'by essence' would not exclude them in mental existence.

Checkpoint
How is the accident excluded from the definition of the self-subsistent (mutahayyiz bi-dhāt)?
A) By the phrase 'by essence' because the accident is pointed to by essence mentally.
B) By the phrase 'sensory indication' because the accident is pointed to only sensorily.
C) By the phrase 'by essence' because the accident is not pointed to at all.
D) By the phrase 'sensory indication' because the accident is pointed to only by dependency on its substratum in external existence.

Key Takeaway
The three categories of the temporal — substance, accident, and neither — are defined by how they relate to sensory indication, which is a key tool for distinguishing created beings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the things you rely on daily, how many of them are accidents (temporary qualities) that inhere in a substance but themselves pass away? What does this fragility reveal about your ultimate reliance?
?
The passage distinguishes sensory indication from mental indication. In your own life, do you tend to trust only what is physically tangible, or do you give weight to intellectual and spiritual realities that cannot be pointed to with the senses? Which has led you to truer knowledge?
?
If everything temporal is either substance, accident, or neither, then your own existence—your body and your soul—falls into one of these categories. Which are you most identified with: your changing accidents (moods, possessions) or your enduring substance (your essential self)? How would your priorities shift if you truly lived by this distinction?

Definition of Inherence: Unified Sensory Indication
Sanusi defines inherence and clarifies its conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that inherence (ḥulūl) is defined as specific attachment resulting in a single sensory indication, and how this definition avoids objections regarding God's attributes.

Core Idea
Inherence means the accident and its substratum share one sensory pointer.

Sanusi begins by defining inherence as it applies to accidents in a self-subsistent substance. He stresses that true inherence involves a unique specificity, such that pointing to the accident is the same as pointing to the substance. This careful definition prevents misunderstandings about God's attributes and clarifies borderline cases like water in a jug.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the core definition of ḥulūl as specific attachment with unified sensory indication.
Notice the example of color and the colored.
2
Examine the water-in-jug counterexample
Consider why water in a jug does not count as true inherence according to this definition — the indications are separate.
3
Understand the caution about water in a rose
Sanusi adds a caution: even when indications are one, there must be specificity; water permeating a rose lacks this because each exists independently.
4
Address the objection about God's attributes
The definition is limited to accidents in self-subsistents, so it does not apply to God's attributes, which are not accidents.
This shows the theological care in the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحال في المتحيز هو العرض ونعني بالحلول فيه ( أي في المتحيز ) أن يختص به بحيث تكون الإشارة ( الحسية ) إليهما واحدة كاللون مع المتلون فإن الإشارة إلى أحدهما عين الإشارة إلى الآخر ( دون الماء مع الكوز ) فإن الإشارة إليهما ليست واحدة، فإن الماء حالاً في الكوز اصطلاحاً وإن كان حالاً فيه لغة، وما ذكره تفسير للحلول في المتحيز كما صرح به، فلا يتجه عليه أنه لا يتناول حلول صفات الواجب تعالى في ذاته، فالأولى أن يفسر بالاختصاص الناعت وما ليس متحيزاً ولا حالاً فيه أعني الذي جعلناه قسماً ثالثاً من أقسام الممكن الحادث وهو المسمى بالمجرد لم يثبت وجوده . قوله : ( أن يختص به احتراز عن الماء الساري في الورد فإنه وإن كان الإشارة إليهما واحدة لكن لا اختصاص لأحدهما بالآخر، فإنه فرع وجود كل منهما في نفسه، ولا وجود للورد بدون الماء الساري فيه . قوله : ( فلا يتجه عليه أنه لا يتناول إلخ) الأظهر أن يقال : لا حلول للصفات في ذاته تعالى بل هي قائمة به تعالى . قوله : (لا يتناول حلول إلخ لعدم الاتحاد في الإشارة أما في العقلية فلامتناع اتحاد الشيئين في الإشارة العقلية وأما في الحسية فلامتناعها في الواجب وما قيل: إنه على تقدير قبوله الإشارة الحسية يتحد الإشارة إليهما فممنوع لجواز استلزام المحال المحال . قوله : ( وهو المسمى بالمجرد أي الممكن لا يكون متحيزاً، ولا حالاً فيه يسمى مجرداً بالاتفاق وأما كونه حادثاً أو قديماً ذاتاً أو صفة فخارج عن مفهومه، ولذا يستدل الحكماء على قدمه بأنه لو لم يكن قديماً لزم أن يكون مادياً، لأن كل حادث مسبوق بمادته، وجعله المتكلمون قسماً للحادث بناء على أن كل ممكن حادث عندهم. قوله : (لم يثبت وجوده إلخ فالقسمة المذكورة قسمة عقلية، وما قيل : إنه إنما يتم لو لم يجوز العقل قسماً رابعاً فمدفوع بأن القسمة المذكورة في الحقيقة دائرة بين النفي والإثبات، كأنه قيل : الحادث إما متحيز بالذات ،أولا والثاني إما متحيز بالعرض أو لا فكيف يتصور قسم رابع؟
The inherent in the self-subsistent is the accident. By 'inherence in it' (i.e., in the self-subsistent) we mean that it is specifically attached to it such that the sensory indication to both is one, like color with the colored, for the indication to one of them is identical to the indication to the other — unlike water with the jug, for the indication to them is not one, even though water is called in the jug terminologically although it is in it linguistically. What he mentioned is an interpretation of inherence in the self-subsistent, as he stated, so it is not valid to object that it does not include the inherence of the attributes of the Necessary Exalted in His essence; rather, it is better to interpret it as qualifying specific attachment. And what is neither self-subsistent nor inherent — I mean that which we made a third division of the possible temporal, and which is called the abstract — its existence is not proven. His statement: (that it is specifically attached to it) is a caution against water permeating a rose, for although the indication to both is one, there is no specificity of one to the other, since it depends on the existence of each in itself, and the rose has no existence without the water permeating it. His statement: (so it is not valid to object that it does not include, etc.) The more apparent is that one should say: There is no inherence of attributes in His essence, Exalted, rather they are subsistent by Him, Exalted. His statement: (does not include inherence, etc.) because there is no unity of indication — as for mental indication, because the unity of two things in mental indication is impossible; as for sensory, because it is impossible in the case of the Necessary; and what is said: that if sensory indication is accepted for Him, the indication to both would be one, is rejected because it is permissible that impossibility entails impossibility. His statement: (and it is called the abstract) i.e., the possible that is neither self-subsistent nor inherent is called abstract by agreement; as for whether it is temporal or eternal, essence or attribute, that is outside its concept. Hence the philosophers argue for its eternity: if it were not eternal, it would have to be material, because every temporal is preceded by its matter. The mutakallimun made it a division of the temporal on the basis that every possible is temporal in their view. His statement: (its existence is not proven, etc.) So the mentioned division is a rational division. And what is said: that it is only valid if the intellect does not allow a fourth division, is refuted because the mentioned division in reality revolves around negation and affirmation, as if it were said: The temporal is either self-subsistent by essence or not; the second is either self-subsistent by accident or not; so how can a fourth division be conceived?
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage defines inherence (ḥulūl) as a specific attachment such that sensory indication to both the substratum and the accident is one — exemplified by color and the colored, as opposed to water in a jug where the indication is not one. This definition is limited to inherence in a self-subsistent, so it does not apply to the attributes of God. The commentator clarifies the caution against a case like water in a rose, where there is no specificity. Then the passage discusses the third category, the abstract (mujarrad), which is neither self-subsistent nor inherent. The mutakallimun do not affirm its existence; it is only a rational division. The commentator explains that the division is exhaustive (self-subsistent or not, and then self-subsistent by accident or not), so no fourth category is possible. He also notes that philosophers argue for the eternity of the abstract, but mutakallimun deny that.

Key Terms
الحلول
الاختصاص
المتحيز
العرض
الإشارة الحسية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Inherence is simply being spatially inside another thing, like water in a jug.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that true inherence requires a specific attachment such that sensory indication to both is one, which excludes cases like water in a jug where indications are separate.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why is water in a jug not an example of true inherence?
A) Because water is not an accident.
B) Because the sensory indications to water and jug are not one.
C) Because the jug is not a self-subsistent.
D) Because water is not specifically attached.

Key Takeaway
Inherence (ḥulūl) is defined as a specific attachment that makes sensory indication to the accident and its substratum identical, a condition not met by mere spatial containment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that God's attributes are not inherence, but rather attributes of His essence. Does your heart truly affirm that God is absolutely unlike His creation, or do you find yourself inadvertently imagining Him as having spatial or composite characteristics? Take a moment to repent of any anthropomorphic thoughts and affirm tanzīh.
?
Today's lesson challenges you to examine how you perceive the connection between God and His attributes. Do you ever speak of God's mercy or power as if they are separate 'things' inhering in Him, rather than being aspects of His very essence? Commit to correcting your understanding in prayer tonight.
?
You now know that inherence requires unified sensory indication. How does this precision affect the way you think about created things? Will you stop attributing to God the categories of created existence, and instead let your belief in His uniqueness transform your worship from this moment?

The Abstract (Mujarrad): Its Status and Logical Division
Sanusi introduces the abstract as the third category and explains its status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the abstract (mujarrad) is a logical category of possible beings that are neither self-subsistent nor accidents, and why mutakallimun consider its existence unproven.

Core Idea
The abstract is a possible being that is neither a substance nor an accident, but its existence is only rational, not actual.

After defining inherence, Sanusi turns to the third division of temporal possible beings: the abstract (mujarrad). This category includes entities that are neither self-subsistent by essence nor inhering in a self-subsistent. He explains that the existence of such entities is not affirmed by the mutakallimun, and that the division into three is logically exhaustive, leaving no room for a fourth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the third category
Read the passage to find the definition of mujarrad as what is neither self-subsistent nor inherent.
2
Understand the disagreement between philosophers and mutakallimun
Note that philosophers argue for the eternity of the abstract, while mutakallimun consider every possible to be temporal.
This shows a key difference.
3
Examine the logical division
The commentator explains that the division of the temporal into three is exhaustive through a negation/affirmation process.
Understand why no fourth category is possible.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحال في المتحيز هو العرض ونعني بالحلول فيه ( أي في المتحيز ) أن يختص به بحيث تكون الإشارة ( الحسية ) إليهما واحدة كاللون مع المتلون فإن الإشارة إلى أحدهما عين الإشارة إلى الآخر ( دون الماء مع الكوز ) فإن الإشارة إليهما ليست واحدة، فإن الماء حالاً في الكوز اصطلاحاً وإن كان حالاً فيه لغة، وما ذكره تفسير للحلول في المتحيز كما صرح به، فلا يتجه عليه أنه لا يتناول حلول صفات الواجب تعالى في ذاته، فالأولى أن يفسر بالاختصاص الناعت وما ليس متحيزاً ولا حالاً فيه أعني الذي جعلناه قسماً ثالثاً من أقسام الممكن الحادث وهو المسمى بالمجرد لم يثبت وجوده . قوله : ( أن يختص به احتراز عن الماء الساري في الورد فإنه وإن كان الإشارة إليهما واحدة لكن لا اختصاص لأحدهما بالآخر، فإنه فرع وجود كل منهما في نفسه، ولا وجود للورد بدون الماء الساري فيه . قوله : ( فلا يتجه عليه أنه لا يتناول إلخ) الأظهر أن يقال : لا حلول للصفات في ذاته تعالى بل هي قائمة به تعالى . قوله : (لا يتناول حلول إلخ لعدم الاتحاد في الإشارة أما في العقلية فلامتناع اتحاد الشيئين في الإشارة العقلية وأما في الحسية فلامتناعها في الواجب وما قيل: إنه على تقدير قبوله الإشارة الحسية يتحد الإشارة إليهما فممنوع لجواز استلزام المحال المحال . قوله : ( وهو المسمى بالمجرد أي الممكن لا يكون متحيزاً، ولا حالاً فيه يسمى مجرداً بالاتفاق وأما كونه حادثاً أو قديماً ذاتاً أو صفة فخارج عن مفهومه، ولذا يستدل الحكماء على قدمه بأنه لو لم يكن قديماً لزم أن يكون مادياً، لأن كل حادث مسبوق بمادته، وجعله المتكلمون قسماً للحادث بناء على أن كل ممكن حادث عندهم. قوله : (لم يثبت وجوده إلخ فالقسمة المذكورة قسمة عقلية، وما قيل : إنه إنما يتم لو لم يجوز العقل قسماً رابعاً فمدفوع بأن القسمة المذكورة في الحقيقة دائرة بين النفي والإثبات، كأنه قيل : الحادث إما متحيز بالذات ،أولا والثاني إما متحيز بالعرض أو لا فكيف يتصور قسم رابع؟
The inherent in the self-subsistent is the accident. By 'inherence in it' (i.e., in the self-subsistent) we mean that it is specifically attached to it such that the sensory indication to both is one, like color with the colored, for the indication to one of them is identical to the indication to the other — unlike water with the jug, for the indication to them is not one, even though water is called in the jug terminologically although it is in it linguistically. What he mentioned is an interpretation of inherence in the self-subsistent, as he stated, so it is not valid to object that it does not include the inherence of the attributes of the Necessary Exalted in His essence; rather, it is better to interpret it as qualifying specific attachment. And what is neither self-subsistent nor inherent — I mean that which we made a third division of the possible temporal, and which is called the abstract — its existence is not proven. His statement: (that it is specifically attached to it) is a caution against water permeating a rose, for although the indication to both is one, there is no specificity of one to the other, since it depends on the existence of each in itself, and the rose has no existence without the water permeating it. His statement: (so it is not valid to object that it does not include, etc.) The more apparent is that one should say: There is no inherence of attributes in His essence, Exalted, rather they are subsistent by Him, Exalted. His statement: (does not include inherence, etc.) because there is no unity of indication — as for mental indication, because the unity of two things in mental indication is impossible; as for sensory, because it is impossible in the case of the Necessary; and what is said: that if sensory indication is accepted for Him, the indication to both would be one, is rejected because it is permissible that impossibility entails impossibility. His statement: (and it is called the abstract) i.e., the possible that is neither self-subsistent nor inherent is called abstract by agreement; as for whether it is temporal or eternal, essence or attribute, that is outside its concept. Hence the philosophers argue for its eternity: if it were not eternal, it would have to be material, because every temporal is preceded by its matter. The mutakallimun made it a division of the temporal on the basis that every possible is temporal in their view. His statement: (its existence is not proven, etc.) So the mentioned division is a rational division. And what is said: that it is only valid if the intellect does not allow a fourth division, is refuted because the mentioned division in reality revolves around negation and affirmation, as if it were said: The temporal is either self-subsistent by essence or not; the second is either self-subsistent by accident or not; so how can a fourth division be conceived?
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage defines inherence (ḥulūl) as a specific attachment such that sensory indication to both the substratum and the accident is one — exemplified by color and the colored, as opposed to water in a jug where the indication is not one. This definition is limited to inherence in a self-subsistent, so it does not apply to the attributes of God. The commentator clarifies the caution against a case like water in a rose, where there is no specificity. Then the passage discusses the third category, the abstract (mujarrad), which is neither self-subsistent nor inherent. The mutakallimun do not affirm its existence; it is only a rational division. The commentator explains that the division is exhaustive (self-subsistent or not, and then self-subsistent by accident or not), so no fourth category is possible. He also notes that philosophers argue for the eternity of the abstract, but mutakallimun deny that.

المتحيز بالذات
المتحيز بالعرض
القسمة العقلية
الممكن الحادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mutakallimun affirm the existence of the abstract (mujarrad) as a real entity.
Correction: Sanusi states that the existence of the abstract is not proven; it is only a rational division.

Checkpoint

A) Temporal
B) Eternal
C) Neither
D) Both

Key Takeaway
The abstract (mujarrad) is a logical category of possible beings that are neither substances nor accidents, but its actual existence is not affirmed by mutakallimun.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The abstract is a rational possibility but not actual. What abstract concepts do you treat as real in your daily life (like 'justice', 'kindness', 'intention')? How would your relationship with Allah change if you truly believed that only what can be pointed to is real?
?
Mutakallimun considered all possibles temporally originated. Do you live as if your own existence is merely an accident in time, or do you see it as part of a divine plan? How does this lesson's emphasis on exhaustive categories push you to examine whether your beliefs about the unseen are based on rational inference or revelation?
?
Sanusi distinguishes between what is self-subsistent and what is inherent. Which aspects of your character do you consider 'self-subsistent' (essential) and which 'accidental'? How can this logical distinction help you purify your intentions?

Second Proof: Necessity of a Self-Evident Premise
Al-Jurjani presents an alternative proof and addresses an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Jurjani argues that even if conception is acquired, it must trace back to a self-evident positive premise, and how he responds to the objection of circularity.

Core Idea
Every evidence chain ends in a self-evident positive premise, which presupposes the self-evident conception of absolute existence.

This passage presents an alternative proof for the self-evidence of the conception of absolute existence. It begins by assuming for argument's sake that knowledge of one's own existence is acquired. Even then, the chain of evidence must terminate in a self-evident premise that includes a positive judgment of existence. This leads to an objection: the proof seems to assume what it is trying to prove. The passage then clarifies that the proof is valid when understood as an analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the core proof
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noticing the key terms and the structure of the argument.
The passage includes an objection and its resolution.
2
Identify the assumption
The proof begins by conceding, for the sake of argument, that knowledge of one's own existence is acquired (kasbi). This is a hypothetical concession.
This step is crucial to avoid circularity.
3
Understand the chain of evidence
Even if acquired, knowledge must be based on evidence. That evidence cannot be two negative propositions, so it must include a positive premise. This positive premise must ultimately be self-evident to avoid infinite regress.
The necessity of a self-evident premise is the core of the proof.
4
Grasp the objection
The objection states that the entire discussion is about whether the conception of existence is acquired; the proof uses a premise that already involves existence, so it seems circular.
The objection is that the proof presumes what it sets out to prove.
5
See the resolution
The commentator explains that the proof can be interpreted as an analogy to the earlier proof about the self-evidence of particular existence, thus avoiding circularity.
The resolution shows that the proof is valid when properly understood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالبديهة بديهي قال : هاهنا (أو نقول بعد التنزل إلى كونه كسبياً لا بد من الانتهاء إلى دليل ( ولا دليل عن سالبتين فلا بد ) في الدليل ( من مقدمة موجبة قد حكم فيها بوجود المحمول (للموضوع ولا يمكن أن يكون العلم بوجود كل محمول للموضوع مستفاداً من دليل آخر بل لا بد من الانتهاء إلى دليل مشتمل على موجبه يكون العلم بوجود محمولها لموضوعها بديهيا ( وأنه يستدعي تصور الوجود المطلق) بطريق البداهة فاتجه الإشكال بأن الكلام في اكتساب التصور، وما ذكرتم من المقدمة قوله : ( فاتجه الإشكال) عطف على قوله : قال هاهنا وأشار بترتبه على ذلك القول إلى أن ما قبله ليس منشأ للإشكال لأنه يمكن حمل الدليل فيه على الطريق الموصل كما في كلام الإمام إنما نشأ الإشكال من هذا القول وهو ظاهر. قوله : ( أو نقول إلخ) قيل : يحتمل أن يكون المعنى أو نقول بعد تسليم التسلسل اللازم من كون العلم بوجود كل دليل مستفاداً من دليل آخر يتم الدليل على بداهة تصور الوجود، فإنه لا دليل على سالبتين إلخ ويحتمل أن يكون تصويراً لحاصل الوجه الأول بطريق آخر، فإن حاصله أن بداهة تصور الوجود الخاص يستلزم بداهة تصور الوجود المطلق، فأشار أولاً إلى استلزام بداهة الوجود الخاص المحمول للمطلوب، وثانياً إلى استلزام بداهة الوجود الخاص الرابط وأنت خبير بأن الاحتمال الثاني إنما يستقيم إذا حمل كلام المصنف على ظاهره ، وأما إذا حمل على التنظير كما يدل عليه قول الشارح فلعله أراد إلخ، فلا . وأما الاحتمال الأول فبطلانه أظهر، إذ على تقدير تسليم التسلسل لا ينفع تحقق المقدمة الموجبة في استلزام بداهة الوجود، لأن وجود كل محمول لموضوع يجوز حينئذ أن يكون مستفاداً من دليل آخر، فلا يثبت بداهة وجود أصلاً فليتأمل . قوله : ( ولا دليل عن (سالبتين ولو سلم فمورد السلب هو النسبة الإيجابية أي نسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع بوجوده له وبه يتم المقصود.
By self-evidence it is self-evident. He said: Here (or we say after descending to its being acquired: it is necessary to end at an evidence (and there is no evidence from two negatives, so it is necessary) in the evidence (from a positive premise in which the existence of the predicate is judged (for the subject), and it is not possible that knowledge of the existence of every predicate for the subject be derived from another evidence, but it is necessary to end at an evidence containing its positive in which the knowledge of the existence of its predicate for its subject is self-evident (and that it requires the conception of absolute existence) by way of self-evidence. So the objection arises that the discourse is about acquisition of conception, and what you mentioned of the premise. His statement: (So the objection arises) is an apposition to his statement: He said here, and he indicated by its dependence on that statement that what precedes is not the source of the objection, because it is possible to interpret the evidence there as the leading path as in the Imam's speech; the objection only arises from this statement, which is apparent. His statement: (Or we say etc.) It is said: it is possible that the meaning is: or we say after conceding the necessary regression from the fact that knowledge of the existence of every evidence is derived from another evidence, the proof for the self-evidence of the conception of existence is completed, for there is no evidence from two negatives, etc. And it is possible that it is a depiction of the summary of the first way by another path, for its summary is that the self-evidence of the conception of particular existence necessitates the self-evidence of the conception of absolute existence. So he pointed first to the necessitation of the self-evidence of the particular existence as predicate for the sought, and second to the necessitation of the self-evidence of the particular existence as copula. And you are aware that the second possibility is only sound if the author's speech is taken literally; but if it is taken as an analogy as indicated by the commentator's statement 'perhaps he intended etc.', then no. As for the first possibility, its falsity is more apparent, because upon conceding regression, the actualization of the positive premise is of no use in necessitating the self-evidence of existence, because the existence of every predicate for a subject could then be derived from another evidence, so no self-evidence of existence is established at all, so consider. His statement: (And there is no evidence from two negatives) even if conceded, the subject of the negation is the affirmative relation, i.e., the relation of the predicate to the subject by its existence for it, and by this the intended purpose is completed.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage presents an alternative proof that the conception of absolute existence is self-evident. It begins by assuming, for argument's sake, that knowledge of one's own existence is acquired. Even then, we must eventually reach an evidence that is self-evident to avoid infinite regress. The evidence cannot be composed of two negative propositions, so it must include a positive premise affirming existence. This positive premise must be self-evident, and it necessarily involves the concept of absolute existence. An objection arises: the entire discussion is about whether the conception of existence is acquired; using a premise that includes existence seems to beg the question. The commentator then analyzes two possible interpretations of the argument. Under the first interpretation, the argument is circular; under the second, it is a legitimate depiction of the first argument. The commentator rejects the first interpretation and favors the second, concluding that the intended purpose is achieved. The final remark clarifies that the 'no evidence from two negatives' refers to the affirmative relation being negated.

Key Terms
بديهة
سالبتين
مقدمة موجبة
تصور الوجود المطلق
اكتساب التصور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection successfully refutes the proof, showing it is circular.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that the proof can be understood as an analogy, not a direct proof, thus avoiding circularity. The self-evidence of particular existence (as predicate) is used to illustrate the self-evidence of absolute existence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why must the ultimate evidence be a positive premise?
A) Because negative premises are always false.
B) Because there is no evidence from two negatives.
C) Because positive premises are more certain.
D) Because the subject must be positive.

Key Takeaway
The self-evidence of absolute existence is established by the necessity of a positive self-evident premise in any chain of evidence, even when conceding acquired knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you doubt the existence of Allah, do you realize that even your doubt presupposes some self-evident truth about reality? What is that truth, and how does this lesson force you to confront it?
?
Think of a deeply held belief you have—perhaps about purpose, morality, or the afterlife. Can you trace the chain of evidence you rely on? Where does that chain end? Is it self-evident to you? If not, what does that mean for your certainty?
?
You have now seen that every chain of knowledge must rest on something self-evident. Examine your own life: do you live as though the existence of absolute existence (Allah) is self-evident, or do you act as if it is uncertain? What would change if you fully embraced its self-evidence today?

The Necessity of a Positive Self-Evident Premise
Presents the argument for self-evidence of absolute existence via positive premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument for absolute existence must ultimately rely on a positive premise whose truth is self-evident.

Core Idea
The chain of proofs must end in a self-evident positive premise.

This lesson presents the core reasoning that if the concept of existence is acquired, it must be traceable back to a self-evident premise. The argument shows that a proof from two negative premises is impossible and that an infinite regress is untenable, necessitating a positive premise that is known intuitively. This positive premise inherently involves the concept of absolute existence, establishing its self-evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the core argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage from the beginning up to the phrase 'بطريق البداهة'. Try to follow the logical steps without getting into the commentary.
The argument is compact; pay attention to the conditionals and negations.
2
Identify the key components
Break down the argument: (1) the concession that existence might be acquired, (2) the need for a proof, (3) the rejection of two negative premises, (4) the requirement of a positive premise, (5) the impossibility of infinite regress, and (6) the conclusion that a self-evident positive premise confirms the intuition of absolute existence.
Each step builds on the previous one.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why the conclusion is that the concept of absolute existence is intuitive. How does the argument eliminate the possibility of it being purely acquired?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالبديهة بديهي قال : هاهنا (أو نقول بعد التنزل إلى كونه كسبياً لا بد من الانتهاء إلى دليل ( ولا دليل عن سالبتين فلا بد ) في الدليل ( من مقدمة موجبة قد حكم فيها بوجود المحمول (للموضوع ولا يمكن أن يكون العلم بوجود كل محمول للموضوع مستفاداً من دليل آخر بل لا بد من الانتهاء إلى دليل مشتمل على موجبه يكون العلم بوجود محمولها لموضوعها بديهيا ( وأنه يستدعي تصور الوجود المطلق) بطريق البداهة . قوله : ( بعد التنزل إلخ ) أشار بتقدير الظرف إلى أن قوله نقول : معطوف على نقول المقدر قبل قوله بل لا بد من الانتهاء إلى دليل وأن قوله ولا دليل معطوف على مقدر أعني لا بد من الانتهاء إلى دليل بقرينة السابق، وبهذا أظهر أنه لا يجوز أن يكون قوله أو نقول معطوفا على قوله أنه جزء وجودي ويكون استدلالاً برأسه ببداهة الوجود الرابطي في القضية الموجبة التي هي جزء الدليل على بداهة الوجود المطلق، كما أن قوله : إنه جزء وجودي استدلال ببداهة الوجود المحول عليه لأنه لا يكون للواو العاطفة وجه على أنه يكفي حينئذ أن يقال : أو نقول القضية الموجبة الضرورية متحققة فيكون العلم بوجود محمولها لموضوعها معلوماً بالضرورة، فيكون العلم بالوجود المطلق ضرورياً ولا حاجة إلى إثباتها بأنه لا دليل عن سالبتين . قوله : ( فاتجه الإشكال) عطف على قوله : قال هاهنا وأشار بترتبه على ذلك القول إلى أن ما قبله ليس منشأ للإشكال لأنه يمكن حمل الدليل فيه على الطريق الموصل كما في كلام الإمام إنما نشأ الإشكال من هذا القول وهو ظاهر. قوله : ( أو نقول إلخ) قيل : يحتمل أن يكون المعنى أو نقول بعد تسليم التسلسل اللازم من كون العلم بوجود كل دليل مستفاداً من دليل آخر يتم الدليل على بداهة تصور الوجود، فإنه لا دليل على سالبتين إلخ ويحتمل أن يكون تصويراً لحاصل الوجه الأول بطريق آخر، فإن حاصله أن بداهة تصور الوجود الخاص يستلزم بداهة تصور الوجود المطلق، فأشار أولاً إلى استلزام بداهة الوجود الخاص المحمول للمطلوب، وثانياً إلى استلزام بداهة الوجود الخاص الرابط وأنت خبير بأن الاحتمال الثاني إنما يستقيم إذا حمل كلام المصنف على ظاهره ، وأما إذا حمل على التنظير كما يدل عليه قول الشارح فلعله أراد إلخ، فلا . وأما الاحتمال الأول فبطلانه أظهر، إذ على تقدير تسليم التسلسل لا ينفع تحقق المقدمة الموجبة في استلزام بداهة الوجود، لأن وجود كل محمول لموضوع يجوز حينئذ أن يكون مستفاداً من دليل آخر، فلا يثبت بداهة وجود أصلاً فليتأمل .
By intuition it is intuitive. He said: Here (or we say after descending to the admission that it is acquired: There must be a proof, and there is no proof from two negative premises, so there must be in the proof a positive premise in which the existence of the predicate for the subject has been judged, and it cannot be that knowledge of the existence of every predicate for the subject is derived from another proof, but one must eventually arrive at a proof containing a positive premise whose knowledge of the existence of its predicate for its subject is self-evident, and that it requires the concept of absolute existence by way of intuition. His statement: (after descending, etc.) He indicated by the implied circumstance that his statement 'we say' is conjoined to the implied 'we say' before his statement 'but there must be an end to a proof', and that his statement 'and there is no proof' is conjoined to an implied [phrase] meaning 'there must be an end to a proof' by the contextual indication of what preceded. By this it becomes clear that it is not permissible for his statement 'or we say' to be conjoined to his statement 'it is an existential part' and be an independent argument for the intuition of the copulative existence in the positive proposition which is part of the proof for the intuition of absolute existence, just as his statement 'it is an existential part' is an argument for the intuition of existence predicated of it because there would be no justification for the conjunctive 'waw' and that then it would suffice to say: 'or we say the necessary positive proposition is realized, so knowledge of the existence of its predicate for its subject is known necessarily, thus knowledge of absolute existence is necessary, and there is no need to prove it by [the claim] that there is no proof from two negative premises'. His statement: (then the problem arises) is conjoined to his statement: 'he said here' and he indicated by its dependence on that statement that what precedes is not the source of the problem because it is possible to carry the proof there on the path leading to knowledge as in the words of Imam [al-Razi]; indeed the problem arises from this statement, and it is clear. His statement: (or we say, etc.) It is said: It is possible that the meaning is 'or we say after admitting the infinite regress necessary from knowledge of the existence of every proof being derived from another proof, the proof for the intuition of the concept of existence is completed, for there is no proof from two negative premises, etc.' And it is possible that it is a depiction of the content of the first way in another manner, because its content is that the intuition of the concept of the particular existence entails the intuition of the concept of absolute existence. Thus he first indicated the entailment of the intuition of the particular existence that is predicated of the required, and secondly the entailment of the intuition of the particular copulative existence. And you are aware that the second possibility is only valid if the author's statement is interpreted literally; but if it is interpreted as an analogy as indicated by the commentator's statement 'perhaps he meant etc.', then it is not. As for the first possibility, its falsity is more apparent, because assuming the admission of infinite regress, the realization of the positive premise does not help in entailing the intuition of existence, because the existence of every predicate for the subject might then be derived from another proof, so the intuition of [any] existence is not established at all. So let this be considered.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is a complex argumentative explanation. It begins with the author's statement that the concept of absolute existence is intuitive. The commentary then unpacks the structure of an argument: if we concede that the concept of existence is acquired, we must eventually reach a proof that contains a positive (mujiba) premise whose judgment is self-evident, because an infinite regress of proofs is impossible and a proof from two negative premises is invalid. The commentator clarifies that the two phrases 'or we say' and 'and there is no proof' are conjoined to an implied statement, and that this connection shows that the 'or we say' is not an independent argument for the intuition of copulative existence. He then discusses a possible objection: that the argument might be taken as an independent argument, but that would be inconsistent because the 'waw' conjunction would not fit. The exposition then considers an interpretation where the argument proceeds by admitting the infinite regress to show the self-evidence of the concept of existence. Two possibilities are examined: either the argument is about the necessity of a positive premise after conceding regress, or it is a restatement of the first argument linking particular existence to absolute existence. The commentator finds the second possibility only works under a literal reading, while the first possibility fails because the positive premise does not guarantee self-evidence if regress is allowed. The passage concludes with 'so let this be considered', inviting careful reflection.

The Commentator's Analysis and Two Interpretations
Analyzes the grammatical structure and explores two interpretations of the argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentator's analysis of the argument's grammatical structure and the two possible meanings of the phrase 'or we say' (أو نقول).

Core Idea
The argument's structure is clarified by analyzing its conjunctions and exploring two interpretations.

This lesson unpacks the commentator's detailed explanation of how the argument is structured grammatically and discusses two possible ways to understand the phrase 'or we say'. The commentator shows that one interpretation leads to an independent argument based on copulative existence, but he argues against it. The other interpretation is an alternative formulation of the original argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentator's analysis of conjunctions
Read the passage from the beginning of the commentary (قوله : ( بعد التنزل إلخ )) up to the discussion of the two possibilities. Focus on the grammatical connections the commentator is making.
The commentator is explaining how the phrases are conjoined to implied statements.
2
Identify the two interpretations
The text presents two possibilities for the meaning of 'or we say'. One involves admitting infinite regress, the other is a restatement of the first way. Identify both and note why the commentator prefers one over the other.
3
Understand the rejection of the first possibility
The commentator argues that the first possibility (admitting regress) fails because the positive premise does not guarantee self-evidence if regress is allowed. Understand this reasoning.
This is a key critical point.

By intuition it is intuitive. He said: Here (or we say after descending to the admission that it is acquired: There must be a proof, and there is no proof from two negative premises, so there must be in the proof a positive premise in which the existence of the predicate for the subject has been judged, and it cannot be that knowledge of the existence of every predicate for the subject is derived from another proof, but one must eventually arrive at a proof containing a positive premise whose knowledge of the existence of its predicate for its subject is self-evident, and that it requires the concept of absolute existence by way of intuition. His statement: (after descending, etc.) He indicated by the implied circumstance that his statement 'we say' is conjoined to the implied 'we say' before his statement 'but there must be an end to a proof', and that his statement 'and there is no proof' is conjoined to an implied [phrase] meaning 'there must be an end to a proof' by the contextual indication of what preceded. By this it becomes clear that it is not permissible for his statement 'or we say' to be conjoined to his statement 'it is an existential part' and be an independent argument for the intuition of the copulative existence in the positive proposition which is part of the proof for the intuition of absolute existence, just as his statement 'it is an existential part' is an argument for the intuition of existence predicated of it because there would be no justification for the conjunctive 'waw' and that then it would suffice to say: 'or we say the necessary positive proposition is realized, so knowledge of the existence of its predicate for its subject is known necessarily, thus knowledge of absolute existence is necessary, and there is no need to prove it by [the claim] that there is no proof from two negative premises'. His statement: (then the problem arises) is conjoined to his statement: 'he said here' and he indicated by its dependence on that statement that what precedes is not the source of the problem because it is possible to carry the proof there on the path leading to knowledge as in the words of Imam [al-Razi]; indeed the problem arises from this statement, and it is clear. His statement: (or we say, etc.) It is said: It is possible that the meaning is 'or we say after admitting the infinite regress necessary from knowledge of the existence of every proof being derived from another proof, the proof for the intuition of the concept of existence is completed, for there is no proof from two negative premises, etc.' And it is possible that it is a depiction of the content of the first way in another manner, because its content is that the intuition of the concept of the particular existence entails the intuition of the concept of absolute existence. Thus he first indicated the entailment of the intuition of the particular existence that is predicated of the required, and secondly the entailment of the intuition of the particular copulative existence. And you are aware that the second possibility is only valid if the author's statement is interpreted literally; but if it is interpreted as an analogy as indicated by the commentator's statement 'perhaps he meant etc.', then it is not. As for the first possibility, its falsity is more apparent, because assuming the admission of infinite regress, the realization of the positive premise does not help in entailing the intuition of existence, because the existence of every predicate for the subject might then be derived from another proof, so the intuition of [any] existence is not established at all. So let this be considered.

The Objection and Response on Conception
Presents the objection and the initial response distinguishing conception from assent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that 'conception' might mean absolute apprehension and the commentator's response that reaffirms the distinction between conception and assent.

Core Idea
Conception and assent are distinct; confusing them breaks the argument.

A clever objection tries to dissolve the problem by reinterpreting 'conception' as 'absolute apprehension'. The commentator counters by pointing out that the author himself contrasts 'conception of my existence' with 'assent' (I exist) in his own reply, proving the terms are not interchangeable. This lesson walks you through the objection and the decisive response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Identify the claim: if 'conception' means 'absolute apprehension', then the problem disappears. Notice how the objector tries to reinterpret the author's words.
The objector is trying to salvage the author's position by changing the meaning of a key term.
2
Examine the commentator's response
The commentator quotes the author's own words: 'We do not accept that the reality of my existence is conceived intuitively; indeed, 'I exist' is an intuitive assent.' This shows the author explicitly separates conception of existence from assent of existence.
The response hinges on the author's own usage, not external definitions.
3
Grasp the result
If we accept the objector's reinterpretation, the author's own response becomes incoherent. Thus the objection fails.
This reinforces the importance of textual consistency in theological arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ولا دليل عن (سالبتين ولو سلم فمورد السلب هو النسبة الإيجابية أي نسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع بوجوده له وبه يتم المقصود. قوله : (فاتجه الإشكال فإن قلت : يجوز أن يريد المصنف بالتصور الإدراك المطلق ويكون قوله، وجودي أخذاً بالحاصل من لي وجود فلا إشكال قلت : جوابه مانع من حمل كلامه على هذا حيث قال: وجوابه أنا لا نسلم أن وجودي حقيقته متصورة بالبديهة، نعم أنا موجود تصديق بديهي إلخ، فإنه جعل في هذا الكلام تصور وجودي مقابلا للتصديق الذي هو أنا ،موجود فلو حمل كلامه السابق على الاحتمال المذكور لاختل تقرير الجواب هذا، وقد أجاب بعض الأفاضل عن الإشكال المذكور بوجه آخر حيث قال : واعلم أن الشارح قد حمل كلام المصنف هذا على أنه مسوق لا كتساب تصور وجودي فحكم باتجاه الإشكال ولا يخفى أن مراد المصنف هاهنا تصوير طريق آخر لبداهة تصور الوجود وحاصله وإن سلمنا أن تصور الوجود كسبي لكن يجب انتهاؤه إلى كاسب وجود ضروري، فيثبت المطلوب ثم أراد أن ينتقل من طريق الموصل التصوري إلى الموصل التصديقي لا على معنى أنه يكتسب به تصور وجودي، بل من حيث أنه موصل دعوى ما فقال : أو نقول إلخ فالزم هاهنا أيضاً، وجوداً متصوراً بالبداهة فيثبت المطلوب بهذا الطريق أيضاً هذا كلامه وأنت خبير بأن سياق الكلام يأبى عن هذا التوجيه أما أولاً فلان الواو في قوله ولا دليل مانع عنه عند من له أدنى دربة في صناعة التركيب، إذ الوجه أن يقول : أونقول لا دليل عن سالبتين وأما ثانيا فلانه لو قصد ذلك لكفى أن يقول لا شك في وجود قضية بديهية موجبة، وأما ثالثاً فلأن هذا الوجه حينئذ دليل مستقل، فالوجه أن يعد دليلاً ثانياً، وتصير به الوجوه أربعة لا ثلاثة كما قرره المصنف . قوله: (فلعله أراد كما أنه إلخ) قيل : لا حاجة إليه فإنا لا نقول : لو كان كسبياً لكان اكتسابه بدليل ولا دليل عن سالبتين إلخ، بل نقول : لو كان كسبياً لكان العلم بكسبيته بدليل مركب من مقدمتين إحداهما لإيجابها تشتمل على العلم بوجود خاص بالبداهة وفيه بحث، إذ لا نسلم الملازمة حينئذ فإن كسبية شيء لا تستلزم كسبية العلم بكسبيته، بل الأقرب حينئذ بداهة هذا العلم وإن جاز كسبيته كما حققناه في مباحث العلم.
His statement: (and there is no proof from two negative premises), and if it is granted, then the locus of the negation is the positive relation, i.e., the relation of the predicate to the subject by its existence, and thereby the purpose is achieved. His statement: (then the problem arises). For if you say: It is permissible that the author intends by 'conception' absolute apprehension, and his statement 'my existence' is taken as the obtained from 'I have existence', then there is no problem. I say: His response prevents carrying his statement to that, where he said: 'His response: We do not accept that the reality of my existence is perceived intuitively; indeed, 'I exist' is an intuitive assent, etc.' For he made in this statement 'the conception of my existence' opposite to 'the assent' which is 'I exist'. So if his previous statement were interpreted according to the mentioned possibility, the formulation of this response would be corrupted. And some virtuous ones have answered the mentioned problem in another way, saying: Know that the commentator has interpreted this statement of the author as being presented not for acquiring the conception of existence, so he judged that the problem arises. And it is not hidden that the author's intention here is to depict another way for the intuition of the conception of existence, its content being: Even if we concede that the conception of existence is acquired, it must end at an acquirer of necessary existence, so the required is established. Then he wanted to move from the conceptual path to the assertoric path, not in the sense that it acquires a conception of existence thereby, but insofar as it leads to a claim. So he said: 'or we say, etc.' So it is necessary here also a concept of existence intuitively known, so the required is established by this way as well. This is his statement. And you are aware that the context of speech rejects this interpretation: First, because the 'waw' in his statement 'and there is no proof' prevents it for anyone with minimal training in syntactic structure, since the correct way would be to say 'or we say: There is no proof from two negative premises'. Second, because if he intended that, it would suffice for him to say 'There is no doubt about the existence of an intuitive positive proposition'. Third, because this way then becomes an independent proof, so it should be counted as a second proof, and thus the proofs become four, not three, as the author had set. His statement: (Perhaps he intended, just as, etc.) It is said: There is no need for it, for we do not say: If it were acquired, its acquisition would be by a proof, and there is no proof from two negative premises, etc. Rather we say: If it were acquired, then knowledge of its acquiredness would be by a proof composed of two premises, one of which, due to its positivity, includes knowledge of a particular existence by intuition, and there is a discussion there, for we do not concede the implication then; because the acquiredness of something does not entail the acquiredness of the knowledge of its acquiredness; rather the closer [view] then is the intuition of this knowledge, although its acquiredness is possible, as we have established in the discussions of knowledge.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is an objection and its response. The objection claims that the author's argument about the concept of existence might be interpreted as referring to absolute apprehension (idrak mutlaq), thus the problem disappears. The commentator rejects this by showing that the author himself distinguishes between conception (tasawwur) of existence and assent (tasdiq) in his own response, stating that 'my existence is intuitive assent', so reinterpreting 'conception' as 'assent' would disrupt the entire reply. An alternative answer from some scholars is then presented: the author's intent was merely to present another path (tariq) leading to the intuition of the concept of existence, not to derive it through a proof. However, the commentator refutes this alternative on three counts: the conjunction 'waw' in the original text would be inappropriate, the author could have simply said 'there is no doubt in an intuitive positive proposition', and counting this as an independent proof would increase the number of proofs from three to four, contradicting the author's own count. Finally, a further point is made that even if the concept were acquired, the knowledge of its acquiredness might still be intuitive, undermining the need for a proof from two premises.

Key Terms

إدراك مطلق
بديهة
تصديق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'conception' and 'assent' are interchangeable or that 'conception' always includes assent.
Correction: The passage shows that the author treats them as distinct categories: 'conception of existence' vs. the assent 'I exist'. They cannot be conflated without undermining the argument.

Checkpoint
What does the commentator use to prove that the objector's reinterpretation is invalid?
A) A definition from an earlier text
B) The author's own contrast between 'conception of existence' and 'assent'
C) A grammatical rule
D) An example from everyday language

Key Takeaway
Conception and assent are distinct categories in theological reasoning; confusing them can undermine an argument's validity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Where in your faith might you be confusing conception with assent, and what practical step will you take today to move from mere awareness to heartfelt conviction?
?
When you recite the Shahada, consider: are you just conceiving the words, or are you actively assenting with your whole being? What would it mean for you to ensure that your daily declarations of faith are acts of tasdiq, not just tasawwur?
?
In your own life, is there a truth you have 'accepted' but never really acted upon? That gap is the difference between conception and assent. Write down one specific truth you have conceived but not fully assented to, and commit to a concrete action that proves your assent by tonight.
