Refutation of an Alternative Interpretation
Presents an alternative interpretation by some scholars and a three-pronged refutation by the commentator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the alternative interpretation of the author's intent and the three textual and structural grounds on which the commentator refutes it.

Core Idea
Textual consistency, economy, and structure refute even plausible reinterpretations.

Some scholars proposed a different reading: the author's aim was not to acquire the conception of existence but to present a path leading to an intuitive concept. The commentator demolishes this reading with three precise objections: a grammatical error (wrong use of 'waw'), an unnecessary redundancy (could have stated a positive intuitive proposition), and a structural inconsistency (would increase the number of proofs from three to four). This lesson dissects each refutation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the alternative interpretation
The alternative says the author's intention was to show a path (tariq) to intuitive knowledge of existence, not to acquire the concept through proof. The 'wa' in 'wa la daleel' is seen as a connective.
This interpretation tries to save the author from the earlier objection.
2
First refutation: the conjunction 'waw'
The commentator notes that the correct phrasing would be 'aw' (or), not 'wa' (and). Anyone trained in Arabic composition would see this as an error if that meaning were intended.
This is a subtle grammatical point.
3
Second refutation: redundancy
If the author only wanted to assert that there is an intuitive positive proposition, he could have said directly, 'There is no doubt about the existence of an intuitive positive proposition.' The current formulation is unnecessarily complex.
Economy of expression is a principle in textual interpretation.
4
Third refutation: proof count
This interpretation would make the proposed point an independent proof, so the author's list of three proofs would actually be four, contradicting his own arrangement.
Structural consistency is a key tool in analysis.
5
Final point on knowledge of acquiredness
Even if the concept were acquired, knowledge of its acquiredness could still be intuitive, so the need for a proof from two premises is not necessary.
This is a deeper epistemological nuance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ولا دليل عن (سالبتين ولو سلم فمورد السلب هو النسبة الإيجابية أي نسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع بوجوده له وبه يتم المقصود. قوله : (فاتجه الإشكال فإن قلت : يجوز أن يريد المصنف بالتصور الإدراك المطلق ويكون قوله، وجودي أخذاً بالحاصل من لي وجود فلا إشكال قلت : جوابه مانع من حمل كلامه على هذا حيث قال: وجوابه أنا لا نسلم أن وجودي حقيقته متصورة بالبديهة، نعم أنا موجود تصديق بديهي إلخ، فإنه جعل في هذا الكلام تصور وجودي مقابلا للتصديق الذي هو أنا ،موجود فلو حمل كلامه السابق على الاحتمال المذكور لاختل تقرير الجواب هذا، وقد أجاب بعض الأفاضل عن الإشكال المذكور بوجه آخر حيث قال : واعلم أن الشارح قد حمل كلام المصنف هذا على أنه مسوق لا كتساب تصور وجودي فحكم باتجاه الإشكال ولا يخفى أن مراد المصنف هاهنا تصوير طريق آخر لبداهة تصور الوجود وحاصله وإن سلمنا أن تصور الوجود كسبي لكن يجب انتهاؤه إلى كاسب وجود ضروري، فيثبت المطلوب ثم أراد أن ينتقل من طريق الموصل التصوري إلى الموصل التصديقي لا على معنى أنه يكتسب به تصور وجودي، بل من حيث أنه موصل دعوى ما فقال : أو نقول إلخ فالزم هاهنا أيضاً، وجوداً متصوراً بالبداهة فيثبت المطلوب بهذا الطريق أيضاً هذا كلامه وأنت خبير بأن سياق الكلام يأبى عن هذا التوجيه أما أولاً فلان الواو في قوله ولا دليل مانع عنه عند من له أدنى دربة في صناعة التركيب، إذ الوجه أن يقول : أونقول لا دليل عن سالبتين وأما ثانيا فلانه لو قصد ذلك لكفى أن يقول لا شك في وجود قضية بديهية موجبة، وأما ثالثاً فلأن هذا الوجه حينئذ دليل مستقل، فالوجه أن يعد دليلاً ثانياً، وتصير به الوجوه أربعة لا ثلاثة كما قرره المصنف . قوله: (فلعله أراد كما أنه إلخ) قيل : لا حاجة إليه فإنا لا نقول : لو كان كسبياً لكان اكتسابه بدليل ولا دليل عن سالبتين إلخ، بل نقول : لو كان كسبياً لكان العلم بكسبيته بدليل مركب من مقدمتين إحداهما لإيجابها تشتمل على العلم بوجود خاص بالبداهة وفيه بحث، إذ لا نسلم الملازمة حينئذ فإن كسبية شيء لا تستلزم كسبية العلم بكسبيته، بل الأقرب حينئذ بداهة هذا العلم وإن جاز كسبيته كما حققناه في مباحث العلم.
His statement: (and there is no proof from two negative premises), and if it is granted, then the locus of the negation is the positive relation, i.e., the relation of the predicate to the subject by its existence, and thereby the purpose is achieved. His statement: (then the problem arises). For if you say: It is permissible that the author intends by 'conception' absolute apprehension, and his statement 'my existence' is taken as the obtained from 'I have existence', then there is no problem. I say: His response prevents carrying his statement to that, where he said: 'His response: We do not accept that the reality of my existence is perceived intuitively; indeed, 'I exist' is an intuitive assent, etc.' For he made in this statement 'the conception of my existence' opposite to 'the assent' which is 'I exist'. So if his previous statement were interpreted according to the mentioned possibility, the formulation of this response would be corrupted. And some virtuous ones have answered the mentioned problem in another way, saying: Know that the commentator has interpreted this statement of the author as being presented not for acquiring the conception of existence, so he judged that the problem arises. And it is not hidden that the author's intention here is to depict another way for the intuition of the conception of existence, its content being: Even if we concede that the conception of existence is acquired, it must end at an acquirer of necessary existence, so the required is established. Then he wanted to move from the conceptual path to the assertoric path, not in the sense that it acquires a conception of existence thereby, but insofar as it leads to a claim. So he said: 'or we say, etc.' So it is necessary here also a concept of existence intuitively known, so the required is established by this way as well. This is his statement. And you are aware that the context of speech rejects this interpretation: First, because the 'waw' in his statement 'and there is no proof' prevents it for anyone with minimal training in syntactic structure, since the correct way would be to say 'or we say: There is no proof from two negative premises'. Second, because if he intended that, it would suffice for him to say 'There is no doubt about the existence of an intuitive positive proposition'. Third, because this way then becomes an independent proof, so it should be counted as a second proof, and thus the proofs become four, not three, as the author had set. His statement: (Perhaps he intended, just as, etc.) It is said: There is no need for it, for we do not say: If it were acquired, its acquisition would be by a proof, and there is no proof from two negative premises, etc. Rather we say: If it were acquired, then knowledge of its acquiredness would be by a proof composed of two premises, one of which, due to its positivity, includes knowledge of a particular existence by intuition, and there is a discussion there, for we do not concede the implication then; because the acquiredness of something does not entail the acquiredness of the knowledge of its acquiredness; rather the closer [view] then is the intuition of this knowledge, although its acquiredness is possible, as we have established in the discussions of knowledge.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is an objection and its response. The objection claims that the author's argument about the concept of existence might be interpreted as referring to absolute apprehension (idrak mutlaq), thus the problem disappears. The commentator rejects this by showing that the author himself distinguishes between conception (tasawwur) of existence and assent (tasdiq) in his own response, stating that 'my existence is intuitive assent', so reinterpreting 'conception' as 'assent' would disrupt the entire reply. An alternative answer from some scholars is then presented: the author's intent was merely to present another path (tariq) leading to the intuition of the concept of existence, not to derive it through a proof. However, the commentator refutes this alternative on three counts: the conjunction 'waw' in the original text would be inappropriate, the author could have simply said 'there is no doubt in an intuitive positive proposition', and counting this as an independent proof would increase the number of proofs from three to four, contradicting the author's own count. Finally, a further point is made that even if the concept were acquired, the knowledge of its acquiredness might still be intuitive, undermining the need for a proof from two premises.

بديهة
قضية بديهية
سالبتين

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That any plausible reinterpretation of a text is acceptable as long as it solves a problem.
Correction: The commentator shows that reinterpretations must be constrained by grammar, economy, and the author's own structural intentions, as demonstrated by the three refutations.

Checkpoint
What is the third refutation against the alternative interpretation?
A) It uses the wrong conjunction
B) It makes the text redundant
C) It turns the argument into an independent proof, making the total proofs four instead of three
D) It contradicts a previous statement by the author

Key Takeaway
Textual consistency, economy of expression, and structural alignment are key principles in evaluating interpretations of theological arguments.

Refuting the Claim that Belief Requires No Concept
Al-Jurjani refutes the claim that belief in one's existence requires no concept.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why every belief (tasdiq) necessarily involves conceiving its predicate, and the difference between causal and logical implication.

Core Idea
Belief in a proposition always entails conceiving its predicate.

Al-Jurjani carefully dissects an objection that claims belief in one's existence requires no concept of existence at all. He shows that such a view misunderstands how belief and conception relate: every belief in a proposition necessarily involves conceiving the predicate. This lesson unpacks his refutation and clarifies the logical terminology he employs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its English translation. Notice the dense technical terms.
The passage is a refutation of an objection, so track the argument structure.
2
Identify the key terms
Look for tasdiq (belief), tasawwur (conception), mahmul (predicate), and istid'a' (requirement). The commentary explains them.
These terms are central to the argument.
3
Understand the distinction of 'requirement'
Al-Jurjani clarifies that 'requirement' here is logical implication (mulzim/lazim), not causal dependency. Compare with the example of 'Zayd is standing'.
This distinction is crucial to rebut the objection.
4
See how the objection is rebutted
The objector claimed no concept is needed; Al-Jurjani shows that belief does require conception of the predicate, but only in some aspect, not in essence.
The objector's alternative phrase is deemed irrelevant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: ( نعم أنا موجود إلخ) تصديق لما بعده أورده سنداً للمنع، كأنه قيل: لا نسلم أن تصوره بالكنه بديهي فإن البديهي الذي لا شبهة لنا في حصوله هو التصديق، بأنا موجود وهو يستدعي تصور وجودي بالكنه بل بالوجه والمراد بالاستدعاء استدعاء الملزوم اللازم، فإن التصديق بكل قضية يستتبع تصور المحمول المضاف إلى الموضوع، مثلاً التصديق بأن زيداً قائم يستلزم تصور القيام المضاف إلى زيد وبما حررنا اندفع ما قيل : إن التصديق المذكور لا يستدعي تصور وجودي أصلاً، لا بالوجه ولا بالكنه، إذ لا مدخل له في ذلك التصديق إنما يستدعي تصور الوجود المطلق، فالواجب أن يقول : لا يستدعي تصور الوجود المطلق بالكنه، بل بالاعتبار إذ ليس المراد من الاستدعاء استدعاء الموقوف للموقوف عليه، بل استدعاء الملزوم للازم، وأما نفي استدعائه لتصور الوجود المطلق بالكنه فلا مدخل له في الجواب عن الاستدلال المذكور كما لا يخفى فيكون ذكره لغواً.
His saying: (Yes, I exist, etc.) is a confirmation of what follows, adduced as a support for the denial, as if it were said: We do not concede that his conception in its essence is intuitive, for the intuitive which we have no doubt in attaining is the belief that I exist, and it requires conceiving my existence not in essence but only in aspect. And by 'require' is meant the requirement of the consequent by the antecedent. For every belief in a proposition entails conceiving the predicate attributed to the subject. For example, the belief that Zayd is standing entails conceiving 'standing' attributed to Zayd. And by what we have set forth, what was said is rebutted: that the aforementioned belief does not require any concept of my existence at all, neither in aspect nor in essence, for it has no part in that belief; it only requires the concept of absolute existence. Therefore he ought to say: it does not require the concept of absolute existence in essence, but in aspect, because the intended meaning of 'require' is not the requirement of the effect for its cause, but the requirement of the antecedent for the consequent. As for denying that it requires the concept of absolute existence in essence, that has no bearing on answering the aforementioned argument, as is evident, so mentioning it is superfluous.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is a detailed refutation of an objection. The objector claimed that the belief 'I exist' does not require any concept of existence at all, not even in aspect. The answer explains that every belief (tasdiq) in a proposition necessarily involves conceiving the predicate (mahmul) in relation to the subject. So the belief 'I exist' entails conceiving 'existence' (the predicate) relative to 'I' (the subject). However, this does not require conceiving the essence of existence; it only requires conceiving it in some aspect. The answer further clarifies the meaning of 'requires' (istid'a'): it is not that the effect requires its cause, but rather that the antecedent (the belief) necessitates the consequent (the conception). Therefore, the objection that denied any requirement is invalid, and the objector's proposal to say 'it does not require the concept of absolute existence in essence' is irrelevant because the argument only requires a concept in some aspect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Belief in one's existence does not require any concept of existence at all.
Correction: Every belief necessarily involves conceiving the predicate (here, existence) in some aspect.

Checkpoint
What does the author mean by 'requirement' (istid'a') in this context?
A) Causal dependency.
B) Logical implication of the consequent by the antecedent.
C) Temporal priority.
D) Contingency.

Key Takeaway
Belief in a proposition always entails conceiving its predicate, but this conception is not necessarily of the essence; it can be only in some aspect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you affirm 'I exist,' do you ever pause to consider what you actually mean by 'existence'? This lesson shows you must have some conception of it—but is that conception truly clear, or is it vague and assumed? Are you living with assumptions you have never examined?

Or, like the belief in existence, do you only have an aspectual grasp? How does this humility before knowledge change the certainty with which you hold that belief?
?
The passage distinguishes between requiring a concept in essence vs. in aspect. In your journey to know Allah, do you often mistake a partial, aspectual understanding for complete, essential knowledge? Where in your faith are you claiming to know the 'essence' when you only know the 'aspect'? What would change if you admitted that?

The Sufficiency of a Certain Aspect in Conceiving Accidents
Al-Jurjani answers the objection that the predicate 'existence' as absolute accident must be fully conceived.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Jurjani's answer to the objection that the predicate 'existence' as an accident must be fully conceived, and why a partial conception is sufficient for intuitive belief.

Core Idea
A partial conception of an accident suffices for intuitive belief.

In this passage, Al-Jurjani addresses a subtle objection: if the predicate 'existence' in the statement 'I exist' is an absolute accident, then to have an intuitive belief in my existence, I must intuitively conceive that absolute accident. Similarly, when I say 'my existence,' I refer to a particular instance of that accident with attribution, so that instance must be conceived. Al-Jurjani responds that a conception in some aspect is sufficient.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector claims the predicate is the absolute accident, not a particular, and that 'my existence' requires conceiving a particular instance.
2
Identify the consequence
If the accident must be fully conceived, then intuitive belief seems impossible without full conception.
3
Grasp the answer
Al-Jurjani says conceiving the accident in some aspect (bi-wajh ma) is enough.
4
Reflect on the principle
This shows that for belief, a partial notion of the predicate suffices.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : المحمول في قولك أنا موجود هو ذلك العارض مطلقاً لا خصوصية فرد منه، وأيضاً إذا قلت : وجودي فقد عبرت عن فرد بذلك العارض الإضافة فلا بد أن يكون متصوراً قلت : يكفينا تصور ذلك العارض بوجه ما
If you say: The predicate in your statement 'I exist' is that accident absolutely, not a particular instance of it. And also, when you say 'my existence,' you have expressed an individual by that accident with attribution, so it must be conceived. I say: Sufficient for us is the conception of that accident in some aspect.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer. The objector raises two points: First, the predicate 'existence' in 'I exist' is the accident itself in its absolute sense, not a particular instance, so to have an intuitive belief, the absolute accident must be intuitively conceived. Second, when one says 'my existence,' one expresses an individual instance of that accident with the addition of attribution, so that instance must be conceived. The answer is that we only need to conceive the accident (existence) in some aspect (bi-wajh ma), not in its essence. This is consistent with the earlier point that a certain aspect suffices for the belief.

العارض
الخصوصية
الإضافة
العرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if the predicate is an accident, it must be conceived in its essence.
Correction: The answer clarifies that a partial conception is sufficient.

Checkpoint
What is the author's response to the claim that 'my existence' requires conceiving a particular instance of the accident?
A) He denies that 'my existence' is said.
B) He says we only need a certain aspect.
C) He says the accident is not absolute.
D) He says conception is impossible.

Key Takeaway
Al-Jurjani teaches that for intuitive belief, a partial conception of the accident (in some aspect) is sufficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Think of one area in your life where you demand complete clarity before you act or believe. How does this lesson challenge that demand? Can you take a step today to trust a partial understanding and move forward?

Vague Conception of Existence Suffices
Al-Ījī presents an objection and answer on conceiving existence as an accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ījī defends the view that existence can be conceived vaguely without requiring a full essential concept.

Core Idea
Vague conception suffices for predication.

This passage tackles a subtle objection: if existence is an accident, then when we say 'I am existent', we must have a full conception of that accident. Al-Ījī responds by distinguishing between conceptual and real parts, arguing that a vague awareness (bi-wajh ma) is enough. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read its literal translation. Notice the structure: an objection (فإن قلت...) followed by an answer (قلت...).
The objector raises two points; the answer addresses both.
2
Identify the two objections
The objector first argues that in 'I am existent', the predicate is existence as an accident absolutely, so it must be conceived. Second, when you say 'my existence', you refer to an individual accident, so again it must be conceived.
Both objections rely on the assumption that conception must be full and essential.
3
Find the two parts of the answer
First, al-Ījī says a vague conception (تصور بوجه ما) suffices. Second, he explains that the concept of existence being part of the concept of 'my existence' does not imply the reality of existence is part of the reality of 'my existence'.
The key insight: conceptual inclusion does not entail real inclusion.
4
Reflect on the distinction
Think about why this distinction matters. It shows that we can know something through a concept without knowing its essence fully. This is a common move in kalam to defend the possibility of knowing accidental attributes.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to see if you grasped the main point. Then try the activity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : المحمول في قولك أنا موجود هو ذلك العارض مطلقاً لا خصوصية فرد منه، وأيضاً إذا قلت : وجودي فقد عبرت عن فرد بذلك العارض الإضافة فلا بد أن يكون متصوراً قلت : يكفينا تصور ذلك العارض بوجه ما، وليس يلزم من كون مفهوم الوجود جزءاً من مفهوم وجودي أن يكون حقيقة الوجود جزءاً من حقيقة وجودي لجواز أن يكون هذان المفهومان عارضين لحقيقتهما
For if you say: The predicate in your saying 'I am existent' is that accident absolutely, not a particular individual of it. Also, when you say 'my existence', you have expressed an individual by that accidental relation, so it must be conceived. I say: It suffices for us to conceive that accident in some way (bi-wajh ma), and it does not follow from the fact that the concept of existence is part of the concept of 'my existence' that the reality of existence is part of the reality of 'my existence', because it is possible that these two concepts are accidents to their realities.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objector argues that if existence is an accident, then in the proposition 'I am existent', the predicate is that accident in an absolute sense, so it must be fully conceived. Also, when one says 'my existence', this refers to an individual with that accident, so the accident must be conceived. The author answers that a vague conception (tasawwur bi-wajh ma) of the accident is sufficient; it does not require full essential conception. He further explains that the concept of existence being part of the concept of 'my existence' does not imply that the reality of existence is part of the reality of 'my existence', because both concepts could be accidents to their respective realities. This answer upholds the position that existence is not necessarily self-evidently conceived in essence.

Key Terms

عارض
وجودي
تصور بوجه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If the concept of existence is part of the concept of 'my existence', then the reality of existence must also be part of the reality of 'my existence'.
Correction: Al-Ījī clarifies that conceptual inclusion does not necessitate real inclusion; two concepts can be accidental to their respective realities.

Checkpoint
What is the key point of the author's answer in this passage?
A) The accident must be conceived fully and essentially
B) A vague conception (bi-wajh ma) of the accident suffices
C) The concept of existence is identical to the reality of existence
D) The predicate 'existent' refers to a particular individual

Key Takeaway
A vague conceptual awareness of existence suffices for predication; full essential knowledge is not required.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever feel that you must fully understand a concept before you can act on it? How does al-Ījī's allowance of vague conception challenge your tendency to demand perfect clarity before taking action in your faith?
?
Think of a belief you hold about Allah (like His existence) that you cannot fully articulate. Does the fact that you only have a vague conception of it make you doubt its reality? Why or why not, in light of this lesson?
?
When you say 'I am a believer,' is the reality of your faith fully captured by the concept you have of it? How does the distinction between concept and reality in this lesson help you avoid reducing your faith to a mere mental formulation?

The Necessity of a Self-Evident Premise Without Necessity of Its Existence
The author denies the necessity of existence of the self-evident premise, while affirming its self-evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that while every proof must have a self-evident premise, that premise need not have actual existence in the external world; rather, its truth matters.

Core Idea
A proof needs truth, not existence.

The author now addresses a key nuance in the logic of proof: must every argument end with a premise that is self-evident and also necessarily existing? He clarifies that the requirement is self-evidence conceptually, but not existence externally. This passage shows that proof operates on the level of truth, not ontological reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it, then read the translation. Notice the flow: concession, denial, then clarification.
The passage uses a technical style; take your time.
2
Identify the two distinctions
Find where the author distinguishes between self-evident (daruri) and existence (wujud). The key is that he denies the necessity of existence but affirms the necessity of self-evidence.
The phrase 'نعم لا بد من دليل هو ضروري وأما وجوده فلا' is central.
3
Understand the reasoning
Notice the argument: we infer from the truth of premises, not from their existence. The author gives an example of non-existential proofs. Ponder how truth can be independent of existence.
4
Examine the analogy with definition
The author parallels proof with definition (ma'rifa): just as we use the conception of parts to know the essence without their existence, so too we use truth of premises without their existence. This solidifies the point.
The analogy helps to generalise the principle.
5
Reflect on implications
Consider what this means for theological arguments: proofs can involve non-existent entities (like mental concepts) as long as the premises are true. This broadens the scope of rational proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في التنزل أولا لا بد من الانتهاء إلى دليل وجوده ضروري قلنا ممنوع، نعم لا بد من دليل هو ضروري وأما وجوده فلا إذ قد لا يكون له وجود فإنا نستدل بصدق المقدمتين في نفس الأمر على صدق المدلول فيهما لا بوجودهما في الخارج على وجود المدلول فيه فإن الدليل والمدلول قد يكونان معاً عدميين والحاصل أنا كما نتوصل بصدق مقدمتي الدليل لا بالعلم بوجودهما إلى المدلول، كذلك نتوصل بتصور أجزاء المعرف لا بالعلم بوجودها إلى معرفة الماهية
As for the first concession, it is necessary to end at a proof whose existence is necessary. We say: That is denied. Yes, there must be a proof that is necessary (i.e., self-evident), but as for its existence, it may not be. For we infer from the truth of the two premises in themselves (fi nafs al-amr) to the truth of the conclusion in them, not from their existence in the external world to the existence of the conclusion in it. For the proof and the proved may both be non-existential (adamayni). In sum, just as we arrive at the conclusion through the truth of the premises of the proof, not through knowledge of their existence, so too we arrive at knowledge of the quiddity (mahiyya) through conceiving the parts of the definiens, not through knowledge of their existence.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage addresses a concession: some might say that every argument must end with a self-evident premise whose existence is necessary. The author denies this. He agrees that the premise must be self-evident (daruri), but not that it must exist in the external world. He explains that we infer based on the truth (sidq) of the premises in themselves, not on their external existence. For example, the proof and the conclusion might both be non-existent. The analogy: just as we use truth of premises to reach the conclusion, we use conception of the parts of a definition to know the essence, without needing their external existence. This clarifies that the requirement for a proof is its conceptual truth, not its ontological existence.

Key Terms
دليل
ضروري
صدق
مقدمتين
نفس الأمر
عدم
معرف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A proof's premise must have actual existence in the external world.
Correction: The author clarifies that the premise must be self-evident (true conceptually), but it may not exist ontologically. Inference depends on truth, not existence.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the basis for inferring a conclusion from a proof?
A) The external existence of the premises
B) The truth of the premises in themselves (fi nafs al-amr)
C) The self-evidence of the premises' existence
D) The existence of the conclusion in the external world

Key Takeaway
The validity of a proof depends on the truth of its premises, not on their external existence.

?

Did you feel the need for that truth to 'exist' in a way that made it easier to act upon? What would change in your daily worries if you fully embodied the lesson's principle: the truth is enough, existence follows?

The Objection on Proof's Mental Existence and Its Answer
Objector claims proof must be known and exist in mind; commentator responds

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that a proof must be known in the mind and the commentator's response that only mental existence, not knowledge of that existence, is required.

Core Idea
A proof only needs mental existence, not knowledge of that existence.

This lesson presents a theological objection and its resolution. The objector claims that any proof must be both known and exist in the mind, otherwise circularity or infinite regress occurs. The commentator responds by distinguishing between existence in the mind and knowledge of that existence, showing that the objector's conclusion does not follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the objector's claim: proof must be known and exist in the mind, and must be self-evident or end in self-evidence to avoid circularity or regress.
The objector thinks this supports their position.
2
Identify the commentator's distinction
The commentator says: even if we grant mental existence, what is necessary is existence in the mind, not knowledge of that existence. Understand the difference.
This is the key move in the argument.
3
Connect to the broader debate
Ask yourself: why does this matter? The objector wanted to show that the concept of existence is self-evident. The commentator blocks that conclusion by showing that only existence, not knowledge of existence, is required for a proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المعرف، فلا يتم استدلالكم فإن قيل : المعرف أو الدليل سواء كان وجودياً أو عدمياً لا بد أن يعلم ويوجد في الذهن، ويكون بديهياً أو منتهياً إليه دفعاً للدور أو التسلسل وبذلك يتم مقصودنا قلنا إن سلم الوجود الذهني كان اللازم وجوده في الذهن لا العلم بوجوده فيه
The definition, then your inference is not complete. For if it is said: the definition or the proof, whether existential or non-existential, must be known and exist in the mind, and be self-evident or ending to it to prevent circularity or infinite regress, and by that our purpose is accomplished. We said: If mental existence is granted, what is necessary is its existence in the mind, not knowledge of its existence in it.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objector argues that in any proof or definition, the items must be known and exist in the mind, either self-evidently or by ending at a self-evident premise, to avoid circularity (دور) or infinite regress (تسلسل). This would make the concept of existence self-evident. The commentator replies: even if we grant that the proof must exist in the mind (وجود ذهني), the necessary requirement is only its existence in the mind, not the knowledge of its existence. In other words, it is enough that the concept is present in the mind; we do not need to have separate knowledge that it exists there. Thus, the conclusion that the concept of existence is self-evident does not follow from the objection's premises.

Correction: The commentator clarifies that only mental existence is required, not knowledge of that existence.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The commentator defends that mental existence suffices for a proof; explicit knowledge of that existence is not required.

How does this lesson free you from the burden of having to be consciously aware of every step in your reasoning? Correct Definition of Affirmative Proposition
Defines 'affirmative' as identity in truth, not existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct definition of an affirmative proposition in Islamic logic and why it focuses on truth-value rather than existence.

Core Idea
Affirmation is about identity in truth, not about existence.

In this passage, the commentator corrects a common misunderstanding about affirmative propositions. He argues that an affirmative proposition is not about the existence of the predicate for the subject, but about the truth of the predicate being applicable to the subject. This distinction is crucial for understanding logical judgments, especially in theological discourse.

Guided Path
1
Read the objector's definition
The objector initially defines affirmative as a judgment that the predicate exists for the subject. Notice how the commentator immediately denies this.
2
Examine the corrected definition
The commentator provides a new definition: the affirmative is that in which it is judged that what is true of the subject is true of the predicate. Understand this logical approach.
This is a shift from ontology to logic.
3
Consider the examples
The examples 'Sharik al-Bari is impossible' and 'Zayd is blind' show that truth does not require existence. Analyze how these support the corrected definition.
The first example has both terms non-existent, the second has a predicate that does not exist as a property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( في التنزل ثانياً ( الموجبة ما حكم فيه بوجود المحمول للموضوع ممنوع (بل الموجبة ما حكموا فيه بأن ما صدق عليه الموضوع صدق عليه المحمول، وقد لا يوجدان ( نحو قولك : شريك الباري ممتنع وقد لا يوجد المحمول مع صدقه على الموضوع في الخارج، كقولك : زيد أعمى فصدق المحمول على الموضوع وهو المعتبر في الإيجاب أعم من وجوده له الوجه ( الثاني ) من الوجوه الدالة على بداهة تصور الوجود هو أن يقال : ( قولنا الشيء إما موجود أو معدوم)
His saying: ('In the second descent, the affirmative is that in which the existence of the predicate for the subject is judged' is denied. Rather, the affirmative is that in which it is judged that what is true of the subject is true of the predicate, and they may both not exist, like your saying: 'The partner of God is impossible', and the predicate may not exist while being true of the subject in the external, like your saying: 'Zayd is blind'. So the truth of the predicate of the subject, which is what matters in affirmation, is more general than its existence for it. The second of the aspects indicating the self-evidence of the conceptualization of existence is to say: 'Our statement: Everything is either existent or non-existent'.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage contains two distinct points. First, it corrects the definition of an affirmative proposition (موجبة). The objector defined it as a judgment that the predicate exists for the subject. The commentator denies this and defines it instead as a judgment that what the subject is true of is also true of the predicate. This is a logical definition focusing on identity in truth (صدق), not on existence. He gives examples: 'The partner of God is impossible' — here the subject and predicate are both non-existent, yet the proposition is true. Similarly, 'Zayd is blind' — the predicate 'blind' (أعمى) is true of Zayd even though blindness does not exist as a separate entity. Thus, affirmation is about truth-value assignment, not about ontological existence. Second, the commentator presents the second proof for the self-evidence of the concept of existence: the disjunction 'everything is either existent or non-existent' is self-evident and thus the concept of existence is self-evident because it is a necessary component of this disjunction.

Key Terms

الصدق
المحمول

شريك الباري
أعمى
الإيجاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An affirmative proposition requires the predicate to exist for the subject.
Correction: The commentator shows that an affirmative proposition only requires truth of the predicate about the subject, regardless of existence.

Checkpoint
According to the corrected definition, what constitutes an affirmative proposition?
A) The predicate exists for the subject
B) What is true of the subject is true of the predicate
C) Both subject and predicate exist
D) The predicate is self-evident

Key Takeaway
An affirmative proposition is a judgment that what is true of the subject is true of the predicate, not a judgment of existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you affirm that 'Allah is One', do you rely on His existence alone, or on the truth that what is true of Him (oneness) is true of Him regardless of existence? How does this shift your certainty?

Second Proof: Self-Evidence of Existence
Presents second proof for self-evidence of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof that the concept of existence is self-evident, based on the disjunction 'everything is either existent or non-existent'.

Core Idea
The disjunction of existence and non-existence makes the concept of existence self-evident.

The commentator presents a second argument for the self-evidence of the concept of existence. He claims that the proposition 'everything is either existent or non-existent' is self-evident, and since it necessarily includes the concept of existence, that concept must also be self-evident.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof
The commentator says: 'The second of the aspects indicating the self-evidence of the conceptualization of existence is to say: Our statement: Everything is either existent or non-existent.' Read this carefully.
2
Understand the logical structure
The proof argues that the disjunction 'everything is either existent or non-existent' is self-evident. Since it uses the concept of existence, that concept must also be self-evident. Reflect on why this is considered a proof.
Self-evidence means the concept is understood without needing definition.
3
Connect to the overall context
This is the second of several proofs for the self-evidence of existence. Compare it to other proofs if you have encountered them.

Clarifying Mental Existence in the Second Proof
Responds to objection regarding self-evident premise; distinguishes mental from external existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection regarding the need for a self-evident premise is addressed by distinguishing between mental and external existence.

Core Idea
Mental existence makes the concept of existence self-evident without needing external proof.

This passage responds to a refined objection to the second proof. The objector argues that every proof must end at a self-evident premise. The commentator clarifies that the relevant existence is mental, not external, and shows how this answers the objection. We will follow the exchange and see how the concept of absolute existence becomes self-evident.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Notice the interplay between objection and response.
2
Identify the objection and initial clarification
The objector says the proof must end at a self-evident premise. The commentator responds by specifying that the required existence is mental (وجود ذهني), not external.
This shift from external to mental existence is crucial.
3
Understand the implication for absolute existence
If mental existence is self-evident, then absolute existence, being a part of that concept, is also self-evident. This completes the purpose of the proof.
Absolute existence (الوجود المطلق) is the subject under investigation.
4
Examine the second objection and its response
A further objection claims that what is necessary is knowledge, not mental existence. The commentator responds that even if it is knowledge, it is a relation, and if granted as mental existence, it is existence in the mind, not knowledge of that existence.
This reinforces that the concept's presence in the mind is enough.
5
Reflect on the resolution
Consider how the argument satisfies the requirement for a self-evident premise. The objector's challenge is answered without needing to posit an external existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فإن قيل تحرير للدليل المذكور بقوله فلا بد من الانتهاء إلى دليل يلزم من وجوده وجوده بحيث يندفع الجواب المذكور أي المراد من الوجود الذهني لا الخارجي، وحينئذ لا شك في لزوم كون وجوده أي تصوره بديهيا.
قوله : ( وبذلك يتم مقصودنا ) لأنه إذا كان وجوده الذهني بديهياً فيكون الوجود المطلق الذي هو جزؤه أيضاً بديهياً.
قوله : (إن سلم الوجود إلخ) أي اللازم هو العلم ولا نسلم كونه وجوداً ذهنياً، بل هو تعلق بين العالم والمعلوم، وإن سلم فاللازم من كونه معلوماً أن يكون موجوداً في الذهن لا العلم بوجوده فيه حتى يلزم كون العلم بالوجود المطلق بديهياً.
His saying: ('For if it is said: a formulation of the mentioned proof by his saying: there must be an ending to a proof whose existence necessitates its existence, such that the mentioned answer is repelled.') i.e., what is meant by mental existence, not external. And then there is no doubt about the necessity of its existence i.e., its conceptualization being self-evident.

His saying: ('And by that our purpose is accomplished') because if its mental existence is self-evident, then absolute existence, which is part of it, is also self-evident.

His saying: ('If existence is granted, etc.') i.e., what is necessary is knowledge, and we do not grant that it is mental existence; rather it is a relation between the knower and the known. And if granted, then what is necessary from being known is that it exists in the mind, not knowledge of its existence in it, so that it would necessitate knowledge of absolute existence being self-evident.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage elaborates on the second proof introduced previously. An objector refines the argument: the proof must end at a self-evident premise (i.e., the concept of existence is self-evident because its mental existence is undeniable). The commentator responds that what is meant by 'existence' here is mental existence (وجود ذهني), not external existence. If we grant that the concept of existence has mental existence, then it is self-evident. Then he says that if its mental existence is self-evident, then absolute existence, being a part of it, is also self-evident. He then addresses a further nuance: someone might say that what is necessary is knowledge, not mental existence. The commentator replies that even if we call it 'knowledge', it is a relation between the knower and the known, and if we grant that it is mental existence, what follows is that it exists in the mind, not that we have knowledge of its existence. So the point is that the concept's presence in the mind is enough; we don't need an additional judgment that it is present.

Key Terms

البداهة
التصور
العلم
التعلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that mental existence implies explicit awareness of that mental existence.
Correction: Mental existence simply means the concept is present in the mind, not that we are aware of its presence. The text clarifies that what is necessary is existence in the mind, not knowledge of that existence.

Checkpoint

A) Its external existence
B) Its mental existence
C) Knowledge of its mental existence
D) Its being a part of the disjunction

Key Takeaway
The second proof is completed by showing that the concept of existence has mental existence, which is self-evident, so absolute existence is self-evident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Self-Evidence of the Judgment of Existence and Non-Existence
Definition of the self-evident judgment and defense against circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the judgment distinguishing existence and non-existence is self-evident and how it avoids circularity.

Core Idea
The judgment of existence vs. non-existence is self-evident, not circular.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif establishes a foundational point: the distinction between existence and non-existence is immediately recognized without proof. However, an objection arises claiming this leads to circularity. The author defends the self-evidence by explaining how knowledge of the whole's self-evidence does not require detailed knowledge of each part.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
Read the passage and note the claim that the judgment distinguishing existence and non-existence is self-evident (badihi). Also note that this judgment depends on conceptions, which are therefore also self-evident.
The claim is the starting point of the discussion.
2
Understand the objection
The objector argues that claiming the judgment is absolutely self-evident with all its parts leads to circularity (musadara). Identify the reasoning: because existence is one of its parts, judging the whole depends on judging that part.
Circularity would undermine the claim.
3
Follow the answer
The author replies that the self-evidence of the whole in reality depends on its parts, but our knowledge of the whole's self-evidence does not require detailed knowledge of each part. It can be known summarily, as in the example of idiots and children.
This is the key logical distinction.
4
See the analogy
The author draws an analogy with the universal major premise in logic: knowledge of the universal does not depend on knowledge of the conclusion about a specific individual. The judgment about Zayd as a human is different from judgment about Zayd in his particularity.
This illustrates how general knowledge can be prior to specific knowledge.
5
Conclude with final clarification
The passage ends by addressing another aspect: it suffices to conceive the existent and non-existent in some manner (bi-wajh ma), not necessarily in essence (bi-l-kunh). The dispute is only about conception in essence.
This resolves a second possible objection.

المرصد الأول - المقصد الأول : في تعريف الوجود والعدم. تصديق بديهي وأنه يتوقف على تصور الموجود والمعدوم فيكون تصور الموجود والمعدوم بل الوجود والعدم بديهيا. وكذلك يتوقف هذا التصديق على تصور تغايرهما الذي هو الأثنينية أو مستلزم لتصورهما المسبوق بتصور الوحدة، فتكون تصورات هذه الأمور أيضاً بديهية. فإن قيل : إن زعمت أنه أي هذا التصديق بديهي مطلقاً أي بجميع أجزائه فمصادرة لأن الوجود من جملة أجزائه، فالحكم بأن ذلك الجميع بديهي موقوف على الحكم بأن الوجود بديهي، فقد توقف مقدمة الدليل على ثبوت المدعى. أو زعمت أن الحكم في هذا التصديق بعد تصور الطرفين بديهي غير محتاج إلى استدلال لم ينفع لجواز أن يكون تصور طرفيه معاً، أو تصور أحدهما الذي هو الوجود مثلاً كسبياً مع كون الحكم في نفسه بديهياً. قلنا : هذا التصديق بديهي مطلقاً ولا مصادرة لأن بداهته مطلقاً في نفس الأمر تتوقف على بداهة أجزائه في نفس الأمر لكن لا يتوقف العلم ببداهته مطلقاً على العلم ببداهة أجزائه أي العلم ببداهة كل واحد منها مفصلاً بل يستتبعه مثلا إذا علم أن هذا التصديق حاصل لمن لا يتصور منه كسب كالبله والصبيان علم إجمالاً أن كل واحد من أجزائه بديهي، فإذا أريد أن يعلم حال الوجود بخصوصه قيل : الوجود جزء من أجزاء هذا التصديق وكل جزء من أجزائه بديهي فظهر أن العلم بالكلية القائلة بأن كل جزء من أجزائه بديهي، لا يتوقف على العلم ببداهة جزء معين منه بخصوصه حتى يلزم المصادرة، وهذا بعينه ما قيل: من أن العلم بكلية كبرى الأول لا يتوقف على العلم بالنتيجة، فإن الحكم على زيد من حيث أنه فرد من أفراد الإنسان إجمالاً غير الحكم عليه باعتبار خصوصيته، فإن الحكم يختلف باختلاف العنوان فالأحكام الجارية على خصوصيات أفراد موضوع الكلية مندرجة فيها بالقوة، فيستدل بالكلية عليها حتى يخرج من القوة إلى الفعل. نعم إذا كان العلم بالكلية مستفاداً من العلم بحال كل فرد بخصوصه لم يمكن الاستدلال بها على حكم الأفراد كما إذا علم أن الوجود والعدم والشيء الذي ردد بينهما كلها بديهية، وعلم بذلك أن هذا التصديق بديهي مطلقاً لم يصح الاستدلال ببداهته على بداهة شيء منها، لأنه دور. وجوابه أي جواب الوجه الثاني أنه يكفي تصورهما أي تصور الموجود والمعدوم بوجه ما والنزاع إنما وقع في التصور بالكنه.
The first observation - first intention: On the definition of existence and non-existence. A self-evident judgment, and it depends on the conception of the existent and the non-existent, so the conception of the existent and the non-existent, rather existence and non-existence, becomes self-evident. Likewise, this judgment depends on the conception of their distinction, which is duality, or something that necessitates their conception preceded by the conception of unity, so the conceptions of these things are also self-evident. If it is said: If you claim that it, i.e. this judgment, is absolutely self-evident, i.e. with all its parts, then it is begging the question because existence is among its parts, so the judgment that the whole is self-evident depends on the judgment that existence is self-evident, so the premise of the proof depends on the establishment of the claim. Or you claim that the judgment in this judgment, after the conception of the two sides, is self-evident, not needing inference, it does not avail, because it is possible that the conception of its two sides together, or the conception of one of them, e.g. existence, is acquired while the judgment itself is self-evident. We say: This judgment is absolutely self-evident, and there is no begging of the question, because its self-evidence absolutely in reality depends on the self-evidence of its parts in reality, but knowledge of its self-evidence absolutely does not depend on knowledge of the self-evidence of its parts, i.e. detailed knowledge of the self-evidence of each one, rather it implies it, e.g. when it is known that this judgment occurs for one who does not acquire from it any acquisition, like the idiots and children, they know in summary that each one of its parts is self-evident. So if one wants to know the state of existence specifically, it is said: Existence is a part of the parts of this judgment, and every part of its parts is self-evident. So it appears that knowledge of the universal proposition that every part of its parts is self-evident does not depend on knowledge of the self-evidence of a specific part of it in particular such that begging the question would occur. This is exactly what was said: that knowledge of the universal major premise of the first figure does not depend on knowledge of the conclusion. For the judgment about Zayd insofar as he is an individual of the human beings in summary is not the judgment about him considering his particularity, for the judgment differs with difference of consideration. So the judgments running on the particularities of the individuals of the subject of the universal are contained in it potentially, so the universal is used to infer them until they go from potentiality to actuality. Yes, if knowledge of the universal is gained from knowledge of the state of each individual in particular, it is not possible to use it to infer the judgments of the individuals, as when it is known that existence, non-existence, and the thing which is distinguished between them are all self-evident, and it is known by that that this judgment is absolutely self-evident, it is not valid to infer from its self-evidence the self-evidence of any of them, because it is circular. And its answer, i.e. the answer to the second aspect, is that their conception suffices, i.e. the conception of the existent and the non-existent in some manner, and the dispute is only about conception in essence.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof regarding the self-evident judgment of the distinction between existence and non-existence. It begins by stating that the judgment that distinguishes between the existent and the non-existent is self-evident (badihi), and that it depends on the conception of the existent and the non-existent, so those conceptions are also self-evident. It further states that this judgment also depends on the conception of their distinction (taghayyur), which is duality (ithnayniyya), or something necessitating that conception, which is preceded by the conception of unity (wahda). Therefore, these conceptions are also self-evident. The text then presents an objection: if one claims that this judgment is absolutely self-evident with all its parts, it would be circular (musadara), because existence is one of its parts, so judging the whole as self-evident depends on judging existence as self-evident, which is the claim itself. Alternatively, if one claims that the judgment itself after conceiving the two sides is self-evident and needs no proof, that does not help either, because it is possible that the conception of the two sides or one of them (e.g. existence) is acquired (kasbi) while the judgment itself is self-evident. The answer (qulna) clarifies: this judgment is absolutely self-evident without circularity because its self-evidence in reality depends on the self-evidence of its parts in reality, but knowledge of its self-evidence absolutely does not depend on detailed knowledge of the self-evidence of each part. Rather, it implies it. For example, when it is known that this judgment occurs for someone who does not engage in acquisition, like idiots (bulh) and children (sibyan), they know in summary (ijmalan) that each part is self-evident. If one wants to know the state of existence specifically, one can say: existence is a part of this judgment, and every part is self-evident, so the universal proposition that every part is self-evident does not depend on knowing the self-evidence of a particular part. This is like the universal major premise in the first figure of logic, where knowledge of the universal does not depend on knowledge of the conclusion. The judgment differs according to the consideration (al-hukm yukhtalifu bi-ikhtilaf al-'unwan). General judgments about individuals are contained in the universal potentially, and the universal is used to infer them. However, if the universal knowledge is derived from knowledge of each individual in detail, then it would be circular to use that universal to infer the individuals. The passage concludes with an answer to a second objection: that it is sufficient to conceive the existent and non-existent in some manner (bi-wajh ma), and the dispute is only about conception in essence (bi-l-kunh).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that claiming the judgment of existence and non-existence is self-evident leads to circularity because existence is part of the judgment.
Correction: Circularity is avoided because knowledge of the whole's self-evidence does not require detailed knowledge of each part's self-evidence; it is known summarily, as seen in idiots and children.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the self-evidence of the judgment of existence and non-existence not involve circularity (musadara)?
A) Because the judgment is acquired through inference.
B) Because knowledge of the whole's self-evidence does not require detailed knowledge of each part's self-evidence.
C) Because existence and non-existence are not parts of the judgment.
D) Because the objection only applies to acquired judgments.

Key Takeaway
The self-evident judgment of existence and non-existence does not involve circularity because it is known summarily, not through detailed knowledge of its parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have read that the difference between existence and non-existence is self-evident—known even to children and the simple-minded. Yet, do you live as if you truly grasp this distinction? When you choose to ignore your Creator, are you not acting as if non-existence were as real as existence? How will you let this self-evident truth shake you out of negligence starting today?
?
The argument shows that you don't need to know every detail for something to be certain. What basic truth about yourself—such as your own existence or your need for God—have you been pretending requires more proof before you act? What step can you take right now to submit to that truth fully?
?
If the judgment of existence is self-evident, then denying it or ignoring it is a form of self-deception. Are there areas in your life where you are acting as if non-existence (death, annihilation) has the final word? Face that fear now: what would it mean for you to truly embrace the reality of your existence as a gift from the Eternal?

The Self-Evidence of Existence and the Answer to Circularity
The author proves existence is self-evident and answers the circularity objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Sanusi establishes that existence is self-evident and why the objection of circularity does not apply.

Core Idea
Existence is self-evident without circularity because the universal premise is known independently.

In this passage, the author tackles a classic objection: if proving existence relies on a syllogism, doesn't that create circularity? He demonstrates that the universal premise is known in a general way, not derived from the specific instance, so there is no circularity. The argument also clarifies how judgments vary by description and introduces the concept of mental entailment to resolve a subtle point about conception.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overanalyzing, then read the English translation carefully. Let the flow of the argument settle in your mind.
The passage is dense with logical terminology; do not worry about every detail yet.
2
Identify the main argument and the objection
The passage has two parts: first, a proof that existence is self-evident using the universal major premise; second, an answer to the objection that this reasoning is circular. Locate where the objection begins (the word 'دور') and how the author responds.
The response involves a distinction between conception by some way and conception by essence.
3
Analyze the key logical moves
Focus on the analogy with the universal major premise of the first syllogistic figure, the idea that judgments about individuals are implicit in the universal potentially, and the clarification that mental entailment suffices to avoid the problem of circularity.
The commentator's explanations in the passage provide helpful cues.

The First Observatory - First Objective: On the Definition of Existence and Non-Existence. AV. And children know in summary that every one of its parts is self-evident. So if one wants to know the state of existence in particular, it is said: existence is a part of the parts of this affirmation, and every part of its parts is self-evident. Thus it appears that knowledge of the universal proposition that every part of its parts is self-evident does not depend on knowledge of the self-evidence of a specific part of it in particular, such that circularity would result. And this is exactly what was said: that knowledge of the universal major premise of the first figure does not depend on knowledge of the conclusion. For the judgment about Zayd insofar as he is an individual of mankind in summary is not the judgment about him in consideration of his particularity, for judgment varies according to the description. So the judgments applying to the particular individuals of the subject of the universal are implicit in it in potentiality, and one deduces from the universal to them until they go from potentiality to actuality. However, if knowledge of the universal is derived from knowledge of the state of every individual in particular, then it is not possible to deduce from it to the judgment of the individuals, just as if one knows that existence, non-existence, and the thing which is ambiguous between them are all self-evident, and knows thereby that this affirmation is absolutely self-evident, it is not correct to deduce from its self-evidence to the self-evidence of any of them, because that would be circular. (And its answer) i.e. the answer to the second aspect: (that it suffices to conceive them both) i.e. conception of the existent and the non-existent in some way, and the dispute only occurred in conception by the essence. The aspect. He said: (if it is known etc.) is an explanation of non-dependence when the knowledge of the self-evidence of the affirmation is derived from evidence of its occurrence to the simple-minded and children without knowledge of the state of the parts in detail. He said: (then if one wants etc.) is an explanation of its entailment of knowledge of the self-evidence of the parts in detail when the self-evidence of existence in particular is known from it. He said: (by the universal major premise of the first) i.e. by the universal major premise, since the production of the first figure does not depend on knowledge of its universality but on knowledge of the universal major premise. He said: (differs according to the description) in knowledge and ignorance, self-evidence and acquisition. He said: (implicit in it in potentiality) i.e. in the state of those judgments being in potentiality, not that their being implicit is in potentiality, for implicitness is actual because the description is considered in terms of its truth on the individuals of the subject; but they are in potentiality because their actual attainment is after joining the minor premise to it. No. He said: (only occurred in conception by the essence) it is not hidden that if the dispute is about conception by the essence meaning something that entails the conception of them both, because that which entails the conception of duality is the conception of distinction, not itself; and if it is an attribute of conception, his saying 'it is duality' is correct, unless it is interpreted as deletion of the genitive i.e. conception of duality. I said: it is permissible that it is an attribute of distinction, since what is meant is not external entailment but mental entailment, i.e. entailment according to conception, so there is no problem. He said: (it is not correct to deduce from its self-evidence etc.) It is said: it is permissible that knowledge of the universal is derived from knowledge of the state of every individual in particular, then the judgments of individuals are forgotten while the universal judgment remains, so deduction is correct in this.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This is an argumentative passage that proves existence is self-evident and defends against the charge of circularity. The author begins by noting that children have a summary knowledge that every part of a self-evident proposition is self-evident. When we want to know the state of existence in particular, we can say 'existence is a part of this affirmation and every part is self-evident,' so the universal that every part is self-evident does not depend on knowledge of any specific part's self-evidence, thus no circularity. This is analogous to the universal major premise in the first figure of syllogism, which is known independently of the conclusion. The author then explains that the judgment about an individual like Zayd differs when considered universally versus particularly; the universal judgment includes particular judgments implicitly (in potentia). However, if the universal itself were derived from knowledge of each particular, then using it to deduce the particulars would be circular. The text then gives the answer to the second objection: it suffices to conceive of existence and non-existence in some way, since the dispute is about conception by essence. The commentator clarifies each phrase: the knowledge of the affirmation's self-evidence does not require detailed knowledge of parts, as shown by children; the universal major premise is known as a universal, not as a particular; judgments vary by description (knowingly, ignorantly, self-evidently, or acquired); particular judgments are implicit in the universal potentially, not actually. The commentator also addresses a subtle point about entailment: the objection that conceiving two things entails distinction is answered by saying the entailment is mental, not external. Finally, a possible escape from circularity is considered: if one derives the universal from individuals but later forgets the individuals, deduction might still be valid, but this is rejected because the universal is not self-evident in reality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is circular because it uses the self-evidence of the parts to prove the self-evidence of existence, which is itself a part.
Correction: The universal premise that every part is self-evident is known independently of the self-evidence of any specific part, so there is no circularity.

Checkpoint
How does the argument avoid circularity when proving that existence is self-evident?
A) By assuming existence is self-evident without proof.
B) By using a universal major premise that is known independently of the particular instance.
C) By appealing to the authority of children and the simple-minded.
D) By denying that circularity is a problem.

Key Takeaway
Existence is self-evident; the universal premise that every part of the affirmation is self-evident is known independently of the specific instance, so there is no circularity.

?
If existence itself is self-evident, then your existence is not an accident or illusion. How does that reality challenge the way you live your daily routine? Will you start treating every waking moment as meaningful?
?
The argument avoids circularity by grounding knowledge in a universal principle. Are there areas in your faith or life where you rely on circular reasoning to avoid facing a hard truth about your own character? What would it look like to let go of that circle and accept accountability?

The Third Proof: Existence Cannot Be Defined
Al-Ījī presents the third proof that existence cannot be defined by limit or description.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why existence cannot be defined by limit or description, and how this demonstrates that existence is a self-evident, primary concept.

Core Idea
Existence is too simple to be defined; any attempt leads to absurdity.

The third proof tackles existence's conceivability from the opposite direction: instead of arguing directly that it is self-evident, it shows that any attempt to acquire its concept through definition necessarily fails. This reinforces that existence is a primary, self-evident notion that cannot be acquired by reasoning from prior concepts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the argument is structured as a conditional and a refutation.
2
Identify the opponent and the claim
The proof only applies to those who admit existence is conceivable but claim it is acquired. Understand why this restriction matters.
It does not apply to those who deny existence is conceivable at all.
3
Examine the two cases of definition
The argument considers definition by limit (حد) and then by description (رسم). For limit, it splits into whether existence is simple or composite. Follow each subcase and the absurdities that arise.
Simple: no parts, so limit impossible. Composite: parts either all existences (part=whole) or none (no existence).
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Since both ways of defining existence fail, the only alternative is that existence is self-evident and not acquired. Ask yourself why this conclusion is necessary.
This argument is a proof by elimination of possibilities.

The First Observatory - First Objective: On the Definition of Existence and Non-Existence. (Third) And it only constitutes a proof against those who admit that existence is conceived by the essence, and claim it is acquired: (that if existence were acquired, then it is either by limit or by description) because the means of acquiring conception are limited to these two. (And both divisions are invalid. As for defining it by limit, it is because the limit, as has passed, is only by parts, and existence is simple, so it cannot have a limit. And his saying 'or not' is raised, since there is no such saying 'otherwise there would be no existence there'. And you may interpret the first as universal affirmation and the second as universal negation; and the existence of the third, i.e. that some of its parts are existences and some are not existences, does not harm because it is invalid by what invalidated the first division. He said: (so the part would be equal to the whole) because when it is assumed that they are existences, they agree in existence and differ according to particularities, i.e. differentiae and individuations, so the part would be equal to its whole in specific or generic essence. And equality of the part, insofar as it is a part, to its whole in specific or generic essence is invalid because it entails the inclusion of the whole in the part, so the part would not be a part nor the whole a whole. We only said 'insofar as it is a part' because a simple body like water, its part is equal to its whole in specific essence, but not insofar as it is a part but insofar as it is an individual of it, for part of water is water. And from this it is known that the specification by part of the form also is without circularity and is nothing, because knowledge of the universal, if it is not self-evident in itself but derived from judgments of each individual, and the opponent disputes it, we are forced to prove it by the judgments of individuals, even if the opponent's assistance is assumed. So for it to be knowledge, it must involve observation of the premises of its proof, even summarily. Thus if one deduces from it to judgments of its individuals, it would be circular. He said: (so the part would be equal to the whole) i.e. the part of the intelligible essence would be equal to its whole, and that
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof that existence is self-evident by showing that it cannot be acquired through definition, and is a classification-and-refutation argument. The author states that the proof only applies to those who admit existence is conceivable by essence and claim it is acquired. If existence were acquired, it would have to be by limit (definition by parts) or description. Both are shown invalid: (1) Definition by limit requires parts, but existence is simple (both mentally and externally), so it has no limit. (2) If existence were composite, its parts would either all be existences or not. If all parts are existences, each part would equal the whole in essence (since they share existence), which is absurd because the part would include the whole. If no part is an existence, then the whole is not existence either, a contradiction. The possibility that some parts are existences and some are not is rejected by the same argument as the first case. The commentator further explains that the 'simple' means both mentally and externally, and the proof would negate composition absolutely. The 'most complete description' combines limit and description and thus entails both impossibilities. The commentator also addresses a subtle point: specifying a part of the form (the differentia) does not lead to circularity if the universal is derived from individuals, but if the universal itself is not self-evident, any deduction from it to individuals would be circular. Thus, existence cannot be acquired by any means, so it must be self-evident.

الرسم
بسيط
مركب
جزء
كل
الماهية
الذاتيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof shows that existence is self-evident to everyone, including those who deny it is conceivable.
Correction: The text explicitly states the proof only works against those who admit existence is conceivable but claim it is acquired. For someone who denies existence is conceivable at all, the impossibility of definition does not prove self-evidence, because it might simply be inconceivable.

Checkpoint
Why can existence not be defined by limit (حد)?
A) Because existence is composite and has many parts.
B) Because existence is simple and has no parts, while limit requires parts.
C) Because existence is self-evident and does not need definition.
D) Because limit is only for physical objects.

Key Takeaway
Existence is a simple, primary concept that cannot be defined by limit or description; its self-evidence is established by the impossibility of acquiring it through definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If existence is self-evident and cannot be defined, what does that say about your own certainty that you exist? Have you ever truly doubted your own existence, and if not, why do you still live as if your existence is fragile?
?
The proof shows that existence is not acquired through any definition—it is given directly. How does this change the way you approach the question 'Why do I exist?' Are you content to accept it as a gift, or do you insist on defining it yourself?
?
Al-Ījī's argument only works for those who admit existence is conceivable. Do you actually live as though existence is conceivable and real, or do your actions betray a hidden doubt? What would it cost you to face that doubt honestly?

Objection and Answer on Mental Distinction
Objection that mental distinction still causes the same problem and its answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that mental distinction causes the same problem is answered by confining the tripartite division to mental existence.

Core Idea
Mental distinction avoids contradiction because mental existence is knowledge, not real precedence.

This passage addresses a potential objection to the claim that genus and species are distinct only in the mind. The objector argues that even mental distinction leads to the same problem of parts existing before the whole. The answer clarifies that the division applies only to mental existence, where parts are simply knowledge and priority is harmless.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the objection and answer. Then read the translation to understand the flow.
2
Identify the objection
The objector argues that even if distinction is mental, the same tripartite division (simultaneous, before, after) applies, causing a contradiction.
The objection is in the sentence beginning with 'فإن قلت'.
3
Understand the answer's key move
The answer restricts the division to mental existence only. Since mental existence is knowledge, precedence of part's knowledge over whole's knowledge is not contradictory.
The answer begins with 'قلت: لا محذور'.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why the problem only arises in external existence: in the mind, parts are known before the whole, which is sensible.
The author ends with 'فتدبر' meaning 'so reflect'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بل التمايز في الذهن، فإن قلت : التمايز الذهني كف في الاستدلال إذ نقول : كل الأجزاء المتمايزة في الذهن إما أن يتصف بوجود مع أو بعد إلخ . قلت : لا محذور حينئذ في الشق الثالث إذ الترديد حينئذ في الوجود الذهني للأجزاء المتمايزة في الذهن لا في الوجود الخارجي لها لعدم التمايز في الخارج حتى يصح الترديد بين الأقسام الثلاثة، فلتكن تلك الأجزاء متصفة بالوجود في الذهن قبل وجود الوجود الذي هو الكل المركب فيه، فإن وجود الجزء في الذهن عبارة عن العلم به، ووجود الكل أيضاً عبارة عن العلم بالكل، وقد يتحقق الأول قبل الثاني بلا محذور، إذ لا محذور في تقديم نفس الوجود الذهني على وجوده فتدبر.
Rather the distinction is in the mind. For if you say: The mental distinction suffices in the inference, for we say: All the parts distinguished in the mind are either described with existence simultaneously or after, etc. I say: There is no harm then in the third branch, because the division then is in the mental existence of the parts distinguished in the mind, not in their external existence due to lack of distinction in the external, so that the division between the three categories is valid. So let those parts be described with existence in the mind before the existence of the existence which is the whole composed therein, for the existence of the part in the mind is knowledge of it, and the existence of the whole is also knowledge of the whole, and the first may be realized before the second without harm, for there is no harm in the precedence of the mental existence itself over its existence. So reflect.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the claim that the distinction between genus and species is only mental. The objector argues that even if the distinction is mental, the same problem arises: the parts of existence in the mind must be either simultaneous with, prior to, or posterior to the whole. In the third case, the part's existence would be before the whole's existence, which seems to imply the part exists before itself. The answer clarifies that the division applies only to mental existence. The parts are distinguished in the mind, not externally, so there is no external precedence. In the mind, the part's existence is knowledge of it; the whole's existence is knowledge of the whole. It is possible for the knowledge of the part to occur before knowledge of the whole without contradiction. The objection is thus resolved.

Correction: The problem only arises in external existence; in the mind, parts are just knowledge, so priority in knowledge is not problematic.

Checkpoint
