B) Because the existence of the part in the mind is knowledge of it, and knowledge can precede knowledge of the whole.
C) Because mental existence does not involve time.
D) Because the whole is not composed of parts in the mind.

Key Takeaway
The mental distinction avoids the contradiction because mental existence is knowledge, not real temporal priority.

In your journey of faith, do you seek to know individual attributes before comprehending the whole of Allah's perfection? How can you ensure that this order leads to deeper awe rather than fragmented understanding?

The Option That Parts of Existence Are Non-Existents
Proposal and defense of parts of existence as non-existents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how describing parts of existence as non-existents avoids contradiction by distinguishing two types of predication.

Core Idea
Parts can be non-existent without making existence impossible.

This lesson presents a creative solution to the puzzle of how existence can be composed of parts like genus and species. Instead of viewing parts as distinct only in the mind, one may describe them as non-existent in a specific sense. The passage then defends this view against the charge of contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to understand the flow of the argument.
Notice the example of ten and the discussion of mental parts.
2
Identify the two types of predication
Look for the terms 'مواطأة' (unanimity) and 'اشتقاق' (derivation). Understand how these two different relations allow the same part to be described as both existence and non-existence without contradiction.
3
Understand the resolution
Trace how the objection (contradiction) is answered by this distinction. Ask yourself: How does this preserve the truth of statements like 'the rational is animal' while maintaining that parts are non-existent?
This connects directly to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو نختار أنه أي جزء الوجود يتصف بالمعدوم أي بمفهوم المعدوم بل بالعدم ولا يكون الوجود حينئذ محض العدمات حتى يكون محالاً بل محض معدومات، فلا يلزم إلا كون الوجود مركباً من أجزاء متصفة بنقيضه وكذا كل مركب من أجزاء متمايزة الوجود في الخارج، فإنه مركب من أجزاء متصفة بنقيضه فالعشرة مثلاً محض أمور لا شيء منها بعشرة أعني الوحدات التي تركب منها العشرة وكذا الحال في الأجزاء الذهنية فإن الحيوان نفسه ليس عين الإنسان في الحقيقة وإن كانا متصادقين وليس يلزم من ذلك كون أحد في ضمنه ومتأخر عنه عند تحليله. قوله: بل بالعدم إن قلت الأجزاء الذهنية يتصف أحدها بالآخر، وبالكل أيضاً فإنه يصدق أن الناطق حيوان وأنه إنسان فلو اتصف أجزاء الوجود بالعدم، ولا شك أنها أجزاء ذهنية اتصف أيضاً بالوجود الذي هو الكل لما قلنا : فيلزم اتصافها بالوجود والعدم معاً، وإنه اجتماع النقيضين قلت بعد تسليم أن الاختيار ليس مبنياً على التنزل وتسليم التمايز الخارجي بين الجنس والفصل المانع عن التصادق اتصاف الأجزاء الذهنية بالكل بمعنى حمله عليها مواطأة واتصافها بالعدم هاهنا بمعنى قيامه بها وحمله عليها اشتقاقا فاللازم أن تصدق على تلك الأجزاء أنها معدومة وأنها وجود ولا محذور فيه بل المحذور أن يصدق عليها أنها موجودة وأنها معدومة.
Or we choose that any part of existence is described as non-existent (المعدوم), that is, by the concept of the non-existent, rather by non-existence (العدم), and existence is not then mere nothings (العد م ات) so as to be impossible, but mere non-things (المعدومات); so no necessitation except that existence is composed of parts described by its opposite. And likewise every composite of parts distinct in external existence, for it is composed of parts described by its opposite. For example, ten is mere things none of which is ten, meaning the units which compose ten. And similarly in the case of mental parts, for animal itself is not identical to human in reality even if they are co-extensive, and no necessitation arises from that that one is within the other and posterior to it upon analysis. He said: Rather by non-existence. If you say: The mental parts are predicated of one another, and also of the whole, for it is true that the rational is animal and that it is human. So if parts of existence are described by non-existence, and there is no doubt that they are mental parts, they would also be described by the existence which is the whole, as we said: so there follows their being described by existence and non-existence together, which is a contradiction. I say: After conceding that the choice is not based on concession, and conceding that the external distinction between genus and species prevents co-extensive predication, the mental parts are described by the whole in the sense of predicating it of them by unanimity (م و اط أ ة), and their being described by non-existence here is in the sense of its inhering in them and predicating it of them by derivation (اش ت ق ا ق ا). So it is necessary that those parts are truly said to be non-existent and to be existence, and there is no harm in that; rather the harm would be if they were truly said to be existent and to be non-existent simultaneously.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage presents an alternative solution to the problem of composing existence from genus and species. Instead of claiming the parts are distinct only in the mind, one may choose that the parts of existence are described as non-existent (معدوم) rather than non-being (عدم). This avoids the impossibility of composition from nothings, because it is composition from non-things, not nothings. The example of ten illustrates this: ten is composed of units, none of which is itself ten, yet ten exists. Similarly, in mental parts, animal is not identical to human even if they are co-extensive. The objection then arises: if the parts are non-existent, how can they be predicated of the whole? For example, we say 'the rational is animal,' so animal (a part) is predicated of the whole. If the part is non-existent, the whole would be both existent and non-existent. The answer distinguishes two types of predication: by way of unanimity (مواطأة) when the whole is predicated of the parts, and by way of derivation (اشتقاق) when non-existence is predicated. Since the parts are non-existent in the sense of having non-existence inhering in them, and the whole is predicated of them by unanimity, there is no contradiction. It is permissible to say the parts are both non-existent (by derivation) and existence (by unanimity), but not existent and non-existent simultaneously.

Key Terms
المعدوم
العدم
الوجود
مواطأة
اشتقاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Calling parts of existence non-existent makes existence itself non-existent or impossible.
Correction: The parts are described as non-existent in the sense of derivation (اشتقاق) meaning non-existence inheres in them, while the whole is predicated of them by unanimity (مواطأة), so they are still existence in that sense. There is no contradiction.

Checkpoint
How does the answer avoid the contradiction when parts of existence are described as non-existent?
A) By denying that the parts are mental.
B) By distinguishing between predication of the whole by unanimity and predication of non-existence by derivation.
C) By claiming that non-existence is not a real attribute.
D) By asserting that existence has no parts.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between predication by unanimity (مواطأة) and by derivation (اشتقاق) allows the same part to be described as both non-existent and existence without contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what ways do you experience a contradiction between how you see yourself and how you are seen by others? Can the distinction between two types of predication help you hold both descriptions without hypocrisy? Reflect on one specific instance and write down how you might reconcile it.
?
The passage argues that parts can be described as 'non-existent' yet still contribute to existence. Consider an aspect of your character or faith that feels 'non-existent' or weak. How can you affirm that this part still exists in some sense, and what does that mean for your spiritual growth? End with a concrete step you will take this week.

The problem of self-evident knowledge and circularity in existence being better known
Commentary examines the claim that existence is better known and the charge of circular reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the charge of circular reasoning against the claim that existence is the most known concept, and how the commentator resolves it through distinctions about self-evidence and the Ash'ari epistemology.

Core Idea
Claiming existence is most known leads to circularity if it relies on self-evidence.

This lesson unpacks a subtle objection in Islamic epistemology: if existence is said to be the most known concept, does that not already assume it is self-evident? The commentator navigates this charge by clarifying what 'better known' means and by defending the Ash'ari view of knowledge. We will follow the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the circularity charge
Read the opening lines where the commentator states that the claim is a petitio principii. Note how the premise depends on the conclusion.
2
Understand the dependence
The commentator explains that 'being better known' requires 'being self-evident'. This is the key move.
3
Follow the rebuttals
The commentator addresses potential objections, including the attribute example and the possibility of knowing essences through properties.
These rebuttals defend the original claim.
4
Contrast Ash'ari and philosopher views
The passage ends with the Ash'ari position that knowledge after investigation is directly created by Allah, not through emanation.
This resolves the epistemological framework.

( لا أعرف من الوجود مصادرة فإن من يسلم كونه بديهيا ) ويدعي أنه كسبي كيف يسلم أنه لا أعرف منه ) بل يقول : كونه أعرف يتوقف على كونه بديهياً، فتتوقف مقدمة الدليل على ثبوت المدعى وما ذكرتم من الاستقراء ليس بصحيح عندنا ( قوله ) في الاستدلال ثانياً على كون الوجود أعرف لا قوله : ( فإن صفة الجزء ليست إلخ) أي لا يلزم أن يكون جزءاً للمركب أي من حيث إنها صفة له وقائمة به، ليست جزءاً للمركب، فلا يرد أن الهيئة السريرية صفة للخشب مع أنها جزء للسرير. قوله : ( لجواز أن يكون إلخ ) بأن يكون له نسبة مخصوصة بسببها يحصل في الذهن كنه الشيء، فإن الذهن قد ينتقل من الضد إلى الضد ومجرد الاستبعاد لا ينفع. قوله : (بل يقول إلخ) أضرب عما قاله المصنف : وضم إليه مقدمة إشارة إلى أن ما ذكره المصنف غير كاف في إثبات لزوم المصادرة . قوله : ( يتوقف على كونه (بديهياً لأن المراد بالأعرفية الأقدمية في التصور، فلو لم يكن بديهياً كان معرفته أقدم منه في التصور، وتوهم البعض أن الأعرفية بمعنى الأظهرية في الانكشاف فمنع توقفه على البداهة فوقع فيما وقع. قوله: (وما ذكرتم إلخ) دفع لما يرد أن قوله كيف يسلم إلخ منع لمقدمة مدللة، وذا لا يجوز بأن منعها راجع إلى منع دليله . قوله : ( لجواز أن يكون من الخواص إلخ) وذلك لأن المعرفات والحجج معدات لفيضان المطلوب من المبدأ الفياض فيجوز أن يستعد الذهن القوي لفيضان كنه الحقيقة منه بمجرد تصور الخواص فلا يرد أنه كيف يمكن كون الخواص كاشفة لكنه الحقيقة مع أنه لا مناسبة عقلية بينهما، تؤدي إلى الكشف على أن هذا التقرير، إنما يحتاج إليه على مذهب الفلاسفة، وأما عندنا فالعلم بعد النظر الصحيح بمحض خلق الله تعالى بلا أعداد وتوليد، بل بطريق جري العادة كما مر فالأمر أظهر. قوله : (بل يقول كونه أعرف يتوقف على كونه بديهياً توضيح لمراد المصنف فإن لزوم المصادرة لا يظهر من عبارته ظهوراً تاماً بخلاف عبارة الشارح، لكن فيه بحث وهو أن الأعرفية في نفس الأمر تتوقف على نفس البداهة، ونفس البداهة لا يتوقف على الأعرفية بل مستتبعة إياها وإنما للوقوف عليها هو العلم بالبداهة لا يقال : العلم بالبداهة يتوقف على العلم بالأعرفية اللازم
('There is no better known than existence' is a petitio principii, because whoever concedes that it is self-evident and claims that it is acquired, how can he concede that there is nothing better known than it?) Rather, he says: its being better known depends on its being self-evident; thus the premise of the proof depends on the establishment of the claim, and what you mentioned of induction is not correct according to us. (His saying) in the second inference that existence is better known? No. His saying: (For the attribute of the part is not etc.) i.e., it is not necessary that it be a part of the composite, meaning in that it is an attribute of it and subsisting in it, and it is not a part of the composite, so the objection does not arise that the form of the bed is an attribute of the wood while being a part of the bed. His saying: (Because it is possible that etc.) meaning that it has a specific relationship by which the essence of the thing is obtained in the mind, for the mind may move from one contrary to another, and mere improbability is of no use. His saying: (Rather he says, etc.) He turns away from what the author said and attaches to it a premise indicating that what the author mentioned is insufficient to prove the necessity of petitio principii. His saying: (It depends on its being self-evident) because what is meant by 'better known' is priority in conception, so if it were not self-evident, its knowledge would be prior to it in conception. And some erroneously thought that 'better known' means more manifest in disclosure, so they denied its dependence on self-evidence, and thus fell into error. His saying: (And what you mentioned etc.) is a rebuttal of the objection that his saying 'how can he concede' is a denial of a proven premise, and that is not permissible because the denial of it goes back to the denial of its proof. His saying: (Because it is possible that from the properties etc.) because definitions and proofs are preparations for the emanation of the sought from the overflowing principle, so it is possible that a strong mind is prepared for the emanation of the essence of the reality from it by merely imagining the properties. So it is not objected: how can properties be disclosive of the essence when there is no intellectual connection between them leading to disclosure, except that this explanation is only needed according to the philosophers; as for us, knowledge after correct investigation is by pure creation of God without preparation and generation, but by way of habit as previously mentioned, so the matter is clearer. His saying: (Rather he says: its being better known depends on its being self-evident) is an explanation of the author's intent, for the necessity of petitio principii does not appear fully from his expression as opposed to the expression of the commentator, but there is investigation in it: that better known in itself depends on self-evidence itself, and self-evidence itself does not depend on better known but rather entails it; however, to know it is the knowledge of self-evidence. It is not said: knowledge of self-evidence depends on knowledge of the necessary better known.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the objection that claiming existence is the most known (أعرف) leads to circular reasoning (مصادرة). The commentator explains that if existence is self-evident (بديهي), then one cannot claim it is acquired (كسبي). The argument shows that the premise that existence is better known depends on it being self-evident, which means the proof's premise relies on the very claim being proved, thus circular. The commentator then addresses various sub-objections: 1) The attribute of a part is not a part of the composite, using the example of the form of a bed being an attribute of wood but not a part of the bed. 2) The possibility that specific properties can lead to knowledge of essence through a mental transition from one thing to another, not just through logical inference. 3) The distinction that 'better known' means priority in conception (القدم في التصور), not just clarity, so self-evidence is necessary. 4) The rebuttal that denying a premise of a proof is not allowed because it amounts to denying the proof itself. 5) The commentator contrasts the philosophers' view that cognition requires preparation and emanation with the Ash'ari view that knowledge after correct investigation is directly created by God without causal generation. Finally, he clarifies that the author's intended meaning is that the necessity of circularity depends on understanding 'better known' as requiring self-evidence, and that self-evidence entails being better known, not the reverse. The passage ends mid-sentence with a discussion of whether knowledge of self-evidence depends on knowledge of the necessary better known.

جري العادة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'better known' means simply more obvious or manifest in disclosure.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that 'better known' means priority in conception (الأقدمية في التصور), which requires self-evidence.

Checkpoint

A) Because it assumes existence is self-evident without proof.
B) Because the premise that existence is better known depends on establishing that it is self-evident, which is the same claim.
C) Because existence cannot be known at all.
D) Because the arguments for it rely on induction which is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The charge of circularity against existence being most known is resolved by distinguishing between self-evidence and acquired knowledge within an Ash'ari framework.

Two Proofs that Existence is Kasbi and the Meaning of 'Need for a Definition'
Sanusi presents two proofs that existence is kasbi and explains the need for a definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two proofs that existence is a kasbi concept requiring definition, and the distinction between lack of self-evidence and need for a definition.

Core Idea
Existence is not self-evident; it must be defined through its relation to essence.

Sanusi argues that existence (wujud) is a kasbi concept—one that is not self-evident but requires a definition. He gives two proofs based on whether existence is identical to essence or superadded to it. This lesson unpacks these proofs and a key nuance about what 'need for a definition' really means.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument flow naturally.
2
Identify the two proofs
Find the two distinct proofs Sanusi gives. Notice the conditional structure: 'either it is identical to essence... or it is superadded.'
The first proof relies on the doctrine of al-Ash'ari; the second on the view of others.
3
Understand the nuance about 'need for a definition'
Sanusi clarifies that the proof only shows lack of self-evidence, not that a definition is actually obtained. Consider why this distinction matters for the argument.
The common misunderstanding is assuming 'need for a definition' implies actual acquisition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يدعي أنه كسبي (محتاج إلى معرف) لوجهين الأول أنه إما نفس الماهية كما هو (مذهب الشيخ) فلا يكون بديهياً كالماهيات (فإنه ليس كنه شيء منها بديهياً إنما البديهي بعض وجوهها) وإما زائد (عليها كما هو مذهب غيره) فيكون الوجود حينئذ (من عوارضها أي من عوارض الماهيات) فيعقل الوجود (تبعاً لها) لأن قوله: (محتاج إلى معرف) فسر بذلك لأن الدليل المذكور إنما يثبت الاحتياج إلى المعرف دون الحصول منه فلا بد من ضم مقدمة أخرى وهي أنه قد عرفت بتعريفات، فيكون ذلك فيه مناقشة لأن اللازم من الدليل المذكور عدم بداهته وهو لا يستلزم الاحتياج إلى المعرف لجواز كونه ممتنع الحصول.
He claims that it (existence) is acquired (kasbi), in need of a definition (ma'rif), for two reasons: first, because it is either identical to essence (mahiyyah) as is the doctrine of the Shaykh (al-Ash'ari), so it is not self-evident (badihi) like essences, for no inner reality (kunn) of any essence is self-evident; only some of their aspects (wujuh) are self-evident. Second, because it is superadded to it as is the doctrine of others, so existence then is among the accidents of essences, so existence is understood (yu'qal) in dependence upon them (taba'an laha), because His statement: 'in need of a definition' is interpreted thus because the mentioned proof only establishes the need for a definition, not that it is obtained from it, so another premise must be added, namely that it has been known through definitions. So there is a criticism in that, because the necessary implication of the mentioned proof is its lack of self-evidence, which does not entail the need for a definition, since it is possible that it is impossible to obtain.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof: the author claims that existence (wujud) is an acquired concept (kasbi) requiring a definition (ma'rif). Two proofs are given. The first proof: if existence is identical to essence (as per al-Ash'ari's doctrine regarding particular existences), then it is not self-evident because the inner reality (kunn) of no essence is self-evident; only some aspects (wujuh) are self-evident. The second proof: if existence is superadded to essence (as per the doctrine of others), then it is an accident ('arid) and is understood in dependence on its subject. Since essences are not self-evident in their inner reality, existence cannot be self-evident either. The commentator then explains the phrase 'need a definition': the proof only establishes the need for a definition, not that the definition is actually obtained; one must add the premise that it is known through definitions. A criticism follows: the proof only shows lack of self-evidence, not necessarily need for a definition, because it could be impossible to obtain. The passage thus presents the core argument and an initial critical nuance.

Key Terms
وجود
كسبي
بديهي
ماهية
عارض
محتاج إلى معرف
كنه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming 'need for a definition' implies that existence is actually definable and defined.
Correction: The proof only shows lack of self-evidence; it does not guarantee that a definition is attainable. It could be impossible to obtain, as the critic points out.

Checkpoint
What is the first proof that existence is kasbi based on?
A) Existence is identical to essence and therefore not self-evident.
B) Existence is an accident and depends on its subject.
C) Existence is self-evident by itself.
D) Existence is impossible to define.

Key Takeaway
Existence is a kasbi concept because it is either identical to non-self-evident essences or an accident dependent on them, proving it requires definition—though this only shows lack of self-evidence, not actual definability.

The lesson argues that existence is not self-evident and requires a definition. When you think of your own existence, do you define it by your essence (what you are) or by your actions? Which view aligns more with the lesson's reasoning, and what does that mean for how you live?

Resolving the Contradiction in the First Proof
The commentator resolves the problem by offering two interpretations of the disjunction in the first proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the apparent contradiction between the first proof and al-Ash'ari's view on absolute existence is resolved through interpreting 'either' as a division (taqsim) rather than a disjunction (tardid).

Core Idea
The word 'either' can divide kinds, not just present alternatives.

The commentator tackles a critical objection: the first proof seems to assume that existence is either identical to essence or superadded, but al-Ash'ari does not accept absolute existence as identical. How can the proof be sound? The resolution lies in the precise meaning of the Arabic word 'imma' (either). The commentator shows that it can be understood as a division into two kinds, both of which are acquired, thereby preserving the proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the full translation to understand the overall argument.
Pay attention to the phrase 'imma ... imma'.
2
Identify the problem
The problem is that the first proof uses a disjunction (either existence is identical to essence or superadded), but al-Ash'ari denies absolute existence. Why is this not contradictory?
The commentator raises this objection before resolving it.
3
First resolution: taqsim (division)
The word 'imma' can be for division (taqsim) rather than disjunction (tardid). Here, absolute existence is divided into two kinds: particular existences (identical to essences for al-Ash'ari) and absolute existence (superadded for others). Both are acquired, so absolute existence is acquired.
The first resolution reinterprets the disjunction as a classification.
4
Second resolution: two possibilities
Alternatively, 'imma' indicates two conceptual possibilities for absolute existence: either it is identical to absolute essence (as al-Ash'ari holds for particulars) or superadded. On both possibilities, it is acquired.
The commentator favors this interpretation because it aligns with the subject of dispute.
5
Note the commentator's preference
The commentator prefers the second resolution, supported by the singular use of 'essence' and its qualification. However, the first is also supported by the plural reference in the commentary.
Both resolutions show the proof is sound.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كسبياً ومع قوله : ( إنه إما نفس الماهية لا خفاء في أن النزاع في الوجود المطلق وأنه لا يمكن كونه نفس الماهيات فإنه يلزم اتحاد الماهيات وأنه ليس مذهب الأشعري، إذ ليس عنده وجود مطلق فلا صحة للترديد المذكور والقول بأن الشق الأول لمجرد الاستظهار ومدار الاستدلال على الشق الثاني لا يقبله طبع سليم فلا بد لتصحيحه من العناية فإما أن يقال أن من يدعي كونه كسبياً يدعي كسبية مطلق الوجود الشامل للوجود المطلق والوجودات الخاصة ويقول : إن مطلق الوجود قسمان وجودات خاصة هي نفس الماهيات عند الأشعري، ووجود مطلق هو عارض للماهيات عند غيره وكلاهما كسبيان فيكون مطلق الوجود كسبياً فكلمة إما للتقسيم لا للترديد وإما أن يقال: إن الوجود المطلق له احتمالان عند العقل إما أن يكون نفس الماهية المطلقة كما هو مذهب الشيخ في الوجودات الخاصة وإما عارضاً للماهية المطلقة كما هو مذهب غيره وعلى الاحتمالين يكون كسبيا وإفراد لفظ الماهية هاهنا وتوصيف لفظ ماهية بمعينة في الجواب يؤيد هذا التوجيه وهو الأظهر عندي لموافقته محل النزاع وإن كان إرجاع الشارح الضمير في قوله من عوارضها إلى الماهيات بصيغة الجمع مؤيداً للاحتمال الأول.
[The word] 'acquired' and with His statement: 'it is either the essence itself' – it is not hidden that the dispute is about absolute existence (al-wujud al-mutlaq) and that it cannot be identical to essences, for that would necessitate the identity of essences, and that is not the doctrine of al-Ash'ari, since he does not hold absolute existence. So the aforementioned disjunction is not valid, and the claim that the first disjunct is merely for support and the proof relies on the second disjunct is not accepted by a sound nature. So for its correction, careful attention is needed. For either it is said that the one who claims it is acquired claims the acquisition of absolute existence, which includes absolute existence and particular existences, and says: absolute existence is of two kinds: particular existences which are identical to essences according to al-Ash'ari, and absolute existence which is an accident of essences according to others, and both are acquired, so absolute existence is acquired. Thus the word 'either' is for division (taqsim), not for disjunction (tardid). Or it is said: absolute existence has two possibilities in the mind: either it is the absolute essence itself, as is the doctrine of the Shaykh regarding particular existences, or it is an accident of the absolute essence, as is the doctrine of others, and on both possibilities it is acquired. The singular use of the word 'essence' here and its qualification with a specific essence in the answer supports this interpretation, and it is the more apparent to me because it agrees with the subject of dispute, even though the commentator's reference of the pronoun in his statement 'from its accidents' to essences in the plural supports the first possibility.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis and resolution of a problem in the first proof. The problem: the first proof assumes existence is identical to essence, but al-Ash'ari does not hold absolute existence (al-wujud al-mutlaq) to be identical to essences; he only holds particular existences to be identical. So the disjunction 'either identical or superadded' seems invalid. The commentator offers two ways to resolve this. First: the one claiming existence is acquired is claiming acquisition of absolute existence, which includes both absolute existence (superadded) and particular existences (identical). The word 'either' (imma) is for division (taqsim) into two kinds, not for disjunction between alternatives. Both kinds are acquired, so absolute existence is acquired. Second: absolute existence has two conceptual possibilities: it could be identical to absolute essence (as the Shaykh holds for particulars) or superadded to absolute essence (as others hold). On both possibilities, it is acquired. The commentator favors the second interpretation because it aligns with the subject of dispute, though the first is supported by the commentator's plural reference. The passage thus vindicates the soundness of the original disjunction.

وجودات خاصة

ترديد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that the first proof contradicts al-Ash'ari's position because it assumes absolute existence could be identical to essence.
Correction: The proof can be interpreted as a division of kinds (taqsim) or as two conceptual possibilities, both of which are compatible with al-Ash'ari's denial of absolute existence as identical. The word 'either' does not force an alternative that contradicts al-Ash'ari.

Checkpoint
How does the commentator resolve the problem that the first proof seems to conflict with al-Ash'ari's denial of absolute existence?
A) By rejecting the first proof entirely.
B) By interpreting 'either' as a division (taqsim) rather than a disjunction (tardid).
C) By claiming absolute existence is self-evident.
D) By saying the proof is only metaphorical.

Key Takeaway
The apparent contradiction is resolved by understanding 'imma' as a division (taqsim) into two kinds of existence (particular and absolute), both acquired, or as two conceptual possibilities for absolute existence, both acquired.

The Self-Evident Is Only Aspects and Infinite Regress Repelled
Sanusi clarifies that self-evident aspects are negative concepts and blocks regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that self-evident aspects are negative concepts (salub) which prevents infinite regress in the chain of acquisition of theoretical aspects.

Core Idea
Self-evident aspects are not real things; they are negative concepts, so no regress.

This passage addresses a key objection: if every aspect is a reality, then understanding one aspect would require another, leading to infinite regress. The commentator shows that the self-evident aspects are negative attributes (salub), which have no essence, thus terminating the chain.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once. Focus on the structure: statement, explanation, objection, response.
Notice the parenthetical objection citing the commentator's own work.
2
Identify the key claim about the self-evident aspects
The passage states that only some aspects are self-evident, and these are from the negative attributes (salub). They have no essence and are not true accidents.
This is the core of the argument.
3
Understand how this repels the infinite regress objection
The objection claimed that if an aspect is a reality, understanding it would require another aspect, leading to regress. The response is that the self-evident aspects are not realities; they are negative concepts, so no regress occurs.
The regress is avoided because negative concepts do not have an inner reality to be understood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( إنما البديهي بعض وجوهها وهو الذي ينقطع إليه سلسلة اكتساب الوجوه النظرية ويكون ذلك الوجه من السلوب فليس له ماهية حتى يكون كنهه كسبياً إذ الماهيات هي الوجودات بل مفهوم سلبي يصدق على الماهية وليس عارضاً حقيقة حتى يكون تعقله بالكنه تبعا لتعقل معروضه بالكنه فاندفع ما قيل إنه لا يمكن أن يكون بعض الوجوه بديهياً بالكنه لكونه وهو أنه قوله إنما البديهي بعض وجوهها) فيه بحث أشار إليه الشارح في بعض مصنفاته يلزم التسلسل في تصورات الوجوه بل عدم إمكان تعقل شيء لأن الوجه حقيقة من الحقائق ويمكن أن يدفع هاهنا بأن مرادهم نفي بداهة كنه شيء من الماهيات الموجودة إذ هذا القدر يكفي لهم في الاستدلال على كسبية تصور الوجود ولا يلزم كون الوجه حقيقة موجودة . قوله: (فيعقل تبعاً لها إن أراد تبعية تصور الوجود لتصور الماهيات بالكنه فممنوع وسنده وجود الواجب تعالى وإن أراد تبعية تصور الوجود لتصورها ولو بالوجه فمسلم لكن تصور بعض الوجوه بديهي بالتحقيق والاعتراف فلا يلزم كسبية تصور الوجود.
His statement: 'only some of their aspects are self-evident' – and that is what terminates the chain of acquisition of theoretical aspects, and that aspect is among the negative attributes (salub), so it has no essence such that its inner reality would be acquired; for essences are existences, rather it is a negative concept (mafhum salbi) that applies to the essence and is not truly an accident such that its understanding by inner reality would be dependent on understanding its subject by inner reality. Thus is repelled what was said: that it is impossible for some aspects to be self-evident by inner reality because it is... and that is that his statement 'only some of their aspects are self-evident' contains an investigation to which the commentator alluded in some of his works: that it necessitates an infinite regress in the conceptions of aspects, indeed the impossibility of conceiving anything, because the aspect is a reality among realities. And it can be repelled here by saying that their intention is to deny the self-evidence of the inner reality of any of the existing essences, for this much suffices for them in the inference to the acquisition of the concept of existence, and it does not necessitate that the aspect is an existing reality. His statement: 'so it is understood in dependence upon them' – if he intends the dependence of the concept of existence upon the concept of essences by inner reality, it is denied, and its proof is the existence of the Necessary Exalted; but if he intends the dependence of the concept of existence upon their concept even by aspect, it is granted, but the concept of some aspects is self-evident in verification and admission, so the acquisition of the concept of existence is not necessitated.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage is a clarification and defense. The author states that the self-evident (badihi) is only some aspects (wujuh) of essences, which cuts off the chain of acquisition of theoretical aspects. These aspects are negative concepts (salub), not true accidents, so understanding them does not require understanding the inner reality of the subject. The objection that this leads to infinite regress in the conceptions of aspects (since each aspect is a reality) is answered: the intent is only to deny the self-evidence of the inner reality of any existing essence, which is sufficient for the argument; the aspect need not be an existing reality. Then a second point: regarding the claim that existence is understood in dependence on essences – if this means dependent on understanding the inner reality of essences, it is denied (proof: the existence of the Necessary Existent); if it means dependent on understanding by any aspect, it is granted, but since some aspects are self-evident, existence does not become necessarily acquired. The passage thus defends the original position against two objections.

تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Self-evident aspects must be real entities, leading to infinite regress in understanding.
Correction: Self-evident aspects are negative concepts (salub), not real entities, so no regress occurs.

Checkpoint
How does the commentator avoid infinite regress in the conception of aspects?
A) By denying that aspects exist.
B) By stating that the intent is only to deny self-evidence of inner realities, not to posit aspects as real things.
C) By claiming that all aspects are self-evident.
D) By accepting the regress as unavoidable.

Key Takeaway
Self-evident aspects are negative concepts that terminate the chain of acquisition without regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often assume that the truths you hold are fully understood. How does knowing that some self-evident aspects are merely negative concepts humble your intellectual pride? Will you now acknowledge that even your most basic certainties are incomplete?
?
This lesson teaches that the chain of your knowledge terminates in a mere negation. Does this not shake your confidence in your own reasoning? How will you now rely more on Allah as the source of all true knowledge, rather than your own intellect?
?
If the self-evident aspects have no essence, then your very ability to conceive of existence depends on recognizing what it is not. What does this tell you about the limits of human comprehension? Will you now approach the divine attributes with greater reverence, knowing that your concepts ultimately fall short?

The Dependency of Existence on Essences
Sanusi distinguishes two types of dependency of existence on essences and uses the Necessary Existent as proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between understanding existence through the inner reality of essences versus through their aspects, and why existence is not necessarily acquired.

Core Idea
Existence is understood by aspects, not by inner reality, and some aspects are self-evident.

After establishing that some aspects are self-evident, the commentator addresses an objection: if existence is understood in dependence on essences, then existence would be acquired. He distinguishes between two types of dependence, showing that only dependence by aspect is true, and since aspects are self-evident, existence is not acquired.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Focus on the sentence beginning with 'فيعقل تبعاً لها' and its continuation.
Notice the phrase 'إن أراد... وإن أراد' which sets up a conditional analysis.
2
Identify the two interpretations of 'dependence'
The commentator distinguishes dependence on essences 'by inner reality' (بالكنه) from dependence 'by aspect' (بالوجه).
The first is denied, the second is granted.
3
See how the Necessary Existent proves the point
The existence of the Necessary Existent is used as proof that understanding existence does not require understanding the inner reality of essences, since the Necessary Existent's existence is not dependent on any other essence.
This is a powerful theological argument.

Existence as Accident: Objections and Responses
Dialectic on existence as accident: objections and rejoinders.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dialectical argument about whether existence is an acquired concept due to its dependence on essences, and the key responses to objections.

Core Idea
Existence can be conceived without its subject, so it need not be acquired.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection-response chain: if existence is an accident of essence, and essences are not self-evident, then existence would not be self-evident either. But the author and commentator defend the possibility that existence is known without being acquired. Let's follow the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objection claims that existence as an accident depends on its subject, and since essences are not self-evident, existence cannot be self-evident.
Note the key terms: 'accident' (عارض), 'intelligibility' (معقولية), 'acquired' (كسبي).
2
Identify the first response
The first response denies that the accident must be understood in dependence on its subject; one can conceive the accident without noticing the subject.
3
Examine the second objection and response
A further objection says existence is the foremost concept, so how can it be dependent? The response concedes that a specific essence (like heat) can be self-evident, so the accident is understood through it.
The term 'self-evident by inner reality' (بديهي بالكنه) appears.
4
Follow the commentator's clarifications
The commentator explains each phrase, clarifying that 'accident' refers to the extension not the concept, and that dependence on the acquired does not entail acquisition.
Pay attention to the distinction between concept (مفهوم) and extension (ما صدق عليه).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العارض لا يستقل بالمعقولية لكن الماهيات ليست بديهية ( فلا يكون ) الوجود ( بديهياً أيضاً لأن التابع للكسبي أولى بأن يكون كسبياً ) والجواب لا نسلم أنه إذا كان عارضاً للماهية عقل تبعاً لها إذ قد يتصور مفهوم العارض دون ملاحظة معروضة) ومن يدعي أن تصور الوجود أول الأوائل في التصورات كيف يسلم أن تعقله تبع لتعقل غيره ( سلمنا لكن يكفي لتصور العارض ) تصور ماهية معينة وقد تكون ضرورية) فيعقل العارض تبعاً لهذه الماهية الضرورية فلا يلزم كونه كسبياً ( وقد يجاب عنه ) أي عن هذا الوجه ( بأنه يعقل) العارض (تبعاً للماهية المطلقة) الصادقة على الماهيات كلها ماهية من الماهيات وقد فرض كسبية كنهها وأنه ينافي ما ذكره في الشق الثاني من أن كسبية المعروض تستلزم كسبية العارض لأنه يعقل تبعاً له. قوله : ( لأن العارض لا يستقل بالمعقولية لاشتماله على المعروض الذي هو غير مستقل بالمفهومية لكونه إضافة وهذا الحكم منشؤه اشتباه مفهوم الشيء بما صدق عليه فإن العروض الذي هو إضافة معتبر في مفهوم العارض لا فيما صدق عليه. قوله ليست بديهية أي بالكنه . قوله : (بديهياً) أي بالكنه . قوله : ( لأن التابع إلخ إذ له احتياجان احتياج لذاته واحتياج بواسطة ما يحتاج إليه وهذا الحكم منشؤه توهم أن ما يحصل عقيب الكسب فهو كسبي وليس كذلك فإن الكسبي ما يحصل بالكسب. قوله : ( مفهوم العارض أي مفهوم ما صدق عليه العارض وكذا في معروضه لأن الكلام فيما صدق عليه لا في مفهومهما . قوله: (وقد تكون (ضرورية أي بالكنه كالحرارة والبرودة فهو منع لقوله لكن الماهيات ليست بديهية. قوله : ( لأن التابع للكسبي أولى بأن يكون كسبياً مردود بما أشير إليه في مباحث النظر من أن العلم بالبديهي قد يكون تابعاً للكسبي ومنه علم العالم بأن له هذا العلم الكسبي . قوله : ( إذ قد يتصور مفهوم العارض) فيه أن العارض إذا كان إضافة أو مستلزماً لها لا يتصور بدون المضاف إليه والظاهر أن الوجود من هذا القبيل فلا يتصور بدون المضاف إليه الذي هو معروضه فالأولى أن يجاب بما ذكرنا الآن أو بالجواب الذي أدعى فيه الاستدراك إذ لا استدراك على هذا التقدير فتدبر . قوله : ( وقد تكون (ضرورية أي بالكنه كتصور الحرارة وادعاء كسبية الجميع باطل أو نقول : معناه قد يكون تصور تلك الماهية المعينة بديهياً ولو بالوجه والتصور بالوجه يكفي في المتبوعية كما أشرنا إليه فلا يرد منع بداهة شيء من الحقائق .
The accident does not stand alone in intelligibility, but essences are not self-evident, so existence is not self-evident either, because the dependent on the acquired is more worthy of being acquired. The answer: we do not grant that if it is an accident of an essence, it is understood in dependence on it, for one may conceive the concept of the accident without noticing its subject. And whoever claims that the concept of existence is the foremost of concepts, how can he grant that its understanding is dependent on the understanding of another? We grant, but for the conception of the accident, a conception of a specific essence suffices, and that essence may be necessary, so the accident is understood in dependence on this necessary essence, and thus it does not necessitate its being acquired. And it may be answered about this [face] that the accident is understood in dependence on the absolute essence (al-mahiyyah al-mutlaqah) which applies to all essences – an essence among essences – and its inner reality has been assumed to be acquired, and that contradicts what he mentioned in the second disjunct, that the acquisition of the subject necessitates the acquisition of the accident because it is understood in dependence on it. His statement: 'because the accident does not stand alone in intelligibility due to its inclusion of the subject which is not independent in conceptuality because it is a relation' – this judgment originates from confusing the concept of a thing with its extension, for 'accidentalness' which is a relation is considered in the concept of the accident, not in its extension. His statement: 'are not self-evident' meaning by inner reality. His statement: 'self-evident' meaning by inner reality. His statement: 'because the dependent, etc.' – for it has two needs: a need in itself and a need by means of what it needs. This judgment originates from the illusion that what is obtained subsequent to acquisition is acquired, but it is not so, for the acquired is what is obtained by acquisition. His statement: 'the concept of the accident' – meaning the concept of what the accident applies to, and likewise for its subject, because the discussion concerns their extensions, not their concepts. His statement: 'and it may be necessary' meaning by inner reality, like heat and cold, so it is a rebuttal of his statement 'but essences are not self-evident.' His statement: 'because the dependent on the acquired is more worthy of being acquired' is rebutted by what was indicated in the discussions of theoretical reasoning, that knowledge of the self-evident may be dependent on the acquired, such as the scholar's knowledge that he has this acquired knowledge. His statement: 'for one may conceive the concept of the accident' – therein [it is argued] that if the accident is a relation or entails one, it cannot be conceived without the relatum, and it appears that existence is of this kind, so it cannot be conceived without the relatum which is its subject; so it is more appropriate to answer with what we have now mentioned or with the answer in which a rectification is claimed, for there is no rectification on this assumption, so reflect. His statement: 'and it may be necessary' meaning by inner reality, like the conception of heat, and the claim of acquisition of all is false. Or we say: its meaning may be that the conception of that specific essence is self-evident even by aspect, and conception by aspect suffices for followership as we have indicated, so the denial of the self-evidence of any reality is not valid.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is a multi-layered objection-and-answer section. It begins with the claim: an accident does not stand alone in intelligibility, so existence (as accident) depends on its subject; since essences are not self-evident, existence is not self-evident. The response: one can conceive the concept of an accident without considering its subject. Then a further objection: existence is the foremost of concepts, so how can it be dependent? Concession: but a specific essence may be self-evident (like heat), and then the accident is understood through it, so existence may not be acquired. Another answer: the accident is understood through the absolute essence, which is assumed acquired, but that would contradict the second disjunct. Then the commentator explains each phrase: 'accident does not stand alone' confuses concept with extension; 'self-evident' means by inner reality; 'dependent on the acquired' is rebutted because self-evident knowledge can be dependent on acquired knowledge; the objection that an accident as relation cannot be conceived without relatum is answered by the earlier point. Finally, the possibility of a self-evident essence (by aspect) is affirmed, so existence may be known without being acquired. The passage thus provides a comprehensive defense of the possibility that existence is not necessarily acquired.

معقولية
إضافة
كسب
بديهي بالكنه
بديهي بالوجه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One cannot conceive an accident (like existence) without its subject.
Correction: The passage states that one can conceive the concept of the accident without noticing its subject; the concept of accident does not require its subject in the mind.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is one response to the claim that an accident cannot be conceived without its subject?
A) Existence is not an accident.
B) One can conceive the concept of an accident without noticing its subject.
C) All subjects are self-evident.
D) The accident is identical to its subject.

Key Takeaway
Existence can be known without being acquired because its concept is independent of its subject in the mind.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe in Allah's existence, but do you ever treat it as just a concept you've 'acquired' from teachers or books, rather than a living reality? How can you shift from knowing about existence to knowing existence itself?
?
If existence is self-evident, why do you sometimes act as if it is doubtful—prioritising worldly affairs over the eternal? What would change if you truly lived as if the Most Evident were always present?
?
The passage argues that an accident can be conceived without its subject. When you think of your own existence, do you separate it from your essence? What does that reveal about your understanding of yourself as a contingent being dependent on the Necessary?

The Second Objection and Its Two Answers
The author presents the second objection and two responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection to the self-evidence of existence and the two answers: the distinction between verbal and conceptual definition, and the clarification that the existence under dispute is the self-evident being in concrete individuals.

Core Idea
Defining a self-evident concept can be a mere verbal clarification, not a denial of its self-evidence.

This passage tackles a powerful objection: if existence is self-evident, why do scholars define it? The author provides two nuanced answers that clarify what kind of definition is being used and what exactly is the existence under debate. Understanding these answers is crucial to defending the self-evidence of existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection carefully: if existence were self-evident, rational people would not bother defining it, just as they do not prove self-evident propositions.
Notice the parallel between definitions of concepts and proofs of propositions.
2
First answer: verbal definition
The first answer distinguishes a definition that conveys the concept (which would negate self-evidence) from a verbal definition that merely clarifies the intended meaning of a word. A self-evident concept can be verbally defined.
The author calls this a 'verbal definition' and says its outcome is assent, not conception.
3
Second answer: the real subject of dispute
The second answer argues that those who defined existence were actually defining an imagined entity they thought caused being in concrete individuals, not the self-evident being itself. That imagined entity was not self-evident, so defining it does not undermine the self-evidence of the real being.
This answer hinges on the distinction between existence and being in concrete individuals (al-kawn fi al-a'yan).
4
Reflect on the overall defense
Consider how both answers together defend the claim that existence (as being in concrete individuals) is self-evident, despite the fact that some scholars have written definitions of existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني أن يقال لا شك أنه لا يشتغل العقلاء بتعريف التصورات البديهية كما لا يبرهون على القضايا البديهية فلو كان الوجود ضرورياً لم يعرفوه والجواب أن تعريفه ليس لإفادة تصوره حتى ينافي كونه بديهيا بل تعريفه لتمييز ما المراد بلفظ الوجود من بين سائر المتصورات ولتلتفت النفس إليه بخصوصه فيكون تعريفاً لفظياً مآله التصديق كما مر والأمور البديهية يجوز تعريفها اللفظ فإن البديهي وإن كان حاصلا في الذهن بديهة لكن قد يكون مجهولا من حيث أنه مدلول لفظ مخصوص ومراد به فيعرف ليعلم أنه مدلوله ومراد به وقد أجيب عن الوجه الثاني أيضاً بأن أحداً لم يشتغل بتعريف الكون في الأعيان الذي وقع النزاع فيه لكن جماعة لما تصوروا أنه أي الوجود ليس هو الكون في الأعيان بل هو شيء يوجب الكون في الأعيان ولم يكن ذلك الشيء الذي توهموا أنه الوجود ضروريا اشتغلوا بتعريفه وذلك لا ينافي بداهة الكون في الأعيان.
The second aspect: it is said that there is no doubt that rational people do not occupy themselves with defining self-evident concepts just as they do not prove self-evident propositions. So if existence were necessary, they would not define it. The answer: its definition is not for the purpose of conveying its conception such that it contradicts its being self-evident, but rather its definition is to distinguish what is meant by the word 'existence' from among all other concepts and to turn the soul's attention to it specifically. Thus it is a verbal definition whose outcome is assent, as previously mentioned. And self-evident matters may be given a verbal definition, for the self-evident, although it is present in the mind intuitively, may be unknown in terms of its being the meaning of a particular word and intended by it, so it is defined to make known that it is its meaning and intended. And a response was also given to the second aspect: that no one has occupied himself with defining the 'being in concrete individuals' (al-kawn fi al-a'yan) that is the subject of dispute; rather a group, when they conceived that existence is not being in concrete individuals but rather something that necessitates being in concrete individuals, and that thing which they imagined to be existence was not necessary, they occupied themselves with defining it, and that does not contradict the self-evidence of being in concrete individuals.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection to the claim that existence is self-evident (badihi) and provides two answers. The objection states: if existence were self-evident, rational people would not bother defining it, just as they do not prove self-evident propositions. Therefore, the fact that existence is defined shows it is not self-evident. The first answer distinguishes between a definition that conveys the concept (which would contradict self-evidence) and a verbal definition that merely clarifies which meaning is intended by a particular word. Since self-evident concepts can be unknown in terms of their linguistic designation, a verbal definition is permissible and does not negate self-evidence. The second answer argues that those who defined existence were not defining self-evident being in concrete individuals, but rather an imagined entity that they thought caused that being. Since that imagined entity was not self-evident, they defined it. This does not undermine the self-evidence of the actual being in concrete individuals that is the real subject of dispute.

Key Terms
الوجود
بديهي
تعريف لفظي
تمييز
كون في الأعيان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a concept is defined, it cannot be self-evident.
Correction: A verbal definition clarifies which meaning a word refers to, without conveying a new concept, and thus does not negate self-evidence.

Checkpoint
According to the second answer, why did some scholars define existence?
A) Because existence is not self-evident.
B) To distinguish the intended meaning of the word 'existence'.
C) Because they were defining a different concept, not the self-evident being in concrete individuals.
D) To refute the skeptics who denied existence.

Key Takeaway
The fact that existence is verbally defined does not disprove its self-evidence; the definition may be merely verbal, and the real existence (being in concrete individuals) remains self-evident despite definitions of a different imagined concept.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own existence, do you find it self-evident that you exist, or have you ever needed to prove it to yourself? What might that reveal about the nature of self-evident truths?
?
Consider a time when someone misunderstood a word you used because it had multiple meanings. How does that experience help you appreciate the difference between clarifying a word's meaning and defining the concept itself?
?
If the most fundamental reality (like existence) is self-evident, why do scholars sometimes spend pages defining it? What does that tell you about your own assumptions about what is obvious and what needs explanation?

The First Proof: Existence Requires Distinction
Al-Jurjani presents the first proof that existence is inconceivable and clarifies the scope of the dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument that existence is inconceivable, specifically the claim that conceiving existence requires distinguishing it from non-existence, leading to a problematic dependency.

Core Idea
To conceive existence, you must distinguish it from what it is not — but that distinction depends on conceiving existence.

In this passage, al-Jurjani presents the first proof from those who deny that existence can be conceived at all. They argue that any conception of existence must involve distinguishing it from other things, and that this distinction reduces to a negation that ultimately depends on the very concept of existence, creating a circularity. This lesson unpacks the proof and clarifies that the dispute is specifically about essential conception (bi al-kunh), not conceptualization in any sense.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof and its components
Read the Arabic passage slowly, then the translation. Notice how the proof moves from conception to distinction to negation to non-existence to existence.
The proof is dense; take time to follow each step.
2
Understand essential vs. aspectual conception
The author clarifies that the dispute is about essential conception (bi al-kunh), not conception by aspect (bi al-wajh). Conception by aspect still allows distinguishing the aspect, not existence itself.
This distinction answers the objection that the proof would make existence absolutely inconceivable.
3
Reflect on the dependency chain
Trace the chain: conception of existence → distinction (it is not other) → particular negation → absolute non-existence → absolute existence. Why is this problematic?
The chain seems to require already knowing existence to conceive it, creating a circle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرقة الثانية المنكرين لكون الوجود بديهياً من يدعي أنه لا يتصور الوجود أصلاً لا بداهة ولا كسباً بل هو ممتنع التصور واحتجوا على ذلك بأمرين الأول أن تصوره إنما يكون بتميزه عن غيره لأن المدرك متميز بالضرورة عن غير المدرك ومعنى التميز أنه ليس غيره و معنى أنه ليس غيره سلب مخصوص فيتوقف تعقله على تعقل السلب المطلق الذي هو عدم مطلق لا يعقل إلا بعد تعقل الوجود المطلق قوله : ( أنه لا يتصور (الوجود أي بالكنه على ما هو المتنازع فيه . قوله : ( أن تصوره إنما يكون (الخ أي تصوره بالكنه إنما يكون بهذا الطريق بأن يتميز الوجود عن غيره لأن التصور هو الانكشاف والتميز على ما مر وليس الباء للسببية حتى يردان التصور ليس مسبباً عن التميز وأن الدليل الذي ذكره الشارح لا يفيدها وأما تصوره بالوجه فهو في الحقيقة تميز لذلك الوجه باعتبار اتحاده مع ذي الوجه على ما حقق في موضعه فهو ليس تميزا للوجود فلا یرد ما قيل إن هذا الدليل لو تم لدل على امتناع تصور الوجود مطلقا والنزاع في التصور بالكنه وأنه إذا امتنع تصوره مطلقاً كيف يمكن الحكم عليه بأنه ممتنع التصور قوله : ( ومعنى التميز أنه ليس الخ فيه أن التميز عبارة عن الانكشاف والتجلي عند النفس والحكم المذكور لازم له . قوله : ( فيتوقف الخ) بناء على توقف تعقل المقيد على تعقل المطلق. قوله : (الأول) أن تصوره إنما يكون بتميزه إلخ ) فإن قلت هذا الدليل يدل على أن الوجود يتصور مطلقاً مع أن النزاع في الكنه فقط لا يقال التميز لازم للتصور بأمر جزئي إضافي بالنسبة إلى أمر آخر وأما إذا كان الوجه أعم المفهومات كالإمكان العام مثلاً فلا لأنا نقول قد سبق أن ما لا يفيد تميز الشيء عن غيره أصلاً لم يكن سبباً لتصوره . قلت : عدم العلم مطلقاً يستلزم عدم العلم بالكنه وهو المطلوب وكون النزاع في الكنه فقط ممنوع نعم يرد أن هذا الدليل لو تم على عدم إمكان تصور الوجود بوجه من الوجوه فلا يمكن الحكم بامتناع تصوره وغيره من الأحكام الموقوفة على تصوره المذكورة في الدليل المذكور هذا ويمكن أن يقرر الأمر الأول بأن تصوره بتميزه عن غيره ومعناه أنه ليس غيره وهو يتوقف على تصور المسلوب عنه الذي هو الوجود فيلزم الدور. والجواب الإجمالي أنه لو صح لزم أن لا يعقل شيء من الأشياء أصلاً بعين ما ذكر وأنه سفسطة وحله أن التصور يستلزم التميز لا أنه يتوقف عليه. قيل : فيلزم لكل تصور تصديق غير التصديقين الذين بينا لزومهما في تحقق الحد المختار للعلم وهو باطل اتفاقا وقد يجاب بأن الاستلزام الإجمالي والمتفق عليه هو عدم استلزامه للتفصيلي.
The second group—those who deny that existence is self-evident—claim that existence is not conceivable at all, neither intuitively nor acquisitively, but rather it is impossible to conceive. They argued for this with two things. The first: that its conception is only by distinguishing it from other things, because the perceived is necessarily distinguished from the not-perceived. And the meaning of distinction is that it is not other. And the meaning that it is not other is a particular negation, so its intellection depends on the intellection of absolute negation, which is absolute non-existence, which is not intellected except after the intellection of absolute existence. His statement: 'that it is not conceived' – i.e., essentially, as is the subject of dispute. His statement: 'that its conception is only by distinguishing it, etc.' – i.e., its essential conception is only by this way, that existence is distinguished from other things, because conception is disclosure and distinction as previously mentioned, and the 'ba' is not for causality such that it would be objected that conception is not caused by distinction and that the proof mentioned by the commentator does not support it. As for its conception by aspect, it is in reality a distinction of that aspect by virtue of its unity with the owner of the aspect, as established in its place, so it is not a distinction of existence. Thus the objection that this proof, if sound, would indicate the impossibility of conceiving existence absolutely, while the dispute is about essential conception, and if its conception is absolutely impossible, how can it be judged that it is impossible to conceive? His statement: 'and the meaning of distinction is that it is not other, etc.' – in it, that distinction is disclosure and manifestation to the soul, and the mentioned judgment is necessary for it. His statement: 'so it depends, etc.' – based on the dependence of the intellection of the qualified on the intellection of the absolute. His statement: 'the first: that its conception is only by distinguishing it, etc.' – If you say: this proof indicates that existence is conceived absolutely, while the dispute is only about the essence, it is not said that distinction is necessary for conception by a particular additional matter relative to another matter. And if the aspect is more general among concepts like general possibility, then not, for we say: it has preceded that what does not provide distinction of a thing from others at all is not a cause of its conception. I say: absolute lack of knowledge entails lack of essential knowledge, which is the desired conclusion, and the claim that the dispute is only about essence is rejected. Yes, it is objected that this proof, if sound on the impossibility of conceiving existence in any way, then it is impossible to judge its impossibility or other judgments that depend on its conception mentioned in the proof. This, and the first matter can be formulated as: its conception is by distinguishing it from others, and its meaning is 'it is not other', and it depends on the conception of the negated subject which is existence, leading to circularity. The summary answer: if it were correct, it would imply that no thing is intellected at all by the same token, and it is sophistry. The solution: conception entails distinction, not that it depends on it. It was said: then for every conception there would follow an assent other than the two assents we have shown to be necessary in the realization of the chosen definition of knowledge, and that is false by agreement. And it may be answered that the general entailment, which is agreed upon, is that it does not entail the detailed one.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof from the group who deny that existence can be conceived at all. They argue that conceiving existence requires distinguishing it from other things, and that distinction means 'it is not other', which is a particular negation. Understanding this negation depends on understanding absolute negation (non-existence), which itself depends on understanding absolute existence, leading to a circular dependency. The commentary then clarifies that the dispute is about essential conception (bi al-kunh), not conceptualization in any sense. It rejects the objection that the proof would make existence absolutely inconceivable by distinguishing between essential conception and conception by aspect. Further discussion notes that the proof's formulation can be seen as a vicious circle: existence must be conceived to conceive its distinction, but distinction requires negation which requires existence. Two responses are given: first, if this reasoning were sound, nothing could be conceived at all, which is sophistry; second, conception entails distinction, not that it depends on it. The final part addresses an objection that this would require an additional assent for every conception, which is refuted by distinguishing general from detailed entailment.

تميز
سلب
عدم
بالكنه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof claims existence cannot be conceived in any way at all.
Correction: The proof specifically targets essential conception (bi al-kunh), not conception by aspect (bi al-wajh). Conception by aspect is still possible.

Checkpoint
What type of conception does the first proof claim to be impossible?
A) Any conception whatsoever
B) Conception by aspect
C) Essential conception (bi al-kunh)
D) Intuitive conception

Key Takeaway
The first proof argues that essential conception of existence is impossible because it would require a vicious circular dependency on itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'God exists,' do you have an essential conception of that existence, or only an aspectual one? How does this distinction affect the strength of your belief?
?
This proof shows that trying to define existence leads to a circular dependency. Have you ever felt that your faith rests on concepts you cannot fully define? How do you handle that tension?
?
If essential conception of existence is impossible, what does that say about your ability to truly know any existent thing? Is there a point at which you must accept that some things are known only by their aspects? How does that impact your intellectual humility?

Rational Judgments

Responses to the First Proof: Circularity and Entailment
Al-Jurjani addresses objections to the first proof, including a circularity formulation and responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the proof leads to a vicious circle and the responses that conception entails distinction, not depends on it.

Core Idea
The proof's apparent circularity is resolved by noting that conception entails distinction; it does not depend on it.

After presenting the first proof, al-Jurjani anticipates several objections and provides responses. One objection reformulates the proof as a clear vicious circle: conception of existence requires distinction, which requires negation, which requires existence. The responses argue that if this reasoning were sound, nothing could be conceived at all (leading to sophistry), and more fundamentally, conception entails distinction rather than being dependent on it.

Guided Path
1
Understand the circularity formulation
Read the passage where the proof is restated as a circle: conception → distinction → negation → existence. This makes the dependency explicit.
The circle is: to conceive existence, you need to distinguish it; distinguishing it requires conceiving the negated subject (existence).
2
Grasp the 'sophistry' response
The first response: if the circularity argument were sound, it would apply to everything, making all conception impossible, which is absurd (sophistry).
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Appreciate the entailment vs. dependence distinction
The second response: conception entails distinction, not that it depends on it. Entailment does not create a circle because it is a logical consequence, not a prerequisite.
This is the key solution to the circularity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The second group—those who deny that existence is self-evident—claim that existence is not conceivable at all, neither intuitively nor acquisitively, but rather it is impossible to conceive. They argued for this with two things. The first: that its conception is only by distinguishing it from other things, because the perceived is necessarily distinguished from the not-perceived. And the meaning of distinction is that it is not other. And the meaning that it is not other is a particular negation, so its intellection depends on the intellection of absolute negation, which is absolute non-existence, which is not intellected except after the intellection of absolute existence. His statement: 'that its conception is only by distinguishing it, etc.' – i.e., its essential conception is only by this way, that existence is distinguished from other things, because conception is disclosure and distinction as previously mentioned, and the 'ba' is not for causality such that it would be objected that conception is not caused by distinction and that the proof mentioned by the commentator does not support it. As for its conception by aspect, it is in reality a distinction of that aspect by virtue of its unity with the owner of the aspect, as established in its place, so it is not a distinction of existence. Commentary
This passage presents the first proof from the group who deny that existence can be conceived at all. They argue that conceiving existence requires distinguishing it from other things, and that distinction means 'it is not other', which is a particular negation. Understanding this negation depends on understanding absolute negation (non-existence), which itself depends on understanding absolute existence, leading to a circular dependency. The commentary then clarifies that the dispute is about essential conception (bi al-kunh), not conceptualization in any sense. What is the main response to the circularity formulation of the first proof?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt trapped by a circular argument in your own thinking about Allah's attributes? How does the insight that conception entails and does not depend on distinction free you from that trap? Take a moment to identify one such circle in your thoughts and replace dependence with entailment.
?
The objection led to the absurd conclusion that nothing can be conceived. Are there beliefs you hold that, if followed consistently, would lead to a form of sophistry? Examine your heart: are you willing to accept logical consistency even when it challenges your comfort?
?
The solution distinguishes between entailment and dependence. In your relationship with Allah, do you sometimes treat His attributes as dependent on your understanding rather than entailed by His reality? How can you shift your mindset to acknowledge that His reality entails your knowledge, not the other way around?

The First Objection: Circularity in Defining Existence and Non-Existence and Its Answer
Al-Jurjani presents an objection and its answer regarding the definition of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that defining existence as an added property leads to circularity, and al-Jurjani's answer that existence is conceived by its own distinction and that affirmation/negation are not identical to existence/non-existence.

Core Idea
Existence is self-evident; logical negation is not ontological non-existence.

Al-Jurjani tackles a fundamental challenge: if existence is a property added to essences, then conceiving existence requires conceiving its opposite, non-existence, and vice versa. This would create a vicious circle. He offers a sophisticated answer that distinguishes between how we conceive existence and the nature of logical negation. This lesson unpacks that objection and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial answer
Read the first part of the passage where the objection is stated and the initial answer that existence is conceived by its own distinction.
Notice the phrasing 'conception by distinction in reality, not by knowledge of distinction'.
2
Understand the distinction argument
The answer claims existence is known directly, not by comparing with non-existence. Think about how this breaks the circularity.
3
Examine the second part: negation vs. non-existence
Even if dependence is granted, al-Jurjani argues that logical negation (salb) is not the same as ontological non-existence. Affirmation in a proposition does not require the predicate's existence.
This is key to Islamic logic.
4
Reflect on the commentator's expansions
The commentator ('qāla') adds depth, discussing the dependence of specific negation on general negation and the possibility of regress. Consider the caution that perhaps existence is not conceivable at all.
This is a deeper philosophical point.
