First Observatory - First Objective: On the Definition of Existence and Non-Existence. Because it is added to it, a circularity results: the conception (ta‘aqqul) of each one of existence and non-existence depends on the conception of the other. The answer: its conception [i.e., of existence] is by its distinction from other things in reality, not by knowledge of its distinction from it, such that the conception of negation (salb) which leads to circularity would be necessary in its conception. We grant that, but negation and affirmation (ījāb) are not the same as non-existence and existence, as you have known in the self-evidence of existence (badāhat al-wujūd). For you have known there that what is considered in an affirmative proposition is the truth (ṣidq) of the predicate of the subject, and that does not entail the existence of the predicate in itself nor its existence for the subject, but rather entails the subject's being qualified by it. So affirmation is not identical to existence nor does it necessitate its conception. Similarly, negation is the removal of that truth and qualification, so it is not identical to non-existence nor does it necessitate its conception either. However, the term 'existence', 'attainment', 'establishment', and 'realization' are sometimes applied to that truth and qualification because of its similarity to their true meaning which is our topic. Commentator says: (Regarding 'depends on the conception of each one') i.e., the conception of the inner reality (kunnh) of each one of existence and non-existence depends on the conception of the inner reality of the other, unlike if existence is conceived by a face (bi-al-wajh), then the conception of the face of existence depends on the conception of its face, and it is possible that the two faces are different. Commentator says: (And that does not entail) because the meaning of truth is union in identity, whether both are existent or non-existent, or the predicate is non-existent and the subject existent. Commentator says: (But rather entails the subject's being qualified) and what is said that the aforementioned qualification is the relational existence (al-wujūd al-rābiṭī), i.e., the existence of the predicate for the subject: if it is intended that we call it relational existence, there is no objection; but if it is intended that it is an existence for the predicate in any way, it is denied, for a non-existent matter does not smell the scent of existence. Commentator says: (Nor does it necessitate its conception) he mentioned it to emphasize the difference, otherwise it has no role in negating the necessity of circularity. Commentator says: (Because of its similarity to their true meaning) considering the ordering of effects upon that qualification, like ordering upon existence. Commentator says: (The answer: its conception etc.) and also the dependence of the conception of the specific negation on the conception of the general negation is only complete if the general is essential (dhātī) to the specific and the specific is conceived by its inner reality. And it was said: even if that dependence is granted based on the discussion of the absolute and the restricted, its dependence on its conception by its inner reality is denied; rather it is valid that the specific negation be understood with a conception of the absolute by some face. Then it is said: the conception of the existence of the absolute by some face, not by its inner reality, depends on the conception of the specific negation, which depends on the conception of the absolute negation by some face, which depends on the conception of the absolute existence by some face, not by its inner reality. Thus the dependent and that on which it depends are different. But there is discussion because you have verified that conception by a face also requires distinction, even from some of what is other than the conceived. And this proof indicates that existence is not conceived at all, so circularity or regress necessarily follows in the conceptions of existence. Let it be considered.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection regarding the definition of existence and non-existence and its answer. The objection states that if existence is taken as something added to actual entities (like a property), then conceiving existence requires conceiving non-existence, and vice versa, leading to a vicious circle. The answer begins by distinguishing between conceiving something through its own distinctness in reality versus through knowledge of its distinction from others. The author argues that existence is conceived by its inherent distinction, not by needing to know its difference from non-existence, so the circularity is avoided. The discussion then shifts to the relationship between negation/affirmation and existence/non-existence. The author clarifies that in a proposition, 'truth' (sidq) means the predicate applies to the subject, which does not require the predicate's own existence. Thus affirmation is not identical to existence, and negation (removal of that truth) is not identical to non-existence. This further diffuses the circularity because conceiving existence does not require conceiving negation. The commentator (the 'qāla' sections) expands on each point: explaining the dependence of conceptions, the meaning of truth as identity in essence, the nature of relational existence, and a deeper analysis of how specific negation depends on general negation and the possibility of regress. The passage ends with a caution that this line of reasoning might imply existence is not conceivable at all, leading to inevitable circularity or regress, which requires careful thought.

الوجود (wujūd/existence)
العدم ('adam/non-existence)
الدور (dawr/circularity)
السلب (salb/negation)
الإيجاب (ījāb/affirmation)
التصور (taṣawwur/conception)
بداهة الوجود (badāhat al-wujūd/self-evidence of existence)
الصدق (ṣidq/truth)
الوجود الرابطي (al-wujūd al-rābiṭī/relational existence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Conceiving existence necessarily requires conceiving non-existence.
Correction: Al-Jurjani argues that existence can be conceived by its own inherent distinction without reference to non-existence, and furthermore, logical negation is not the same as ontological non-existence.

Checkpoint
In the answer to the circularity objection, how is existence initially conceived?
A) By knowing its distinction from non-existence
B) By its own distinctness in reality, not by knowledge of its distinction
C) By conceiving the negation of non-existence
D) By accepting a regress

Key Takeaway
Al-Jurjani dissolves the circularity by distinguishing between the self-evident conception of existence and the logical operations of affirmation and negation, which are not identical to existence and non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the self-evidence of existence is so basic that you don't need to conceive non-existence first, why do you sometimes feel God's existence is distant or uncertain? Is your heart seeking clear proof while ignoring the self-evident presence of the Creator in every moment? What would it take for you to stop demanding constant signs and simply acknowledge the undeniable existence that you already know?
?
This lesson says that affirming something with your tongue (ījāb) is not the same as it truly existing in your life. When you say 'I believe in God,' does that affirmation change how you live each hour? If not, what is the disconnect between your verbal affirmation and your heart's true acknowledgment? How can you bridge that gap today?
?
The author argues that we conceive existence without needing non-existence. Yet you sometimes entertain doubts that require imagining a world without God. Why do you entertain those thoughts? What does it say about your reliance on God when you allow hypothetical non-existence to unsettle your certainty? Will you choose today to trust the self-evident reality of God rather than the illusion of absence?

The Second Objection: Mental Representation and Two Similars
Al-Jurjani handles the objection that conceiving existence requires a mental form, leading to two existences in the mind.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why conceiving existence does not require a mental form and how presence knowledge avoids the charge of two similar existences.

Core Idea
Conception of existence is by presence, not by mental image, avoiding two similars.

Al-Jurjani presents the second objection: if conception requires mental acquisition, then conceiving existence would produce a second existence in the soul, leading to two similar existences. He then gives a two-part answer: first, denying mental existence altogether; second, even if granted, asserting that existence is known by presence knowledge, which does not require an extracted form.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the passage that states the objection: conceiving existence leads to two similars in the soul. Identify the key terms: 'mental existence' and 'two similars'.
The objection relies on the assumption that conception equals mental acquisition of the quiddity.
2
Identify the two answers
The author gives two answers: first, deny mental existence altogether; second, even if granted, use presence knowledge. Trace both lines in the text.
Notice that the second answer is conditional: 'even if we grant it'.
3
Understand presence knowledge
Presence knowledge means the known object is directly present to the knower, not via a form. Relate this to self-awareness: we know our own essence without an image.
This avoids having two separate existences in the soul.
4
See how presence avoids similars
If existence is known by presence, there is no multiplicity in existence. The soul's own existence and the known existence are the same presence, so no two similars arise.
The commentator emphasizes that on this view, no multiplicity or similarity remains.

عن التماثل .
First Observatory - First Objective: On the Definition of Existence and Non-Existence. We conceive something, then two similars (al-mithlān) would gather in the soul, namely its existence and the conceived existence. The answer: what you mentioned, that the conception of a thing is the acquisition of its quiddity in the soul, is a statement of mental existence (al-wujūd al-dhihnī), and we do not grant mental existence. And even if we grant it, it suffices for its conception (i.e., the conception of existence) that it is obtained for the soul. Thus knowledge of existence would then be presence knowledge (ʿilm ḥuḍūrī), which does not require the acquisition of a form extracted from the known object in the world; rather the known object itself is present to it. Whether we say absolute existence is essential to the existence of the soul or accidental to it, in both cases it is present to us, just as we conceive our essence by our essence, not by a form extracted from our essence existing in our essence. Or we deny, on the assumption of granting mental existence, the similarity of the universal form which is... Commentator says: (And the conceived existence) because, by virtue of its acquisition in the mind, it is a particularized form subsisting in the soul, being a particular knowledge, so it is an individual of absolute existence just as its existence is an individual of it. It is said: in the soul, the two similars gather. And upon this, the answer mentioned in some books is rebutted, that the conceived existence is a universal quiddity acquired in the soul and its existence is an individual from it subsisting in the soul, and there is no similarity between the universal and its individual, nor between that which is acquired in the soul and that which subsists in it. Commentator says: (A statement of mental existence) meaning the acquisition of things themselves in the mind. Commentator says: (We do not grant mental existence) i.e., in the aforementioned sense, for it contains two denials: we do not grant acquisition absolutely in the mind, and even if granted, we do not grant the acquisition of the quiddities themselves in it; rather what is acquired are their phantoms (ashbāḥ). Commentator says: (And even if he grants) i.e., grants mental existence in the aforementioned sense, we do not grant that in our case, because that only applies to things external to the soul. As for things subsisting in the soul, it suffices for their conception that they themselves are obtained, and existence is among them. This is based on their statement that knowledge of external things is impressed knowledge (ʿilm inṭibāʿī), and knowledge of the soul and things subsisting in it is presence knowledge (ʿilm ḥuḍūrī), for which their presence themselves to the soul suffices, meaning that it does not need an extracted form from them; not meaning that mere subsistence in the soul is sufficient for knowledge, lest it be objected that then the attributes subsisting in the soul and essential/accidental matters would be known to us, while experience belies this. All Commentator says: (Regarding 'upon the assumption' etc.) indicates that it is conjoined to his statement 'it suffices for its conception', not to his statement 'we do not grant', as the illusion might arise from their agreement in the first person plural form. Commentator says: (And we do not grant mental existence) and even if granted, perhaps what exists in the mind are phantoms of things that differ from them in reality, as is the view of some, but this view is contrary to verification as will come. Commentator says: (And it suffices for its conception that it is obtained for the soul) and that existence obtained for the soul subsists in it, not like the subsistence of accidents in their loci, so on this assumption one should not imagine the gathering of two similars at all, for there is no multiplicity in existence, let alone similarity.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection and its answer. The objection: if conception is the acquisition of the quiddity in the soul, then when we conceive existence, the existence itself (as an object) would be acquired as a mental form. Since the soul itself has its own existence, two existences (the soul's existence and the conceived existence) would exist in the soul, and being similar, they would be 'two similars' (al-mithlān). The answer proceeds in two steps. First, the author rejects the premise of mental existence (al-wujūd al-dhihnī) altogether, meaning he does not accept that conception involves the actual thing itself coming into the mind. Second, even if mental existence is accepted, he argues that for existence (and other things subsisting in the soul), conception is not by acquiring a form but by presence (ḥuḍūr). Knowledge of existence is 'presence knowledge' (ʿilm ḥuḍūrī), where the known object is directly present to the knower, as in self-awareness. This avoids having two separate existences. The commentator elaborates: he explains that the objection relies on mental existence, which the author denies. Even if one grants mental existence, the author denies that quiddities themselves are acquired; rather only their phantoms (ashbāḥ) are in the mind. For things like existence that are already present in the soul (such as the soul itself), presence knowledge suffices. The commentator also addresses a potential rebuttal: that the conceived existence as a mental form is a particular individual of absolute existence, leading to similarity with the soul's existence. The author's response is that if existence is known by presence, there is no multiplicity; the existence of the soul is the same as the presence of existence, so no two similars. The passage ends with the commentator clarifying the grammar of the conditional statements and emphasizing that on the presence account, no multiplicity or similarity arises.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that mental existence is necessary for conception of any object.
Correction: The author shows that for objects already present in the soul (like existence itself), conception is by presence knowledge, not mental acquisition. For external objects, mental existence may be granted but only in the sense of phantoms, not the quiddities themselves.

Checkpoint

B) By presence knowledge, where existence itself is present to the soul
C) By comparing it to non-existence
D) By accepting that two existences reside in the soul

Key Takeaway
Conception of existence is achieved through presence knowledge, not mental representation, thus avoiding the charge of two similars.

intermediate
6 min
The Objection of Two Similars and Denial of Mental Existence
Iji presents the objection of two similars and the first answer denying mental existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that conceiving existence leads to two existences in the soul and the first answer that denies mental existence, instead proposing knowledge by presence.

Core Idea
Denying mental existence avoids the problem of two similars when imagining existence.

A major objection against conceiving existence is that it would require two existences—the soul's own existence and the imagined existence—to coexist in the mind, which seems absurd. The first answer tackles this by rejecting the very notion that imagining a thing involves its quiddity being present in the mind (mental existence). Instead, the defender proposes that existence is known directly by presence, without a mental image.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the part describing the objection: when we imagine existence, two existences (the soul's and the imagined) come together in the soul. Note the problem this poses.
The objection assumes mental existence.
2
Identify the first answer
The answer begins by denying mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni). Find where the author says 'we do not grant mental existence.'
This is the key move.
3
Explore the alternative: knowledge by presence
Even if mental existence were granted, the author says that for existence, its acquisition for the soul suffices. This leads to knowledge by presence (ilm huduri), where the known itself is present.
Compare with knowing your own essence directly.
4
Reflect on the illustration
The passage likens this to how we imagine our own essence by itself, not by a form abstracted from it. Consider how this refutes the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نتصور شيئاً ( فيجتمع) حينئذ في النفس ( المثلان ) أعني وجودها والوجود المتصور ( والجواب ) أن ما ذكرتم من أن تصور الشيء حصول ماهيته في النفس قول بالوجود الذهني ونحن لا نسلم الوجود الذهني ولئن سلم فيكفي في تصوره) أي تصور الوجود (حصوله للنفس) فيكون العلم بالوجود حينئذ علماً حضورياً لا يحتاج فيه إلى حصول صورة منتزعة من المعلوم في العالم بل يكون المعلوم نفسه حاصلاً له حاضراً عنده سواء قلنا الوجود المطلق ذاتي لوجود النفس أو عارض له فإنه على التقديرين حاضر عندنا وذلك ) كما نتصور ذاتنا بذاتنا لا بصورة منتزعة من ) ذاتنا حالة في ذاتنا
We imagine something, so then two similars come together in the soul: namely, its existence and the imagined existence. The answer: That which you mentioned — that the imagination of a thing is the acquisition of its quiddity in the soul — is a statement positing mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni), and we do not grant mental existence. And even if it is granted, it suffices for its imagination — i.e., the imagination of existence — its acquisition for the soul. So knowledge of existence then becomes knowledge by presence (ilm huduri) which does not require the acquisition of a form abstracted from the known in the world; rather, the known itself is acquired by it, present to it, whether we say that absolute existence is essential to the existence of the soul or accidental to it, for in both cases it is present to us. That is like how we imagine our own essence by itself, not by a form abstracted from our essence subsisting in our essence.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its first answer. The objection claims that when we imagine existence, two existences — the soul's own existence and the imagined existence — would be similar in the soul, which is problematic. The answer begins by pointing out that the objection assumes 'mental existence' (al-wujud al-dhihni), i.e., that the quiddity of the imagined thing itself is present in the mind. The author denies this premise: 'we do not grant mental existence.' Then even if mental existence were granted, he offers a second line: for existence, it is sufficient that the soul has its own existence present to it (knowledge by presence), without needing a mental form. The illustration compares this to how we know our own essence directly, without an intermediary image. The key nuance is that the author distinguishes between knowledge by presence (huduri) and knowledge by representation (hurt), and he asserts that existence is known directly, not via a conceptual form.

Key Takeaway
The first answer to the objection of two similars is to deny mental existence and affirm that existence is known directly by presence.

The First Definition of Existence and Non-Existence
Iji presents the first definition of existence as the established in entity and non-existence as the negated in entity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first definition of existence and non-existence as presented by Iji, and why the term 'entity' is crucial.

Core Idea
Existence is what is established in itself; non-existence is what is negated in itself.

This lesson introduces a foundational definition found in Islamic theology: the existent is that which is established with entity, and the non-existent is that which is negated with entity. The addition of 'entity' ensures we are talking about real existence in itself, not relational or conceptual existence. This distinction is vital for later discussions on the nature of God and creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the terms 'al-thabit al-ayn' and 'al-manfi al-ayn'.
These phrases are the core definitions.
2
Identify the two definitions
The passage defines both 'existent' and 'non-existent'. Write down each definition in your own words.
3
Understand the role of 'entity'
Reflect on why the word 'entity' (al-ayn) is added. What does it exclude? This step connects to the learning objective.
The commentary will clarify.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من قال بأنه ( أي الوجود ( يعرف ( حقيقة لكونه كسبياً عنده ) ذكر فيه عبارات الأولى أنه أي الموجود هو ( الثابت (العين والمعدوم هو المنفي العين وفائدة لفظ العين التنبيه على أن المعرف هو الموجود في نفسه والمعدوم في نفسه لا الموجود لغيره
Then whoever says that it — i.e., existence — is known in reality because according to him it is acquired (kasbi), mentions among statements concerning it: the first is that the existent (al-mawjud) is that which is established with entity (al-thabit al-'ayn), and the non-existent (al-ma'dum) is that which is negated with entity (al-manfi al-'ayn). The benefit of the word 'entity' is to indicate that what is defined is that which is existent in itself and non-existent in itself, not that which exists for something else.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage introduces a definition of existence and non-existence given by those who hold that existence is known by definition (kasbi) rather than being self-evident. The first definition stated is: the existent (al-mawjud) is 'the established with entity' (al-thabit al-'ayn), and the non-existent (al-ma'dum) is 'the negated with entity' (al-manfi al-'ayn). The term 'entity' (al-'ayn) is added to emphasize that the definition refers to the thing in itself (al-mawjud fi nafsihi) and not to a relational existence (e.g., existence in the mind). This definition covers both substance and accident, as both have 'entity' in the sense of being established in themselves. The author clarifies that the point is to define what it means for something to be real in itself, not merely attributed existence.

Correction: The addition of 'entity' (al-ayn) indicates that the definition concerns real existence in itself, not relational or mental existence.

B) To specify that existence is for something else.
C) To show that what is defined is existence in itself, not relational existence.
D) To restrict existence to substances only.

Key Takeaway
The existent is defined as that which is established in itself, and non-existence as that which is negated in itself, with 'entity' marking this intrinsic reality.

Second Answer: Denial of Similitude Between Universal Image and Particular Existence
Iji denies similitude between universal image and particular existence by limiting the rule to accidental subsistence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Iji's second answer to the objection of two similars, specifically how he denies similitude by distinguishing between different modes of subsistence.

Core Idea
The impossibility of two similars applies only to accidental subsistence, not to existential inherence in the soul.

After granting mental existence, a key objection remains: a universal mental image and the particular existence in the soul would be two similars in one subject. Iji's second answer refines this by focusing on how things subsist. He argues that the rule against two similars applies only when they subsist like accidents in a subject, but existence in the soul is not an accident. This lesson unpacks that subtle but crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'Or we deny — on the supposition of granting mental existence — ...'
The passage is a single, dense sentence; take your time parsing it.
2
Understand the context
Recall the objection: if the universal image (quiddity of existence) and the particular existence in the soul are two similars in one subject, that is impossible. Iji's second answer challenges the assumption that they are similars in the relevant sense.
3
Identify the denial and its basis
Find where Iji states 'we deny... the similitude' and then 'on the ground that the impossible is that two similars should subsist in one subject in the manner of accidents.' This is the core move.
The argument hinges on the phrase 'قيام الأعراض بمحالها' (subsistence of accidents in their loci).
4
Examine the distinction in subsistence modes
Contrast two modes: (1) accidental subsistence in a subject (like whiteness in a body), where two similars are impossible; (2) existence subsisting in the soul, which is not an accident. Therefore the rule does not apply.
This distinction preserves the possibility of mental existence without falling into absurdity.
5
Reflect on the significance
Reflect on how this answer protects the doctrine of mental existence from a key objection. Iji shows that careful ontological distinctions can resolve apparent contradictions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو نمنع على تقدير تسليم الوجود الذهني مماثلة الصورة الكلية التي هي ماهية الوجود للوجود الجزئي الثابت للنفس على أن الممتنع هو أن يقوم المثلان بمحل واحد قيام الأعراض بمحالها وليس قيام الوجود بالنفس كذلك
Or we deny — on the supposition of granting mental existence — the similitude of the universal image, which is the quiddity of existence, to the particular existence established for the soul, on the ground that the impossible is that two similars should subsist in one subject in the manner of accidents subsisting in their loci, whereas the subsistence of existence in the soul is not like that.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage provides a second, alternative answer to the objection of two similars. Even if mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni) were granted, the author denies that the universal image (al-surah al-kulliyyah) — which is the quiddity (mahiyyah) of existence — is similar to the particular existence (al-wujud al-juz'i) that belongs to the soul. The argument is that the impossibility of two similars in one subject applies only when they subsist like accidents in a substrate. The subsistence of existence in the soul is different; it is not an accident inhering in a subject. Therefore, the feared similitude does not arise. The author thus refines the objection by distinguishing between different modes of subsistence.

Key Terms
مماثلة
الصورة الكلية
الوجود الجزئي
قيام الأعراض بمحالها

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The denial of similitude is based on the impossibility of two similars in general.
Correction: The denial is specifically because the mode of subsistence (existence in the soul) is not like accidental subsistence in a subject, so the general rule against two similars does not apply.

Checkpoint
Why does the author deny similitude between the universal image and particular existence?
A) Because the universal image is a concept, not a real thing.
B) Because the subsistence of existence in the soul is not like the subsistence of accidents in a subject.
C) Because mental existence is completely denied, so no comparison is possible.
D) Because the universal image and particular existence are identical.

Key Takeaway
Iji's second answer refines the objection by distinguishing modes of subsistence: the rule against two similars applies only to accidental inherence, preserving the coherence of mental existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You hold countless mental images of things you have never seen. When you think of 'justice' or 'mercy', do you ever confuse that mental picture with the actual reality? Iji's distinction shows that the mental image is not the same as the particular existence. How often do you act as if your thoughts about something are identical to the thing itself? Will you pause today to examine one such assumption and ask: 'Is my mental image truly similar to the reality, or am I conflating them?'
?
The debate over two similars seems abstract, but it touches the foundation of your knowledge. If your mental concepts were exactly like external objects, you could never err. But you do err. So what does that say about the relationship between your mind and the world? Are you willing to humble your certainty and admit that your mental pictures are not identical to reality? Make a commitment: before you claim something is 'absolutely true', first ask if your mind has mistaken its own image for the thing itself.

The Objection of Similarity Between Mental Form and Existence
Al-Jurjani refutes the objection of similarity between mental form and existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani defends the integrity of mental existence against the objection of similarity with extramental existence.

Core Idea
Mental forms are not identical to existence; they are universal, existence is particular.

This passage tackles a subtle objection: if we know existence through a mental form, then that form resembles existence, leading to absurdities. Al-Jurjani offers a layered defense, clarifying the nature of mental forms and existence itself. Let's walk through his arguments.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector claims that the mental form of existence is similar to existence itself, leading to the impossibility of two similars in one subject. Read the opening line.
2
See the initial answer and its insufficiency
Al-Jurjani first notes that 'form' might mean the known thing, not knowledge. The initial denial of similarity between universal and particular is dismissed as insufficient.
No rational person would claim similarity between a universal and its particular.
3
Follow the corrected interpretation
The proper interpretation is to take 'form' as knowledge and deny similarity between the cognitive form and the existence fixed for it, because absolute existence is not the complete quiddity of both.
Even if existence is essential to the form, it is not essential to the existence fixed for the quiddity.
4
Grasp the second line of defense
Even if similarity is conceded, existence in the soul is not like an accident inhering in a subject; it is a purely abstract concept, not a superadded attribute.
Thus, the unification of two similars does not apply.
5
Conclude with the key distinction
The universal form is not similar to partial existence because the quiddity of existence in partial existence is not as a form or shadow. The addition of existence to quiddity is only mental.
This concludes the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( مماثلة الصورة إلخ) توصيف الصورة بقوله التي هي ماهية الوجود يشعر بأن المراد بالصورة المعلوم الذي هو موجود نقلي دون العلم الذي هو موجود أصيلي فإن الصورة تطلق عليهما على ما سيجيء في بحث العلم فحينئذ يكون حاصل الجواب منع المماثلة بينهما بناء على عدم المماثلة بين الكلي وفرده وبين الحاصل في النفس والقائم به ولا يخفى أن هذا الجواب لا يطابق الاستدلال على ما قررناه وأن دعوى التماثل بين الكلي وفرده مما لا يجترئ عليه عاقل فالتوجيه أن تحمل الصورة على العلم ويراد بقوله التي هي ماهية للوجود ماهيته بشرط قيامها المماثلة بالنفس فيرجع إلى منع بين الصورة العلمية القائم بالنفس وبين وجودها الثابت لها بناء على منع كون الوجود المطلق تمام ماهيتما حتى يتحقق التماثل بينهما فإنه وإن كان ذاتياً للصورة فلا نسلم ذاتيته للوجود الثابت لها فإن قلت تلك الصورة متشخصة فكيف يصح وصفها بالكلية؟ قلت كليتها باعتبار مطابقتها لكثيرين بمعنى أن كل واحد من أفرادها إذا حصل في الذهن يكون الحاصل منه هذا النقش بعينه لا ينافي تشخصها الذهني وتوصيف الصورة بالكلية والوجود بالجزئي للإشعار إلى سند منع التماثل بينهما . قوله : ( على أن الممتنع إلخ) أي ولو سلم المماثلة بينهما فالممتنع أن يكون كل واحد في محل واحد حلول الأعراض لأنه حينئذ يلزم اتحاد المثلين ضرورة اتفاقهما في الماهية والتشخص الحاصل بسبب الحلول في المحل والوجود القائم بالنفس ليس كذلك فإنه أمر انتزاعي محض يتصف به الأشياء في الذهن وليس أمرا زائداً على الماهية في الخارج. قوله : (هو الموجود في نفسه (الخ فمعنى الثابت المعين الذي ثبت عينه ونفسه، فيشمل الجوهر والعرض. قوله: (للوجود الجزئي) فإن قلت الصورة الكلية متحققة في ضمن الوجود الجزئي فالمحذور بحاله قلت ماهية الوجود متحققة في الوجود الجزئي لا بطريق كونها صورة وظلا لشيء بخلاف الصورة الكلية الحاصلة في النفس فلا مماثلة أصلاً . قوله : ( وليس قيام الوجود بالنفس كذلك يعني لو سلم أن قيام الصورة كذلك فظاهر أنه ليس قيام الوجود كذلك لما سيجيء من أن زيادة الوجود على الماهية إنما هي في الذهن فقط، هكذا قيل وهو الظاهر من عبارة الشارح ويحتمل أن يراد منع قيام الصورة بها كذلك، ولهذا لم يلزم زوجية النفس بحصول الزوجية فيها وأن يراد بقيام الأعراض بمحالها قيام موجب المحل بالحال لا زيادة الحال في الخارج كما لا يخفى على المتأمل، وسيأتي تتمة هذا الكلام في بحث الوجود الذهني .
His saying: (The similarity of the form etc.) describing the form by his saying 'which is the quiddity of existence' suggests that what is meant by 'form' is the known thing which is derived existence, not knowledge which is original existence, for the term 'form' is applied to both, as will come in the discussion of knowledge. Then the gist of the answer is the denial of similarity between them based on the lack of similarity between the universal and its particular, and between what subsists in the soul and what subsists by it. And it is not hidden that this answer does not match the argument as we have established, and that the claim of similarity between the universal and its particular is something no rational person would dare. So the correct interpretation is to take 'form' as meaning knowledge, and what is meant by his saying 'which is the quiddity of existence' is its quiddity with the condition that its similarity to the soul is considered, so it returns to denying the similarity between the cognitive form subsisting in the soul and the existence fixed for it, based on denying that absolute existence is the complete quiddity of both so that similarity would be realized between them. For even if it is essential to the form, we do not accept its essentiality to the existence fixed for it. If you say: that form is individuated, so how is it valid to describe it as universal? I say: its universality is by virtue of its correspondence to many, meaning that each of its individuals, when it occurs in the mind, what occurs is this very impression, which does not contradict its mental individuation. And describing the form as universal and existence as particular is to indicate the basis for denying the similarity between them. His saying: (That the impossible etc.) means: even if we concede the similarity between them, the impossible thing is that each of them should be in one subject with the indwelling of accidents, because then the unification of the two similars would necessarily follow due to their agreement in quiddity and the individuation resulting from indwelling in the subject, but existence subsisting in the soul is not like that, for it is a purely abstract thing that characterizes things in the mind and is not something superadded to the quiddity in the external. His saying: (He is the existent in himself etc.) so the meaning of al-thabit al-mu'ayyan (the fixed determinate) is that which has been fixed in its entity and itself, so it includes substance and accident. His saying: (For partial existence) if you say the universal form is realized within the partial existence, so the objection remains. I say: the quiddity of existence is realized in partial existence not by way of being a form and shadow of something, unlike the universal form that occurs in the soul, so there is no similarity at all. His saying: (And the subsistence of existence in the soul is not like that) means if it is conceded that the subsistence of the form is like that, then it is apparent that the subsistence of existence is not like that, because as will come, the addition of existence to the quiddity is only in the mind. Thus it is said, and it is the apparent meaning of the commentator. And it is possible that what is meant is the denial of the subsistence of the form in it in that manner, and for this reason the soul does not become paired by the occurrence of pairing in it, and that what is meant by the subsistence of accidents in their subjects is the subsistence of the subject's cause by the state, not the addition of the state in the external, as is not hidden from the contemplative. And the completion of this discussion will come in the investigation of mental existence.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer concerning the similarity between the mental form (al-sura) and existence (al-wujud). The objector claims that if existence is known through a mental form, then that form resembles existence, leading to the impossibility of two similars in one subject. The commentator (al-Jurjani) provides a multi-layered response. First, he clarifies that 'form' here means the known thing (al-ma'lum) which is derived existence, not knowledge itself. The initial answer denies similarity between the universal form and its particular, but this is deemed insufficient because no rational person would claim similarity between a universal and its particular. Then the commentator offers a corrected interpretation: the form is taken as knowledge, and the similarity is denied between the cognitive form and the existence fixed for it, because absolute existence is not the complete quiddity of both. Even if existence is essential to the form, it is not essential to the existence fixed for the quiddity. He then addresses the objection that the form is individuated yet described as universal: its universality is by correspondence to many individuals, while its mental individuation remains. Next, he presents a second line of defense: even if similarity is conceded, the impossibility is that two similars should inhere in one subject like accidents, but existence in the soul is not like that—it is a purely abstract concept, not a superadded accident. He then comments on the meaning of 'al-thabit al-mu'ayyan' and explains that it covers both substance and accident. Finally, he argues that the universal form is not similar to partial existence because the quiddity of existence is realized in partial existence not as a form, and the subsistence of existence in the soul is different from the subsistence of accidents. The passage concludes with the idea that the addition of existence to quiddity is only mental.

Key Terms
مماثلة (mumathala)
صورة (sura)
كلّي (kulli)
جزئي (juz'i)
وجود ذهني (wujud dhihni)
ماهية (mahiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the mental form is identical to the extramental existence of the thing.
Correction: This passage clarifies that they are not similar because the mental form is universal while existence is particular, and existence is an abstract concept, not a superadded attribute.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why is the similarity between the mental form and existence denied?
A) Because the mental form is universal and existence is particular
B) Because the mental form is accidental and existence is substantial
C) Because existence is not a thing that can be known
D) Because the mental form is a shadow of existence

Key Takeaway
The mental form is not similar to extramental existence because it is universal and existence is particular, and mental existence is an abstract concept, not a superadded attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your understanding of Allah’s existence, do you unconsciously assume that your mental concept of His existence is identical to His actual existence? How does al-Jurjani’s argument about the lack of similarity between mental form and extramental existence challenge you to purify your conception of God? What concrete step will you take this week to remind yourself that your mental image is not the reality?
?
The passage warns against claiming similarity between a universal and its particular. In your daily judgments about people, where might you be applying a universal rule (like ‘all Muslims are good’) to a particular person and failing to see the nuance? What accountability does this lesson create for you to avoid such unjust generalizations, especially in how you view other believers?
?
You often think you ‘know’ something when you have a mental form of it. But does that form truly capture the reality? This lesson says mental existence is merely an abstract concept. In what area of your religious life are you most vulnerable to confusing your mental construct with the actual truth—whether about a text, a person, or a concept—and what will you do to seek the reality beyond your mental image?

Rational Judgments

First Proof: Existence Must Be Common
First proof that existence is a common meaning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that existence (al-wujud) is a common meaning and why certainty about it would be impossible if it were not common.

Core Idea
Existence must be common; otherwise, certainty about it would be impossible.

This passage presents the first proof that existence is a shared meaning across all existents. The proof uses a conditional argument: if existence were not common, then whenever we hesitate about the specific natures of things, we would lose certainty about existence itself. This demonstrates that existence cannot be unique to each thing but must be a common attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once to get a sense of its structure, then read the literal translation. Notice how the argument is set up as a conditional.
The passage includes commentary, but focus on the main matn lines.
2
Identify the conditional structure
The proof begins: 'if it were not common, certainty of it would be impossible.' Identify this conditional and see how it is unpacked.
The key is the impossibility of certainty when there is hesitation about particularities.
3
Understand the two cases
If existence is not common, it is either identical to the particular natures or specific to them (essentially or accidentally). In both cases, any doubt about the natures leads to doubt about existence.
This shows that certainty about existence requires it to be common.

Source Text

المرصد الأول - المقصد الثاني : في أنه مشترك
ذهبوا إلى كونه مشتركاً معنى ( لوجوه الأول ) أنه ( لو لم يكن مشتركاً لامتنع الجزم به) أي الوجود عند التردد في الخصوصيات من أنواع الموجودات وأشخاصها (ضرورة أنه ( أعني الوجود على تقدير كونه غير مشترك ( إما نفس الخصوصيات نفسها أو مختص بها ذاتياً كان لها أو عرضياً، (فيزول اعتقاده زوال اعتقادها أما على الأول فلان التردد في الخصوصيات عين التردد في الوجودات التي هي أعيان تلك الخصوصيات

قوله : ( إلى كونه مشتركاً معنى أي في الكل
قوله : ( إنه لو لم يكن مشتركاً ) أي أصلاً .
قوله : ( لامتنع الجزم به أي بقاء الجزم لقوله فيزول اعتقاده .
قوله : ( عند التردد في (الخصوصيات أي في خصوصية أية خصوصية كانت، فالتعريف للعهد الذهني والمراد عند التردد في الخصوصيات أو عند اعتقاد خصوصية أخرى، إلا أنه تركه اللفظ لأنه إذا امتنع الجزم به عند التردد كان امتناعه عند اعتقاد خصوصية أخرى بطريق الأولى، والقرينة على ذلك قوله مع زوال اعتقادها، فإن زوال اعتقاد الخصوصية أعم من أن يكون بالتردد فيها، أو باعتقاد خصوصية أخرى وبما ذكرنا انطبق أول الكلام وآخره، وظهر وجه تعرض الشارح لبيان بطلان التالي على تقدير اعتقاد خصوصية أخرى بقوله، وكذا إذا اعتقدنا الخ، ولك أن تخصص قوله مع زوال اعتقادها بالتردد ويؤيده أن الشارح خص بيان زوال اعتقاده مع زوال اعتقادها بصورة التردد وعلى التوجيه الأول يكون التعرض لها لكونها مذكورة في المتن صريحاً، وأما زوال اعتقاده مع زوال اعتقادها في صورة اعتقاد خصوصية أخرى، فلازم منه بطريق الأولى وعلى التوجيه الثاني يكون قول الشارح، وكذا إذا اعتقدنا دليلاً برأسه على الاشتراك ويؤيده ذكر النتيجة بعده .
قوله : ( من أنواع الموجودات المراد بها ما عدا الأشخاص بقرينة المقابلة. قوله: (أما نفس الخصوصيات أي نفس خصوصية ما من الخصوصيات والمراد بالخصوصيات الماهية المخصوصة تعبيرا عن الشيء بوصفه . قوله : ( فيزول اعتقاده أي الاعتقاد بالوجود الذي كان حاصلاً أولا وهو الاعتقاد المطابق للواقع وزواله إما بزوال نفس الاعتقاد كما إذا كان الاختصاص معلوماً أو مشكوكا وإما بزوال مطابقته للواقع كما إذا كان خالي الذهن منه فاندفع البحثان المشهوران أحدهما أنا لا نسلم زوال الاعتقاد بالوجود عند زوال الاعتقاد بالخصوصية، لأن ذلك عند العلم بالعينية أو الاختصاص أو الشك فيه ويجوز أن يكون خالي الذهن عن الاختصاص وعدمه وثانيهما أن اللازم من الدليل على تقدير تمامه العلم باشتراك الوجود لا اشتراكه في نفس الأمر والمدعي هو الثاني .
قوله : ( وإنما ذهبوا (الخ) هذا مشعر بأنه جعل قوله لوجوه متعلقاً بقوله ذهب، والأولى تعلقه بنفس المدعى المعبر عنه بأنه مشترك وإن كان الأول أقرب لفظاً .
The First Observatory - The Second Goal: On it being common. They held that it is common in meaning (due to aspects, the first) that (if it were not common, certainty of it would be impossible) i.e. of existence when there is hesitation about the particularities from the types of existents and their individuals (necessarily because it (meaning existence on the supposition of its being non-common) is either the particularities themselves or specific to them, whether essential or accidental, (so its belief would cease with the cessation of their belief. As for the first, because hesitation about the particularities is the same as hesitation about the existences that are those particularities themselves. His saying: (to its being common in meaning) i.e. in all. His saying: (that if it were not common) i.e. at all. His saying: (certainty of it would be impossible) i.e. the continuation of certainty, because of his saying 'its belief would cease'. His saying: (when hesitation about the particularities) i.e. about any particularity whatsoever, so the definition is for the mind's reference, and the intended meaning is when hesitation about the particularities or when believing another particularity, except that he omitted the wording because if certainty of it is impossible at hesitation, then its impossibility at believing another particularity is all the more so. The evidence for that is his saying 'with the cessation of their belief', for the cessation of belief about the particularity is more general than being due to hesitation about it or due to believing another particularity. And by what we have mentioned the beginning of the discourse and its end are consistent, and the reason for the commentator's exposition of the falsity of the consequent on the supposition of believing another particularity by his saying 'and likewise if we believe, etc.' appears. And you may restrict his saying 'with the cessation of their belief' to hesitation, and it is supported by that the commentator restricted the exposition of the cessation of his belief with the cessation of their belief to the case of hesitation. On the first interpretation, the exposition is because it is explicitly mentioned in the text. As for the cessation of his belief with the cessation of their belief in the case of believing another particularity, it is a necessary consequence all the more so. On the second interpretation, the commentator's saying 'and likewise if we believe' is an independent proof for commonality, and it is supported by the mention of the conclusion after it. His saying: (from the types of existents) the intended by them is other than the individuals, by the indication of the contrast. His saying: (either the particularities themselves) i.e. a particularity itself from among the particularities, and by 'particularities' is meant the specified quiddity, expressing the thing by its description. His saying: (so its belief would cease) i.e. the belief about existence that was first obtained, and it is the belief corresponding to reality. Its cessation is either by the cessation of the belief itself, as when the specification is known or doubted, or by the cessation of its correspondence to reality, as when the mind is empty of it. Thus the two well-known inquiries are repelled: first, we do not grant the cessation of belief about existence at the cessation of belief about the particularity, because that is when the identity or specification is known or doubted, and it is possible that the mind is empty of specification and its opposite; second, what follows from the proof, supposing its completeness, is knowledge of the commonality of existence, not its commonality in reality, and the claimant is the second. His saying: (and they only held, etc.) This indicates that he made his saying 'by aspects' related to his saying 'they held', but it is better to relate it to the very claim expressed by 'it is common', even though the first is closer in wording.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof that existence (al-wujud) is a common meaning (mushtarak ma'nawi). The proof is conditional: if existence were not common, then certainty (al-jazm) about it would be impossible when one hesitates about the specific natures (al-khususiyyat) of existents. The reasoning is that if existence were not common, it would either be identical to those particular natures or specific to them (either essentially or accidentally). In either case, any doubt about the particular natures would entail doubt about existence itself, so certainty about existence would be lost. The commentary explains each part: 'when hesitation about the particularities' includes also the case of believing a contrary particularity (a fortiori). The cessation of belief (zawal al-i'tiqad) can occur either by losing the belief itself or by losing its correspondence to reality. The proof then refutes two well-known objections: first, that one might not lose belief in existence when losing belief in the particularity if the mind is empty of the relation; second, that the proof only establishes knowledge of commonality, not commonality in reality. The response is that the realization of the belief's cessation covers both cases. The final comment suggests that the phrase 'by aspects' (li-wujuh) should grammatically relate to the claim rather than to the verb 'they held'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof only shows that we know existence is common, not that it actually is common in reality.
Correction: The proof argues that certainty about existence requires it to be common in reality, because certainty is about reality, not just subjective knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, what would happen if existence were not common when we hesitate about particular natures?
A) We would still be certain of existence.
B) Certainty about existence would be impossible.
C) Existence would become a particular itself.
D) The particular natures would become common.

Key Takeaway
The first proof shows that existence must be a common meaning; otherwise, we could never be certain of it when we consider different specific natures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof shows that your certainty about existence is independent of your knowledge of specific natures. When you face doubts about Allah's attributes or actions, does that ever shake your certainty that He exists? Name one specific doubt you have harbored, and then ask yourself: did I still know He exists? If yes, how does this lesson affirm that your faith is grounded in reality?
?
The text says certainty about existence would cease if existence were specific to particular natures. Look at your own life: have you ever had a time when you were so confused about a person or concept that you doubted its very existence? What does that tell you about the nature of existence itself? Commit today to reflecting on the commonality of existence when you next face confusion about the details of your faith.
?
This proof calls you to realize that existence is a shared reality that does not depend on your understanding. In a moment of spiritual dryness, when you feel distant from Allah, the fact of His existence remains certain. How can you use this certainty to rebuild your connection with Him? Write down one practical step you will take this week to remind yourself that He is real even when you don't feel close.
